For Metals Pricing, Fixes Aren't In
Commodities Report
For Metals Pricing, Fixes Aren't In
By Francesca Freeman
520 words
19 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
It isn't so easy to fix the fixes.
The century-old method of setting silver prices -- daily chats among a small coterie of banks -- is being
scrapped for something more modern. Exactly what isn't clear, and that is causing the one thing that is
anathema to bullion traders: uncertainty.
The waters were further muddied Wednesday, when the World Gold Council weighed in on overhauling the
gold fix.
"The market is in suspended animation," said David Govett, head of precious metals at brokerage firm Marex
Spectron. "It's like nobody quite knows what's going to happen and who's going to do it, which I don't think is
ever very good for a market."
The gold and silver fixes provide daily benchmarks used to price, for example, mining sales contracts and
exchange-traded funds. In use since 1919 and 1897, respectively, they have recently been set by way of
conference calls among banks -- four for gold and three for silver. After coming under regulatory scrutiny as
part of a broader examination of financial benchmarks in the wake of a global scandal involving the rigging of
interest rates, the silver fix was declared dead in April when Deutsche Bank
AG pulled out of the process as part of a wider retreat from commodities. The fix will be conducted for the
final time on Aug. 14.
The London Bullion Market Association -- an industry body that represents the precious-metals sector -- will
convene a meeting of its members in London on Friday to hear proposals for a replacement, after canvassing
interested parties.
The LBMA said its survey of 440 market participants showed a clear desire for an electronic, auction-based
benchmark with an increased number of contributors.
The intervention of the World Gold Council left some industry participants scratching their heads. The WGC,
which represents the mining sector, has invited the bullion industry to a meeting in July to discuss a potential
overhaul to the gold fix.
"This adds to the confusion" in the market, said Mr. Govett. "The silver fix needs to be changed before we can
know what to do about gold."
Natalie Dempster, the WGC's managing director of central banks and public policy, acknowledged that the
body wouldn't expect to be the administrator of any overhauled fix, but said the organization has been
considering a forum on the gold fix for some time.
"The silver and gold markets are very different. Whatever solution comes out for the silver fix cannot
necessarily be transposed onto the gold fix," Ms. Dempster said.
For some, though, clarity can't come soon enough. Benchmark prices are vital for producers of silver and
gold, who use them for supply deals, said Lenic Rodriguez, chief executive of Canadian silver-mining
company Aurcana Corp.
"Whose price [do we use] if there isn't a benchmark?" said Mr. Rodriguez.
Adam Lawrence, CEO of the official U.K. Royal Mint, said, "Markets don't like not knowing what's going on.
You don't want to leave the market in flux for too long."

Wealth Management (A Special Report) --- Alternative Investing: ETFs Let You Bet on a Continuing Boom in the Social-Media Sector
Wealth Management (A Special Report) --- Alternative Investing: ETFs Let You Bet on a Continuing
Boom in the Social-Media Sector
By Gregory Zuckerman
864 words
16 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
R4
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Q. The What'sApp deal suggests the social-media world is hot. Are there ways to use ETFs or other vehicles
to bet on this sector?
A. Heads turned in February when Facebook agreed to buy messaging company WhatsApp Inc. for $19
billion -- the largest-ever purchase of a company backed by venture capital. WhatsApp, with only 55
employees and just $20 million in sales last year, has seen usage of its unlimited and quick text messaging
service more than double in the past nine months to 450 million monthly users. Shares of Facebook are up
almost 20% this year, better than the overall market. Still, the sector has had its ups and downs. Shares of
Twitter, for example, have plunged about 42% this year.
The only way to invest in a social-media business such as WhatsApp is to make a courageous bet that its
heady growth can continue. Competition in social media is fierce and consumer tastes change quickly, raising
risks for investors.
Matthew Tuttle, president of Tuttle Tactical Management LLC in Stamford, Conn., says investors could
consider exchange-traded funds such as Global X Social Media Index (SOCL). The ETF is a bet on global
companies that provide social networking, file sharing and other Web-based media applications, including
Facebook, LinkedIn and Twitter.
Investors need to weigh the risks, however. The ETF "is highly concentrated -- 50% is in the top-five holdings
and 25% is in China," Mr. Tuttle says.
Other choices are more limited. First Trust ISE Cloud Computing Index (SKYY), for example, tracks
companies actively involved in cloud computing. But the fund holds only a few firms, such as Facebook, that
are focused on social media.
---
Q. Gold is rebounding but it still seems risky. What are the best ways to invest in gold and silver?
A. Gold prices have rebounded after enduring a difficult recent few years. So far this year, gold prices are up
about 6%, a tad better than the overall stock market.
It isn't clear if gold is attractive at these levels. Stocks have proved resilient, siphoning interest in precious
metals, which often are a haven for investors nervous about the global economy. That is why gold prices are
down about 7% from their 2014 highs, reached in mid-March. And precious metals don't always provide
safety in a down market. Gold did much worse than expected in 2008, for example, despite the stock-market
collapse.
Still, those who fear higher inflation may be inclined to shift some of their portfolio into gold and silver,
analysts say.
Those bullish on gold have several options, according to Mark Thomas, director of Hedge Fund Research at
CTC Consulting/Harris myCFO, a division of BMO Financial Group.
"The most straightforward and efficient option is a gold exchange-traded fund," he says. "These ETFs have
tracked the price of physical gold extremely well since inception and remain one of the cheapest and most
scalable ways to build an exposure."
Yigal Newman, chief investment officer of JSF Financial LLC, a Los Angeles-based financial-advisory firm,
suggests ETFs such as SPDR Gold Trust (GLD) and iShares Gold Trust (IAU) for those interested in gold,
though he's neutral on the investment at the moment.

--- Page 38 ---
Page 38 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
What about buying and storing gold in a vault or somewhere else? "Owning physical gold itself with a clear
title of ownership is an option, but it requires several million dollars to be efficient when considering the cost
of secure storage," Mr. Thomas says.
Robert Gordon, president of Twenty-First Securities Corp., says gold is considered a collectible when held in
the form of coins or bullion, making it less attractive from a tax standpoint. Taxes on long-term holdings of
gold are 28%, compared with the 20% rate on long-term stock and bondholdings. ETFs like GLD also are
taxed at the 28% rate of collectibles. Short-term gold profits are taxed at an investor's ordinary income rate,
just like short-term profits on mutual funds and stocks.
"In terms of taxes, gold isn't all that glittery," he says.
He recommends options on GLD or gold futures contracts, which can have lower tax rates than ETFs, coins
or bullion and tend to be highly correlated to gold prices. "Because many investors and financial advisers
aren't comfortable with futures," they may want to focus on listed options in gold-related investments, such as
an option on the GLD fund.
Buying shares of gold miners is another way to bet on gold, albeit a dangerous one. These shares have
done worse than gold itself in recent years, partly because some gold miners have done a poor job running
their businesses. Those convinced these businesses have improved their operations could purchase GDX,
an ETF that tries to track the performance of gold miners.
---
Mr. Zuckerman is a reporter for The Wall Street Journal in New York and author of "The Frackers: The
Outrageous Inside Story of the New Billionaire Wildcatters." He can be reached at
greg.zuckerman@wsj.com.

WASHINGTON -- If Jeff Bell was looking to capture the hearts and minds of New Jersey voters in his bid to
oust incumbent Sen. Cory Booker, it wasn't readily apparent from his first speech after winning the state's
Republican primary.
That address took place last Friday beneath the U.S. Capitol dome, where he urged the adoption of the gold
standard and bashed the Federal Reserve's interest-rate policies. The audience? About 30 like-minded
conservatives.
The location seemed fitting: Mr. Bell, a former aide to presidents Richard Nixon and Ronald Reagan, is more
Washington policy wonk than glad-handing New Jersey politician -- far more conversant with the finer points
of monetary policy than the Garden State's budget or urban-crime problems.
The last time Mr. Bell won a primary there, Jimmy Carter was president and the Bee Gees topped the music
charts. He lost his second race in 1982, and has since lived in the Washington, D.C., area. This time, he said
he plans to run a campaign largely on national economic policy.
Despite the 30-year gap, he eked out last Tuesday's primary victory over several other little-known
candidates, pulling just over 41,000 votes statewide in a sparse turnout. The result surprised many political
observers, including Mr. Bell.
"We didn't have money for a poll. We didn't know how the race would turn out," he said.
Mr. Bell, 70 years old, moved to northern New Jersey in February, renting two floors of a house after deciding
to run against Mr. Booker. He said his wife wasn't fond of the idea, and joked that his teenage son often
chauffeurs him around, with the two sometimes getting lost.
Since he last ran for public office, Mr. Bell has done everything from working at think tanks and business
lobbying to promoting conservative economic policy on opinion pages and elsewhere.
He spent the last several years lobbying lawmakers, unsuccessfully, to tie the dollar to gold, on behalf of the
conservative group American Principles Project. Not many consider the gold standard a mainstream issue
that can win politically, he said.
"We have to start a grass-roots movement," he said.
Some question whether New Jersey is the right venue. But Mr. Bell said he is convinced New Jersey voters
will understand that the dollar should be pegged to gold and that the Federal Reserve's zero-interest policies
have hobbled the recovery.
"Every peasant in Bangladesh knows that gold holds its value," he said.
Of course, not being in the state for the past 30 years, he said, "hasn't been a plus" for the campaign. Mr. Bell
has moved to shore up statewide support by meeting with the New Jersey Republican State Committee; he
said he hopes for an endorsement from Gov. Chris Christie.
"I heard him speak at Rutgers in 1978 when he was running for office," said Bill Palatucci, a close political
adviser to Mr. Christie. He said he doesn't recall seeing him since, until the two men met last week.
Mr. Palatucci said the governor "would be supportive of the party's nominee," who must prove his ability to
raise funds.

Whereas Mr. Booker's campaign has raised several million dollars, Mr. Bell's is in debt, the Republican said.

and committed to the issues."

speech, Mr. Bell said the crowd's size wasn't surprising.
"I don't think my primary win changed the interest level of this event," he said with a laugh.

Ten Companies Seeking To Replace Silver Gauge
Ten Companies Seeking To Replace Silver Gauge
By Francesca Freeman
436 words
14 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B2
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Ten different groups are vying to replace the historic London silver fix, according to the London Bullion Market
Association.
The LBMA said it will whittle these down in the coming days to a shortlist that will be invited to a meeting in
London on June 20 where they will present their plans to the LBMA's 76 members, which include banks,
metals refiners and miners.
Among those in contention are the London Metal Exchange, exchange-traded fund provider ETF Securities,
and commodities-information firm Platts -- all of whom have said they have applied to replace the fix.
Financial information company Thomson Reuters Corp. has also submitted a proposal, a person familiar with
the matter said. CME Group Inc. recently declared an interest although it is unclear whether it made a formal
proposal to the LBMA.
"We are working closely with the precious metals industry and the LBMA to reduce market disruption by
helping to find a robust, transaction-based way to set the daily spot price so the markets can continue to work
efficiently and seamlessly," Harriet Hunnable, managing director of metals at CME Group, said in a statement
last month.
The fix is a 117-year-old daily ritual that provides a benchmark that is used to price, for example, mining sales
contracts and exchange-traded funds. In recent years, the fix has been set daily at noon following a
conference call by representatives of Barclays PLC, HSBC Holdings PLC and Deutsche Bank AG. But the
benchmark will be set for the final time in August, following Deutsche Bank's decision earlier this year to
resign from the fixing panel. According to people familiar with the matter, the process was deemed unviable
with only two panel members.
The LBMA's effort to find an alternative to the benchmark comes as the silver fix and a similar process for
setting the benchmark price of gold come under the scrutiny of regulators in the wake of a global scandal
involving the rigging of interest rates.
The LBMA said June 6 that a survey of around 440 market participants showed a clear desire for an
electronic, auction-based benchmark with an increased number of contributors.
ETF Securities told The Wall Street Journal earlier in June that its proposal was along these lines. Platts has
made a similar proposal, according to a person familiar with the situation.
Thomson Reuters and Platts, a McGraw Hill Financial Inc.-owned company, compete with News Corp's Dow
Jones & Co., publisher of The Wall Street Journal and Dow Jones Newswires, on business news.

Paul Volcker: Back to the Woods?
Paul Volcker: Back to the Woods?
By Seth Lipsky
971 words
12 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A13
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Former Federal Reserve Chairman Paul Volcker called last month in Washington for a new Bretton Woods,
the 1944 conference of World War II Allies that set up an international gold-exchange regime. His remarks
received little media attention.
This strikes me as an underplayed story, especially as Congress considers taking a serious look at the
Federal Reserve. Some legislators in particular are concerned that the value of the dollar, while stronger than
last year, is still worth less than a 1200th of an ounce of gold.
Mr. Volcker made his remarks at the annual meeting of the Bretton Woods Committee, a nonpartisan
organization that has been getting together since 1983. He did not call for a return to the Bretton Woods
gold-exchange regime per se and only mentioned "gold" twice. But the title of the speech was "A New
Bretton Woods???" Let's take those three question marks to mean that Mr. Volcker wants to put this question
out there emphatically.
Mr. Volcker reprised the history of the Bretton Woods system, which allowed foreign governments to redeem
dollars in gold and established the dollar as the world's leading currency. The system collapsed under the
weight of the Lyndon Johnson-Richard Nixon "guns and butter" strategy -- paying for both the Vietnam War
and expanded welfare benefits and doing so by deficit spending.
"Inevitable" was the word Mr. Volcker used to describe what has been called the "Nixon shock," a series of
policies implemented in 1971 that included suspending the gold convertibility of the dollar. Mr. Volcker didn't
say so directly, but he in effect vindicated Henry Hazlitt, the most prophetic critic of Bretton Woods when the
system was first discussed in the 1940s. Hazlitt, then an editorial writer for the New York Times, believed
Bretton Woods and the International Monetary Fund, which the agreements set up, were an inflation trap.
In his speech, Mr. Volcker also reprised the efforts to muddle through after Bretton Woods. The Plaza accord
of 1985 devalued the dollar against the yen and the mark. The Louvre accord of 1987 sought to stem the
dollar's slide. Or, as Mr. Volcker put it, "a lot of floating, some fixing, some 'do as you please.'"
"By now," Mr. Volcker said, "I think we can agree that the absence of an official, rules-based, cooperatively
managed monetary system has not been a great success. In fact, international financial crises seem at least
as frequent and more destructive in impeding economic stability and growth."
He spoke of the 1970s as "an unhappy decade of inflation ending in stagflation." He touched on the Latin
American, Mexican and Asian crises that followed and the crisis of 2008 and the Great Recession in 2009.
"Not a pretty picture," Mr. Volcker said, adding that he wanted to raise "a neglected question": "Has the
absence of a well-functioning international monetary system been an enabling (or instigating) condition?"
Mr. Volcker made "a plea for attention to the need for developing an international monetary and financial
system worthy of our time." He acknowledged what he styled the "forceful fiscal and monetary policies" of
recent years, but clearly reckons them inadequate.
"The provision of ample liquidity by the key national central bankers is still taking place as we meet," he said.
"But those measures don't really count as structural reforms." He said he could not answer the question of
what approach to take, but tinkering with the International Monetary Fund was "not enough" and would mean
"little without substantive agreement on the need for monetary reform and practical approaches toward that
end."
Mr. Volcker added that he wanted to find "ways of encouraging -- even insisting upon -- needed balance of
payments equilibrium" so that countries don't either suddenly run out of foreign exchange reserves or
accumulate too many. "Nor," he said, "would I reject some reassessment of the use of a single national
currency as the dominant international reserve and trading vehicle."

--- Page 43 ---
Page 43 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The ex-chairman noted that we are a long way from a new Bretton Woods conference, but said that "surely
events have raised, whether we want to admit it or not, some fundamental questions that have been ignored
for decades."
"It's easy to say what's wrong," Mr. Volcker told me over the weekend, "but sensible reforms are a pretty
tough thing."
His remarks will still be music to the ears of many nursing the idea of monetary reform, even if he hasn't
endorsed gold. A rules-based approach is certainly getting new attention, including at an important
conference organized by economist John Taylor last month at the Hoover Institution.
Congress is starting to look at monetary reform. In July 2012 a bipartisan majority in the House voted 327 to
98 for a serious audit of the Federal Reserve. If the Republicans gain the Senate, the audit could end up as
law. As could a bipartisan measure to mark the first century of the Federal Reserve by establishing a
Centennial Monetary Commission to look to the Fed's second century.
That effort is coming from Congress's Joint Economic Committee, led by Rep. Kevin Brady (R., Texas). Sens.
John Cornyn (R., Texas) and Rand Paul (R., Ky.) are nursing the measure in the upper chamber. It strikes
me as a good moment to get Mr. Volcker in and see if he will back a centennial review of the Fed.
The idea, after all, is to see how the Fed could be improved for the next century. The measure has had tough
sledding. But if a paragon like Mr. Volcker got behind the idea and supported a new, rules-based system, the
effort might be able to get off the base-metal dime.
---
Mr. Lipsky is editor of the New York Sun.

Palladium Hits Highest Price in Three Years --- Investors Bet on a Prolonged Interruption in Supplies From South Africa as Its Longest-Ever...
Commodities Report
Palladium Hits Highest Price in Three Years --- Investors Bet on a Prolonged Interruption in Supplies
From South Africa as Its Longest-Ever Mining Strike Continues
By Ira Iosebashvili
682 words
11 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Palladium prices surged to a three-year high, reflecting investors' concerns that a mining strike in No. 2
producer South Africa will choke off supplies.
The metal, which is mainly used in car-exhaust filters, is trading just about $3 below its highest price since
February 2001. Investors are piling into the market, betting a prolonged interruption in supplies out of South
Africa will lead to a shortage for auto makers later this year.
Palladium's industrial uses, and the dependence on South Africa for supplies, have helped keep the metal
aloft even as other precious metals stumbled this year. An improving U.S. economic outlook has caused
many investors to sour on gold and silver, which are primarily owned as a hedge against inflation and
uncertainty. While gold has slipped 2% in the past month, palladium prices are up 7%.
On Tuesday, palladium for September delivery, the most actively traded contract, rose $12.95, or 1.5%, to
$854.55 a troy ounce on the New York Mercantile Exchange's Comex division, the highest since February
2011. The June contract also rose $12.95, to $854.90 an ounce.
Miners in South Africa went on strike in January for the second time in two years, demanding higher wages
and better working conditions. The walkout has sharply reduced palladium exports from South Africa, which
produces 40% of the world's supplies. Global palladium inventories have dwindled and are now sufficient to
meet only 16 weeks of demand, according to CPM Group, a consulting firm. Palladium is usually mined
together with platinum, a more expensive metal.
The lower supplies go, the higher prices will rise, investors say. "If there's not much supply, it all just gets
sucked up" by manufacturers and other palladium consumers, said Frank Holmes, CEO of U.S. Global
Investors, which manages about $1 billion.
Mr. Holmes has raised his fund's stake in palladium exchange-traded funds, which hold the metal for
shareholders, to 8% this year, from 2% in 2013. Mr. Holmes said he sees prices staying high even when the
work stoppage ends, as clean-air standards in China drive up demand for exhaust filters.
The miners' strike, which involves some 70,000 workers, is the longest ever in South Africa. After Monday's
talks ended without an agreement, newly appointed South African Mining Minister Ngoako Ramatlhodi said
he would exit from the talks.
Meanwhile, palladium users have few other places to turn for supply. Russia exports more palladium than
South Africa, but relies on Cold War-era stockpiles to supplement production. Many analysts think those extra
supplies are running low.
"There is no reason to sell palladium right now," said Frank Lesh, a broker at Future Path Trading. "This thing
can go to $1,000." Palladium prices last traded at $1,000 an ounce in February 2001.
Some investors and analysts see ETFs playing a role in driving up palladium prices. Total holdings of
palladium by ETFs stood just shy of 2.8 million ounces at the end of May, exceeding the annual production of
Russia, Commerzbank said.
To be sure, prices would likely drop if the walkout ends. Prices fell nearly 5% in the month after the last major
mining strike was resolved in September 2012, though they later recovered. Demand from the auto industry,
though improving, may not be strong enough to keep prices high without supply concerns also lending
support, analysts say. "Investors will have every excuse to sell above $850 per ounce," said Rohit Savant, an
analyst with CPM Group. "Breaking much further above that won't be easy."

--- Page 45 ---
Page 45 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Still, with Mr. Ramatlhodi's exit from negotiations, many investors and analysts expect protracted talks
between mining companies and the Association of Mineworkers and Construction Union will cause prices to
climb further.
"The sides seem to be a long way apart, and there seems little hope for resolution," said David Wilson, a
Citigroup analyst.

Deals Lift Stocks to Records --- Pickup in Merger Activity, Recent Upbeat Economic Reports Keep

By Dan Strumpf

The Wall Street Journal

English

Stocks tiptoed deeper into record territory, buoyed by a flurry of deal activity.
The gains built on last week's rally and followed several upbeat reports on the U.S. economy that have
bolstered investors' optimism. Shares of smaller companies, which tend to be more exposed to the U.S. than
multinational firms, outpaced the broader market.
The announcement of several deals helped drive trading Monday, extending this year's pickup in merger
activity. A takeover battle for Hillshire Brands ended in favor of Tyson Foods, while Merck agreed to buy a
smaller drug maker and chip maker Analog Devices said it was acquiring a smaller firm.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average climbed 18.82 points, or 0.1%, to a fresh closing record of 16943.10, its
ninth of the year.
The Russell 2000 index of small-cap stocks climbed 10.67, or 0.9%, to 1175.88, bringing gains to 4.4% in the
past four sessions.
Optimism about corporate earnings and continued accommodative policies by the world's biggest central
banks also have helped push stocks higher in recent weeks, while concerns about a prolonged economic
slowdown have abated.
"We believe the U.S. is on a self-sustaining recovery," said Susan Bao, portfolio manager for the $10.7 billion
JPMorgan U.S. Equity Fund. "The market is quietly climbing walls of worry, and so is the U.S. economy."
The S&P 500 index added 1.83 points, or 0.1%, to 1951.27, notching its 19th closing record of 2014. The
Nasdaq Composite Index advanced 14.84 points, or 0.3%, to 4336.24, still shy of multiyear records hit in
March.
Investors continued to be buoyed by Friday's report showing that U.S. employers added 217,000 jobs in May,
the fourth consecutive month of job gains totaling more than 200,000.
Ms. Bao said corporate executives she meets with seem increasingly optimistic about their business
prospects. "We're seeing management teams coming into our office from the boardrooms sounding more
confident than before," she said. Her fund, she said, remains parked in sizable holdings of companies that
benefit from an improving economy, such as manufacturing and industrial shares.
As in recent sessions, trading volumes were relatively light. Many traders say the drumbeat of positive
economic news has left investors wary of selling stocks, while buyers remain content to sit on existing
holdings.
"Going into this week, you've got positive economic momentum . . . but it's low-volume," said Tom Carter,
managing director at brokerage JonesTrading.

a seven-year low on Friday.
Later in the week, data on retail sales, wholesale inflation and consumer sentiment are due.

managing director at Rosenblatt Securities. "All the data is indicating that we're getting a little bit better," but
at the same time, growth isn't strong enough to prompt the Federal Reserve to raise interest rates soon.

Now Is the Time to Raise Interest Rates
Now Is the Time to Raise Interest Rates
By David Malpass
1,108 words
9 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A15
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Stock markets have been hitting record highs, usually a sign of good growth and low inflation. Meanwhile,
bond yields have remained unusually low. With gold prices falling since 2011, that's more like the Great
Moderation of the 1980s and 1990s. This is good news for the economy but entirely inconsistent with
near-zero interest rates. The big question is how Federal Reserve policy reacts to the surge in asset prices. I
believe that they reflect faster, more sustainable growth, pointing to the need for interest-rate hikes soon.
Behind the improvements in markets are improvements in the policies of central banks. The Fed is tapering
its bond purchases and at least discussing interest-rate hikes, while many other central banks have shifted
from discretionary policies (trust us) toward tougher bank regulation and clearer benchmarks for sound
money. These include Taylor-type rules linking interest rates to inflation or growth (for example, Israel and
Canada) and prompt central-bank policy responses to variations in exchange rates (Brazil). The decline in
gold prices and other measures of currency instability suggests less risk of harmful inflation, deflation and
financial system failure.
Creating a narrower range for inflation and currency movements should lead the world toward more
investment and growth -- though it reduces the profits from currency and interest-rate trading, a negative for
some on Wall Street. In many countries such as Switzerland, currency strength now elicits interest-rate cuts
or foreign currency purchases while excessive currency weakness triggers the reverse.
National currencies or virtual global currencies that are strong and stable enhance the current value of future
corporate earnings, an important factor in high equity prices, and reduce the inflation component of bond
yields, helping explain the bond market's resilience.
The result has been a powerful global convergence toward higher prices for equity and real estate and lower
bond yields. The rule of thumb used to be that five-year U.S. Treasury yields should stay near the nominal
GDP growth rate (3.4% year-over-year.) With five-year Treasurys now at 1.6%, some look for a recession,
but it's more plausible that the demand for bonds is up while the supply is down.
More and more retirees are seeking safe investments, and increasing confidence in currencies and inflation
is adding to the value of bonds at a time when huge bond purchases by the Federal Reserve and Bank of
Japan have made them more scarce. Yields will probably take time to correct, rising in stair steps as growth
accelerates and central banks allow interest rates to rise to more normal levels.
The ebullience in financial markets raises concerns that investors have become complacent about risk and
will leverage assets beyond their earning potential -- leading to a "Minsky moment" when asset values
suddenly collapse. The counterargument is that regulators burned by 2007 have become more wary of bank
leverage. This has caused a fundamental change in the operation of monetary policy.
The Fed's massive bond purchases over the past few years would at one time have bled into the economy
through increased bank leverage (more bank deposits and lending backed by more bank reserves). With
bank leverage constrained by regulatory policy, however, there's no longer a linkage from bank reserves to
bank lending. My columns on this page in 2009 and 2010 explained that the Fed's below-market interest
rates and bond buying could be expected to slow U.S. growth by forcing a rationing of credit and by
channeling capital away from small private businesses.
The Fed's purchases of long-term government bonds help well-established long-term borrowers, many of
whom don't really need the money. Because bank leverage has been limited since the crisis, however, the
Fed's purchases have been coming at the expense of smaller borrowers, a problem the European Central
Bank addressed directly in its policy decisions last week.
Until the January taper, bank loans and U.S. private-sector credit had been growing very slowly. Since then
the Fed's weekly data show that bank loans to private businesses have accelerated sharply and are on track

--- Page 49 ---
Page 49 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
to grow by $700 billion in 2014 after only growing $140 billion in 2013. The Fed's flow-of-funds data released
June 5 showed that credit to small businesses reached $4.2 trillion on March 31, up 3.8% over the past 12
months, topping the nominal GDP growth rate (of 3.4%) for the first time in the post-crisis expansion.
Previous upward crossovers in small business lending like this only occurred in 1984, 1996 and 2004, each of
which signaled periods of strong growth and small-business job creation.
It is vital that the Fed take this opportunity to move interest rates above zero. New York Fed President Bill
Dudley gave a strong justification for rate hikes on May 20: "It would be desirable to get off the zero lower
bound in order to regain some monetary policy flexibility." Kansas City Fed President Esther George
expressed urgency on May 29 at a Hoover Institution monetary conference: "I would like to see short-term
interest rates move higher in response to improving economic conditions shortly after completion of the
taper."
The policy environment also has improved substantially since 2012, supporting the case for a higher
fed-funds rate. The growth in federal spending and debt has slowed. There's lower risk of U.S. tax rate
increases than in 2010-12. And European Central Bank President Mario Draghi provided a forceful defense
of the euro in 2011 and 2012, explaining lower bond yields there.
The actual results of the Fed's 2014 taper have been decidedly constructive -- more bank lending, improved
growth prospects, reduced uncertainty about the Fed, higher stock prices and still-low bond yields. The latest
economic data show that May vehicle sales were the highest since 2007 while business investment, orders
and confidence gained. Friday's employment data showed that the dollar value of payrolls grew at a 6.2%
annual rate in March through May, the fastest rate since mid-2006.
The taper went through a two-step reaction in 2013-14 -- howls from Wall Street and then acceptance. The
same is likely for interest-rate hikes -- howls and then a recognition that the sooner the Fed gets on with it,
the better for growth and market integrity.
---
Mr. Malpass is president of Encima Global LLC. He served as deputy assistant Treasury secretary in the
Reagan administration and deputy assistant secretary of state in the George H.W. Bush administration.

Silver-Fix Market Faces Major Revamp
Silver-Fix Market Faces Major Revamp
By Francesca Freeman
434 words
7 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B2
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The silver and gold markets are on the verge of a historic shake-up, after participants in the silver market
outlined their vision for a new silver fix.
The London Bullion Market Association, an industry group, said Friday that a survey of around 440 market
participants showed a clear desire for an electronic, auction-based benchmark to replace the London silver fix
after it is published for the last time in August.
Respondents also called for a tradable pricing system, with an increased number of contributors.
This puts the future of the fix -- a daily ritual that has lasted 117 years and provides a benchmark for miners
to settle sales contracts and, more recently, to price such derivatives as exchange-traded funds -- firmly in the
hands of at least five firms.
Proposals on how the new silver fix should be conducted have been submitted by the London Metal
Exchange, exchange-traded fund provider ETF Securities, and commodities-information provider Platts. CME
Group Inc. also has said it is interested in administering a new silver fix, and a person familiar with the matter
said that Thomson Reuters Corp. also is considering a bid for the benchmark.
LBMA Chief Executive Ruth Crowell said the association's members will give their feedback on these
proposals on June 20.
The silver market "wants greater transparency and the influence [of individual market players] to be
distributed more widely," said Courtney Lynn, treasurer of Coeur Mining Inc., the largest listed U.S.
primary-silver producer.
"My only concern is timing," she said. "To get a robust alternative established in two months with all the
regulatory requirements and infrastructure needs isn't going to be easy."
London Silver Market Fixing Ltd., which administers the current system, said it would cease to publish the
daily benchmark from Aug. 14.
Some think the gold fix, a similar benchmark that has been around since 1919, also needs to be overhauled.
The silver and gold fixes came under scrutiny as part of a wider examination of financial benchmarks in the
wake of a global scandal involving the rigging of interest rates.
The prices of silver and gold are set daily in London by way of conference calls between banks -- four for
gold and three for silver. But when Deutsche Bank AG in April declared its intention to leave both processes
as part of a wider reduction of its commodities business, the silver fix administrator called time on the
benchmark.
According to people familiar with the matter, the process was deemed unviable with just two panel members.

The Dow Jones Industrial Average notched its seventh record close of 2014, adding 98.58 points, or 0.6%, to

Gains were strongest among recently beaten-down small stocks. The Russell 2000 Index of small stocks
jumped 2% to close at its highest level in more than two months.
The Russell had slumped nearly 10% on a closing basis after hitting a record in early March. With Thursday's
rally, the Russell 2000 is down 4.5% from its all-time high and down 0.8% for the year.
Providing fuel for a rally in stocks on both sides of the Atlantic Ocean were stimulus efforts from the ECB
aimed at preventing falling prices from undermining the euro zone's recovery. The ECB cut its main lending
rate and introduced a so-called negative deposit rate, in effect charging banks to park cash with the central
bank, to encourage investors to seek higher returns elsewhere.
Additionally, the ECB said it was prepared to take other measures in the future.
The ECB's moves follow a series of stimulative policy steps from the Federal Reserve and Bank of Japan,
which have diminished the payout from government bonds and helped fuel demand for stocks in recent
years.
In an environment of accommodative central-bank policies, "the grinding path" for stocks "is up," said John
Forlines III, chief investment officer at New York-based JAForlines Global Investment Management, which
directly manages about $500 million.
European stocks marked broad gains, as the Stoxx Europe 600 gained 0.4% to close at a 6 1/2-year high.
For weeks, U.S. investors have fixed their attention on the ECB meeting as a likely driver for price swings.
Many said that U.S. stocks had been vulnerable to an underwhelming response from the central bank and
Thursday's move higher served as a relief rally.
The ECB "delivered," said Chris Diaz, portfolio manager and global head of rates at Janus Capital Group,
which oversees about $174 billion. "I was worried about it: the institution has a history of under-delivering
when it comes to market expectations."
ECB policy changes came ahead of Friday's closely watched reading on the U.S. labor market. Economists
expect that nonfarm payrolls will log an increase 210,000 for May. The unemployment rate is expected to
edge up to 6.4% from 6.3% in April.
On Thursday, initial claims for jobless benefits increased by 8,000 to 312,000 in the latest week, roughly in
line with expectations of 310,000. A separate report on Wednesday showed private-sector payrolls in the U.S.
increased less than expected.

Global Finance: Silver Fix Gets More Interest
By Francesca Freeman
567 words
6 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
ETF Securities and Platts added their names to the list of companies interested in overseeing a new London
silver fix.
The exchange-traded funds pioneer and the commodities-information provider said they have joined the
London Metal Exchange and CME Group Inc. in registering interest in providing a replacement for the
benchmarking process, which will be set for the final time in August.
ETF Securities has been in talks with the London Bullion Market Association about producing a silver
benchmark for several weeks, said its founder and chairman, Graham Tuckwell.
"I've put a proposal forward. It's just a matter of refining the detail to get it in an optimal form for LBMA
members," Mr. Tuckwell said.
The LBMA, a trade association, is consulting with market participants to find a replacement for the fix, which
is used as a benchmark by mining companies to settle sales contracts and to price such derivatives as
exchange-traded funds.
ETF Securities' proposal operates through the company's silver fund, which trades on the London Stock
Exchange and is physically settled through the LBMA's clearing system. "The banks that are involved in the
metal markets would put their bids and offers into the auction" for the silver fund, Mr. Tuckwell said. "It's open,
it's transparent, every bid and offer that goes into the auction can be audited and supervised."
London-based Platts said it is also interested in administering the silver fix. "We have held conversations with
the LBMA, and we look forward to continued collaborative engagement regarding price discovery in the silver
market," said a spokeswoman for the McGraw Hill Financial Inc.-owned company.
Financial-information provider Thomson Reuters Corp. also has expressed an interest, according to a person
familiar with the plans.
Previously, the London Metal Exchange, owned by Hong Kong Exchanges & Clearing Ltd., and CME Group,
two major commodities exchanges, had said they are both separately talking with market participants and the
LBMA about providing a new silver fix.
An LBMA spokesman said the body is yet to reach a decision on which proposal to support and it is
"assessing the feedback and considering those who may be interested in assisting us" in launching a new
benchmark.
The silver fix has gone through various iterations in its 117-year history. Most recently, it has been set daily at
noon in London by way of a conference call involving representatives from Deutsche Bank AG, HSBC
Holdings PLC and Bank of Nova Scotia. But when Deutsche Bank last month declared its intention to leave
the process as part of a wider reduction of its commodities business, the fix administrator announced the end
of the benchmark.
The silver fix and the gold fix, which is set by four banks and has been around since 1919, have recently
come under the scrutiny of regulators as part of a broader examination of financial benchmarks in the wake of
a global scandal involving the rigging of interest rates.
Last month, Barclays was fined GBP 26 million ($43.5 million) by the U.K.'s Financial Conduct Authority for
lax controls after one of its traders manipulated the gold fix at the expense of a client.
Thomson Reuters and Platts compete with News Corp's Dow Jones & Co., publisher of The Wall Street
Journal and Dow Jones Newswires, on business news.

ARENA --- World Cup 2014 -- Soccer in Spanish: Translation Unnecessary
ARENA --- World Cup 2014 -- Soccer in Spanish: Translation Unnecessary
By Reed Johnson
664 words
6 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J

English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
If you're an English-speaking World Cup viewer in the U.S., you'll most likely be tuning in to ABC or ESPN
this summer for game telecasts from Brazil. Or perhaps the BBC, the gold standard of dispassionate sports
analysis.
But the Spanish-language network Univision Deportes also plans to compete hard for English-speaking World
Cup fans by offering what it claims is a more colorful, intense and at times enthusiastically partisan
soccer-watching experience. Even if you don't speak the language, the company is betting you'll want to hear
announcers heralding each score with the exclamation "Golazo!"
"We strongly think that watching the game in Spanish is just more fun," said Daniele Grassi, senior director
for sport digital for Univision Deportes, the two-year-old, soccer-centric sports division of Univision
Communications Inc. "So we think that even the international community that is not Spanish-speaking, or the
main language is not Spanish, or English-speaking users, will want to watch the games with us."
Grassi has cause for optimism. During the 2010 World Cup in South Africa, a number of English-language
media commentators, as well as fans online, expressed their penchant for viewing matches on Univision.
Nate Berg, writing for the New Republic, declared that even though he didn't speak the language fluently, "I
vastly prefer my futbol en espanol." He praised Univision's Pablo Ramirez as "arguably the most entertaining
soccer announcer calling the game today," an opinion shared by a number of soccer cognoscenti.
Univision performed well at the 2010 World Cup, even though it hadn't yet launched Univision Deportes. In 22
of the South African tournament's 64 games, the Spanish-language broadcaster outperformed ESPN and its
parent company, ABC, according to Univision figures. For the 2010 final, Univision had 8.3 million total
viewers compared with ABC's 13.1 million, the company says.
Univision Deportes, which holds exclusive U.S. Spanish-language TV and digital rights to this year's World
Cup, hopes to boost those numbers. The network will have three dedicated studios in Brazil, is dispatching a
team of 150 and will deploy newfangled technologies such as Augmented Reality graphics and multi-touch
screens.
Also, Grassi said, Univision for the first time will devote equal resources to covering the U.S. team and
Mexico's El Tri. That binational focus reflects the network's desire to appeal both to English-speaking drop-ins
and later-generation U.S. Latinos.
"Second-generation, third-generation Hispanics, they start to feel as American as they are Hispanic," he said.
"So they will follow the United States and their own team."
As the U.S. Latino population has soared to more than 50 million, it has fed gains in soccer's U.S. popularity.
Advertisers and media companies also are attuning to English-speaking consumers who happen to have an
affinity for Latin culture. Besides Univision, another Spanish-language sports network that may be poised to
siphon off English-speaking soccer fans in years to come is ESPN Deportes.
Rodolfo Martinez, vice president of production for ESPN International and ESPN Deportes, said his
company's soccer coverage has attracted English-speaking fans by adopting the spirit and "rhythm" of
Spanish-language announcers, even when the telecasts are in English.
He cited play-by-play announcer Fernando Palomo, a bilingual Salvadoran-American who will be calling
World Cup games for ESPN, in English, featuring Mexico, Honduras and other Latin American teams.
But, Martinez said, Palomo will use the emotive Latin style for announcing goals and deliver his play-by-play
in the more rapid manner of Spanish-language telecasts. He will also pronounce Latin players' names with
the correct accents and emphases.

Gold From North Korea Stymies U.S. Firms
Gold From North Korea Stymies U.S. Firms
By Joel Schectman
843 words
5 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
As companies scrambled to meet a deadline to report whether their suppliers used minerals from mines
controlled by armed groups in the Congo region, they stumbled on something even more troubling: Many of
their products may contain North Korean gold.
Dozens of companies disclosed over the past week that their suppliers used gold refined by North Korea's
central bank. These companies include Hewlett-Packard Co., Ralph Lauren Corp., International Business
Machines Corp., Rockwell Automation Corp. and Williams-Sonoma Inc.
IBM, for example, revealed the North Korean gold was used to make its memory-storage systems.
U.S. sanctions law bars importing materials from North Korea even if they come from deep within a supply
chain and are in a completely different form by the time they reach the end user, sanctions experts said. "It's
a problem even if the raw materials are coming very indirectly through suppliers," said Alexandra
Lopez-Casero, an attorney at Nixon Peabody LLP who specializes in sanctions.
North Korea's central bank provides currency for the regime and refines gold. Until 2006, the bank refined
gold bars that were certified by the London Bullion Market Association, a gold marketplace, according to
public records. The bank's refinery likely continues to produce gold, said Bruce Calder, vice president of
Claigan Environmental Inc., a supply-compliance consulting firm that tracks suppliers.
Many of the companies apparently uncovered the North Korean connection for the first time as they searched
their supply chains for so-called "conflict minerals." A provision of the Dodd-Frank Act compelled companies
to question their suppliers and disclose by Monday whether gold, tungsten, tantalum or tin used in their
products came from mines controlled by armed groups in the war-torn Congo region.
An H-P spokeswoman said the company learned that "a small number" of its suppliers may have used the
North Korean refiner as it rooted through its supply chain in January. As part of its disclosure, H-P listed every
refiner its suppliers used rather than trying to determine which facilities made metal that ultimately went into
H-P products.
But after some of its suppliers reported they were using the North Korean facility, the company began an
investigation. "The responses we have received from our suppliers have told us it hasn't been used in H-P
production. . . . We are relying on our suppliers -- we haven't been on the ground," the spokeswoman said.
"We were aware there may be a question, and we wanted to disclose what we have done rather than try to
hide it."
In an emailed statement, an IBM spokesman said the company expects its suppliers to follow conflict-mineral
guidelines and "procure minerals from responsible sources."
A spokeswoman for Ralph Lauren said listing North Korea's central bank was an error and the company
plans to file an amended return with the Securities and Exchange Commission. While some of the company's
suppliers may use gold from the facility, she said, she has confirmed that no metal from North Korea found
its way into Ralph Lauren products.
A spokeswoman for Williams-Sonoma said in an email that the company is investigating the issue and
doesn't yet have "definitive information." She noted that "the company is several layers removed from the
mining of minerals" and doesn't purchase raw minerals. The company believes it received word of the North
Korean facility through a supplier to one of its vendors.
How did so many companies end up reporting North Korean gold in their supply chain, a possible sanctions
violation? Many may not have realized that the list they were getting from their suppliers included a North
Korean facility because of an error in the template they distributed.

--- Page 60 ---
Page 60 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
To query their suppliers, companies relied on a template created by the nonprofit Conflict- Free Sourcing
Initiative. An error in an earlier version of the template mistakenly listed the location of the Central Bank of
DPR [Democratic People's Republic] of Korea as South Korea, said Julie Schindall, a spokeswoman for the
initiative. When suppliers entered the smelter code, South Korea was listed by default.
The North Korean smelter has been removed from the latest version of the template, said Ms. Schindall, "but
whether or not suppliers in China are still using this smelter would be impossible for us to know."
Noting that its filing listed the Central Bank of DPR as being in South Korea, a Rockwell Automation
spokesman said, "It seems like everyone was just working off the same piece of paper." He declined to
comment further.
Michael Littenberg, an attorney who deals with conflict-mineral disclosures, said around 20 corporate clients
have discovered North Korean gold was used by their suppliers. The clients were able to avoid disclosing the
smelter by engaging in additional investigation and determining the North Korean gold didn't make it into their
products. "I think it reveals there is still a long way to go in understanding supply chains," Mr. Littenberg said.

India's Rajan May Have to Increase Rates to Head Off Modi
Heard on the Street
India's Rajan May Have to Increase Rates to Head Off Modi
By Abheek Bhattacharya
513 words
4 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C16
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
[Financial Analysis and Commentary]
Unlike investors overjoyed with Narendra Modi's election, Reserve Bank of India Gov. Raghuram Rajan is
staying calm.
Mr. Rajan's move to hold interest rates steady Tuesday is a reminder of the challenges India's economy
faces. Whatever new growth investors expect Mr. Modi to deliver, retail inflation was high at 8.6% in April.
Mr. Rajan did lower the statutory portion of deposits banks must park in government bonds by half a
percentage point -- cash the banks can now lend out instead.
Yet this seems more a sign of overhauling antiquated regulations than real easing. And there may be little
immediate effect, since the banking system's bondholdings actually exceeded the requirement by six
percentage points.
Even as the central bank sounds pleased that it will hit its 8% inflation target by next January, there are
plenty of reasons Mr. Rajan might find himself in rate-raising mode later this year.
First among them is the possibility of a Modi-sparked growth rebound. With inflation already so high, it is
hard to see India's economy expanding more quickly in the short term without more price pressures.
After years of delays in overhauls that could unlock supply-side bottlenecks, especially infrastructure, India's
potential growth rate was severely compromised to around 5% last year, according to J.P. Morgan.
Growing more rapidly than that, by this calculation, will trigger more inflation.
Mr. Modi could overhaul India's growth potential, but such a process would take years. He may initially
worsen inflation if he kick-starts new investment -- by generating demand for capital goods before any
reforms can increase local supply, says J.P. Morgan's Jahangir Aziz. And if companies import capital goods,
that will widen the trade deficit, weaken the rupee and again risk inflation.
Another risk is food inflation which is likely to worsen this summer. Mr. Modi can help by releasing
government food stocks and initiating changes that destroy food cartels. Mr. Rajan and investors are waiting
for Mr. Modi's budget next month to see if he takes these steps, and the politically tougher decisions to trim
fuel subsidies and reduce the government's deficit.
Then there is India's external front. On the surface, all is quiet, with the current-account deficit down to a
manageable 1.7% of GDP last fiscal year from 4.7% the year before.
Still, 65% of the current-account adjustment comes courtesy of government curbs on imports of gold and
India's importing fewer manufactured goods, neither of which may last. In addition, the deficit was almost
entirely financed by fickle portfolio inflows in the March quarter.
If the Federal Reserve's departure from easy-money policies again sends emerging markets into turmoil, Mr.
Rajan may have to raise interest rates to defend the currency, as he did last year. That should be a reality
check for investors who think Mr. Modi's arrival alone ushers in a new Indian boom.

Bitcoin's Futile Quest to Be a Currency
Bitcoin's Futile Quest to Be a Currency
By Lawrence Parks
729 words
2 June 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A13
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Bitcoin is a fascinating and ingenious technology, but most promoters are mindful of neither the monetary nor
the tax issues. For all practical purposes IRS regulations issued in March preclude bitcoins from being used
as an alternative currency.
The IRS treats bitcoins as property. The result is that bitcoin transactions trigger a taxable event. Buyers incur
a tax liability for the difference in dollars between what they paid for a bitcoin when they acquired it and the
dollar value attributed to the bitcoin when they spend it. Sellers of course are subject to a tax based on the
dollar value of the bitcoins they receive for a good or service.
To comply with these tax regulations, buyers and sellers must log all bitcoin transactions and report them at
tax time. For transactions that require future payment, buyers and sellers undertake an exchange-rate risk
involving the dollar value of bitcoins. This will greatly reduce, or perhaps eliminate entirely, using bitcoins for
settling future payments, which is the principal use of money.
Some bitcoin zealots reject the effect of triggering taxable events on the theory that bitcoin transactions are
anonymous. That is arguable. What is not arguable is that one who doesn't report a taxable bitcoin gain is
guilty of tax fraud, which is a felony.
In other words, the future of bitcoins depends on users willing to log all transactions, report them at tax time,
and pay a tax or to engage in tax fraud. As soon as one of them ends up in prison, that will be the end of it.
Why bitcoins to begin with? We already have a virtual currency, the dollar, which has the purported benefit of
being the world's "reserve currency."
Some people see a problem because dollars can be created at the whim of the Federal Reserve, and as
former Fed Chairman Alan Greenspan once put it, "without limit." This depreciates the purchasing power of
dollars saved or promised for future payment, such as pensions. At least the quantity of bitcoins is
supposedly limited by the mathematical algorithm that creates them. As an interesting aside, the bitcoin folks
call the creation process "mining," an allusion to real money.
Bitcoin supporters understand that dollars are no longer money in the classical sense, i.e., something that
has a unit of dimension defined in the physical world, as Sir Isaac Newton put it circa 1699 when he was
England's Warden of the Mint. With neither debate nor anyone voting for it, the dollar has been transmogrified
into an ethereal concept of money without any tie to the physical world, created out of nothing, and forced into
circulation with legal tender laws.
As Australian comedian Michael Connell so brilliantly put it, what we used to call money has been
transformed into "the idea of money." Mr. Connell's metaphor is that it's like playing musical chairs, but
instead of chairs there is the "idea of chairs." It is absurd.
This brings to mind a related issue: U.S. Gold and Silver Eagles, which are legal tender for their face
amounts under Title 31 of the United States Code. Yet as with bitcoins, the IRS arbitrarily treats these coins
not as currency but as property, thereby preventing their use as money.
Consider: If you pay your taxes with a $50 Gold Eagle, you get credit for $50. No mystery there. However, if
you spend a $50 Gold Eagle (or a $1 Silver Eagle), you trigger a taxable event on the difference between
what you paid for the Gold Eagle and the market value of the gold in the coin when you spend it. Two recent
cases challenging the IRS on this matter went up to the Supreme Court, but the court declined to hear the
cases.
It is thanks to the IRS that no one uses so-called gold clauses, which would give creditors the right to
demand Gold Eagles as payment in contracts. In other words, IRS policy that has no statutory authorization
has rendered inoperative gold clause legislation passed by Congress in 1977.

--- Page 69 ---
Page 69 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Bitcoins have neither the advantage of being legal tender nor laws enabling their use. Folks who are
accumulating bitcoins should take note.
---
Mr. Parks is the executive director of the Foundation for the Advancement of Monetary Education.

Page 70 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Gold Loses Luster as Conflicts Ease
Commodities Report
Gold Loses Luster as Conflicts Ease
By Ira Iosebashvili
625 words
31 May 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B4
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Gold prices fell to their lowest level in nearly four months, as investors abandon the precious metal amid
easing tensions between Russia and Ukraine.
Investors say they see little reason to hold gold now that the threat of a wider conflict has faded following the
results of Ukraine's May 25 presidential election, which Russian President Vladimir Putin has said he is willing
to recognize. As well, indicators point to steady, if slow, growth in the U.S. economy, and stocks are rising.
This lessens investors' desire to maintain exposure to an asset that doesn't offer any yield.
Gold's decline stands in contrast to the performance of U.S. government bonds and Japan's currency.
Treasurys and the yen, which like gold are long-standing haven investments, are benefiting from worries
about growth in other corners of the world, namely Europe and China.
On Friday, gold futures fell $10.70, or 0.9%, to $1,245.60 an ounce, the lowest since Jan. 31. For May, gold
prices slid 3.9%, the biggest monthly price decline since November.
"If you take political risk out of the equation, the pressures are for gold to go down," said John Rutledge, an
investment strategist at Safanad, a New York asset-management firm. Mr. Rutledge declined to disclose his
firm's assets under management or whether it had any bets on gold.
The price of the precious metal hovered around $1,300 an ounce for most of May, as traders and investors
scooped up the metal amid the worst East-West standoff since the Cold War. Some traders use gold as a
hedge, or a way of stabilizing portfolio performance, in times of economic or political uncertainty, believing it
will hold its value while other assets decline. Gold remains higher for the year to date, by 3.6%.
The scales tipped earlier this past week, after the Ukraine election went smoothly and Russia indicated a
willingness to work with the new government. Although fighting between pro-Russian separatists and forces
loyal to Kiev continues, investors increasingly view the conflict as a localized one, with little chance of spilling
beyond Ukraine's borders.
Hedge funds and other investors have cut their bets on the precious metal by a third since the beginning of
May, according to Commodity Futures Trading Commission data. The amount of gold held by SPDR Gold
Shares, the biggest exchange-traded fund backed by physical gold, is around five-year lows. Gold has fallen
nearly 10% from its March high.
Demand for physical gold from Asia has started to erode as well. In April, the World Gold Council warned
that appetite for gold in China, the world's biggest buyer, is waning after investors' decadelong buying spree.
An association of Hong Kong jewelers earlier in May said demand for gold in China is off 30% from last year.
Asian demand was one of the last remaining sources of support for gold this year, said Daniel Belchers,
portfolio manager at Threadneedle Asset Management, which has $1.3 billion in commodities investments.
Mr. Belchers has been bearish on gold. He has reduced his gold holding to 8% of his portfolio, compared
with gold's 11% weighting in the DJ UBS Commodity Index.
Gold prices also have been hit by a spate of stronger-than-expected economic data this past week. The data
hasn't changed expectations about when the Federal Reserve will raise interest rates, an event that many
investors and traders expected to be negative for gold.
"Gold is in no man's land at the moment," said Peter Hug, global trading director at Kitco Metals. "From a
short-term point of view, the market looks lousy."

WEEKEND INVESTOR --- The New Basics -- Wealth Effect: Investing in Wine? Sip, Don't Gulp
WEEKEND INVESTOR --- The New Basics -- Wealth Effect: Investing in Wine? Sip, Don't Gulp
By Brett Arends
871 words
31 May 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B8
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Prices for fine Italian wines are up in the past year. But Bordeaux prices are down. Champagne is up. But
wines from the Rhone are down.
But if you are thinking that now is the time to do some investing, caveat emptor: Wine is a trickier bet than it
may seem.
"The Price of Fine Wine," a report published earlier this year by economics professors Elroy Dimson of the
London Business School, Peter Rousseau of Vanderbilt University and Christophe Spaenjers of HEC Paris,
may at first blush comfort those who hope that their wine cellars will prove a sound investment as well as
something that will impress their friends.
After mining historical price data for top clarets going back to 1899, including the prices fetched in auctions
before World War I, the researchers calculated that over the entire period, the prices of these wines beat
inflation by an average of 5.3 percentage points a year.
That is far ahead of the returns earned from U.S. government bonds or cash. And it isn't that far below the
average return on stocks, which -- depending on which numbers you use -- have earned somewhere around
six percentage points above inflation over that period.
Potential investors also may hope that there are bargains available among the top wines today. Prices, which
had boomed over the previous decade thanks to demand from wealthy Chinese investors, have come
crashing down again with the downturn in emerging-market stocks.
The Liv-Ex 1000, a broad index of fine wines, has fallen by 12% from its peak nearly three years ago. The
most exclusive wines have fallen even further: The Liv-Ex 100 index of the top 100 wines is down by a third
over the same period. Both indexes are developed and tracked by the London International Vintners'
Exchange (Liv-Ex, for short), the world's leading electronic wine market.
"There was a significant bull run, driven mainly by China," up to 2011, says Anthony Maxwell, a director at
Liv-Ex. Meanwhile, wine growers pushed prices "to the edge," especially for 2009 and 2010 vintages, which
oenophiles rate as two of the greatest Bordeaux vintages ever.
But it was always unclear how much of the wine going to mainland China was actually being consumed,
rather than being hoarded for further speculation, he says. The boom began bursting drastically in August
2011. Since then, hoarders have been dumping surplus inventory, driving down prices.
But before you rush to build your own wine cellar, there are a few issues to bear in mind.
The first is that investment returns on wine have been lumpy and inconsistent, according to the research
conducted by Profs. Dimson, Rousseau and Spaenjers.
For example, they found that wine prices showed little gain over inflation for the first 25 years of the 20th
century. Prices skyrocketed during World War II, when the Nazis occupied France, and slumped afterward.
The professors also estimate storage and insurance costs of around 1.2% a year, reducing their estimated
net after-inflation returns to 4.1% -- substantially below that of stocks.
And unlike stocks, bonds and real estate, wine can't be valued in the standard manner -- that is, by
comparing the price of an investment to its expected cash flows. Wine, like gold, commodities and other
collectible assets such as art and luxury cars, generates no cash flow. So to some extent investing involves a
lot of guesswork.

--- Page 72 ---
Page 72 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Furthermore, wine hardly offers the rich a store of value. One of the major drivers of higher wine prices has
been the rising prosperity of the superwealthy. A worsening of economic conditions may hit the value of their
cellars along with their stocks and other investments.
Then there comes the vexed issue of which wines to buy. As noted earlier, wines from different regions have
fared very differently in the past year. And that isn't unusual. For example, the Liv-Ex index of
Lafite-Rothschilds has quadrupled in price in a decade, while its index of Sauternes has barely risen at all.
In the past few years, the collapse in prices for the Liv-Ex 100 has mainly been driven by those of the
best-known Bordeaux, which dominate the index and which were most coveted in China. Meanwhile, top
wines from Italy, Champagne and the New World, as measured by Liv-Ex, have continued upward.
Everyone will have an opinion. Miles Davis, who runs Wine Asset Managers, a London-based wine
investment company with $20 million under management, says he has recently been buying up top Bordeaux
vintages from 1995 and 1996, which he believes offer especially good value.
If there is a solid conclusion from the research, it is that one group of people, at least, may be well advised to
buy top-quality wines to store for the long term: Those who plan someday to drink them.
The best red wines can take decades to mature fully. Instead of spending thousands of dollars per bottle to
buy them at their peak, the serious oenophile will almost surely get a better deal by buying them early and
storing them.

that have shown signs of improvement as stocks grind higher.

benefits fell to 300,000, versus an expected decline to 319,000.

Global Finance: Two Groups Vie for Silver Standard
Global Finance: Two Groups Vie for Silver Standard
By Francesca Freeman
528 words
30 May 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Two of the world's leading commodities exchanges are competing to fix London's silver fix.
The London Metal Exchange and CME Group each said Thursday they are separately talking with market
participants about hosting a new pricing benchmark after the 117-year-old London silver fix is set for the last
time on Aug. 14.
While the LME's proposal is relatively advanced, CME Group is only in the early stages of considering a new
silver fix, people with knowledge of the matter said. Both exchanges are considering benchmarks that are
based on actual trades of the metal, as is the case with the current system.
"We are working closely with the precious metals industry and the [London Bullion Market Association] to
reduce market disruption by helping to find a robust transaction-based way to set the daily spot price so the
markets can continue to work efficiently and seamlessly," said Harriet Hunnable, managing director of metals
at CME Group.
The LME said it already has a proposal that will "provide best-practice regulatory compliance while
maintaining the global position of the London market." The LME, owned by Hong Kong Exchanges and
Clearing Ltd., said it would give more detail "at the appropriate time once the market consultation is
complete."
The news was met with cautious optimism.
"Our concern as a producer is being able to transact at the fix," said Courtney Lynn, treasurer of Coeur
Mining Inc., the largest listed U.S. primary-silver producer. "The challenge is going to be getting broad
acceptance" of a new benchmark, she said.
A spokesman for the LBMA said no decision had been made as to who would administer the fix after Aug. 14.
As well as setting a price that is used by mining companies to settle sales contracts, the fix is used to price
derivatives such as exchange-traded funds. It also affects global jewelry prices.
Ross Norman, CEO of London-based bullion dealer Sharps Pixley, said he had concerns over the time frame.
"To replicate . . . a system that has developed over 117 years is tough to do," he said.
The silver fix has gone through various iterations in its history. Most recently, it has been set daily at noon by
way of a conference call involving representatives from Deutsche Bank AG, HSBC Holdings PLC and Bank of
Nova Scotia. But the benchmark was considered unviable, according to people familiar with the matter, after
Deutsche Bank declared its intention to leave the process as part of a wider reduction of its commodities
business. As a result, last month London Silver Market Fixing Ltd., which administers the fix, announced the
end of the benchmark.
The silver fix and the gold fix, which is set by four banks and has been around since 1919, recently have
come under the scrutiny of regulators as part of a broader examination of financial benchmarks in the wake of
a global scandal involving the rigging of interest rates.
Representatives of Deutsche Bank and HSBC declined to comment. A spokesman for Bank of Nova Scotia
didn't respond to a request for comment.

Investors Return to Emerging World --- In Big Economies, Interest Rates Fall As Growth Outlook Turns Cloudier
Credit Markets
Investors Return to Emerging World --- In Big Economies, Interest Rates Fall As Growth Outlook
Turns Cloudier
By Cynthia Lin, Tommy Stubbington and Min Zeng
805 words
29 May 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Uncertain about the pace of global economic growth, investors piled into U.S. and European government
bonds, extending a rally that few expected at the beginning of the year.
Wednesday's buying sent yields on Treasurys and other sovereign debt to their lowest levels since last
summer.
Investors remain wary about the global economy. Germany reported an unexpected uptick in unemployment
while the U.S. is forecast to report its economy shrank last quarter.
The yield on the 10-year U.S. Treasury note fell to 2.440%, the lowest closing level since June 2013. Bond
yields fall when prices rise.
Demand was equally strong across the Atlantic, amid expectations that the European Central Bank could cut
interest rates as soon as next week to support the economy. The yield on 10-year German bunds fell 0.05
percentage point to 1.287%, the lowest since May 2013, according to Tradeweb. The yield on 10-year U.K.
gilts fell 0.09 percentage point to 2.550%, near the weakest level since last August.
Traders said another catalyst for the rally was typical month-end buying by fund managers to better align their
portfolios with underlying indexes. Many fund managers are holding smaller positions in U.S. debt than
market benchmarks in the wake of a Treasury rally that has brought the 10-year yield down from 3% at the
end of 2013.
U.S. banks also have been big buyers of government debt. Treasury bondholdings of commercial banks and
savings institutions rose to $237.2 billion at the end of March, the highest since 1995, according to data from
the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp.
Traders and analysts said the purchases have been driven by tighter banking regulations aiming at improving
banks' balance sheets after the 2008 financial crisis. Overhauls such as the Dodd-Frank financial law in the
U.S. and global regulations require financial institutions to hold more high-graded debt. The $12 trillion U.S.
Treasury bond market is the world's most liquid bond market.
The wave of bond buying is the latest chapter in a yearlong government-bond rally that has surprised many
investors and unexpectedly made safe debt one of the strongest-performing asset classes in financial
markets.
In Europe, falling inflation and anemic growth have fueled steady demand for German debt, the euro zone's
gold standard. Stronger growth in the U.K. hasn't damped demand for British government debt, with 10-year
yields falling to a nine-month low.
"We've had a rally in some other sovereign debt markets, making Treasurys look cheaper," said Gary Pollack,
head of fixed-income trading at Deutsche Bank's private wealth-management unit.
Wednesday's climb wasn't limited to the debt perceived by investors as safest. Yields on bonds issued by
economically weaker European nations such as Spain, Italy and Portugal also declined. Spain's 10-year yield
fell as far as 2.793%, marking a record low.
"Inflation has turned out to be lower than people expected -- that makes bond yields look less outrageously
low," said Adam Cordery, head of European fixed income at Santander Asset Management, which manages
147 billion euros ($199.8 billion) of assets.

--- Page 77 ---
Page 77 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Cordery has favored safe-harbor bonds in his portfolio since the second half of last year, betting that
weak growth and accommodative central banks would cap any rise in yields.
"All the major central banks are making noises about keeping rates lower for longer," he said. "Even the Bank
of England doesn't sound hawkish, and they're supposed to be the one that moves first."
That yields hover around the year's lows while U.S. economic data has been improving has many bond
analysts scratching their heads.
"We who are usually some of the most bullish in the herd are having a hard time reconciling generally OK
data" with falling yields, said David Ader, government-bond strategist at CRT Capital Group.
Many analysts point to the heavily skewed net trading position at the start of the year, with many investors
betting Treasury prices would fall as the economic recovery picked up pace. Those bets were hit by a U.S.
slowdown last quarter, causing many to unwind the so-called shorts.
"The market in general has been caught off guard by the strength in Treasurys this year," Mr. Pollack said,
adding that he doesn't see yields sinking much further from here.
Like many others, Mr. Pollack sees the 10-year yield ending the year around 3% as U.S. economic growth
accelerates.
