Barclays Is Fined in Gold-Fix Probe
Barclays Is Fined in Gold-Fix Probe
By Francesca Freeman
597 words
24 May 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B2
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The U.K.'s top financial watchdog has fined Barclays PLC GBP 26 million ($43.9 million) for lax controls after
one of its traders manipulated a key gold benchmark at the expense of a client.
The regulator's latest action comes amid investigations by authorities across the globe into alleged rigging of
interest rates and currencies by bank traders to benefit their positions.
The U.K.'s Financial Conduct Authority said Friday that the manipulation of the London gold fix resulted from
Barclays "failing to adequately manage conflicts of interest between itself and its customers."
The regulator said this latest incident was especially disappointing since Barclays already had reached a
$450 million settlement in 2012 with U.S. and U.K. regulators and admitted wrongdoing for its role in
manipulating the London interbank offered rate, or Libor.
The FCA said former Barclays trader Daniel James Plunkett exploited weaknesses in the bank's systems to
influence the gold fix, which plays a key role in the $20 trillion-a-day gold market. As a result of the incident,
which took place on June 28, 2012, Barclays avoided paying out $3.9 million to a client on a derivatives
contract, although it later compensated the customer in full, the FCA said. The incident came to light only
after the customer had complained to Barclays, the FCA said.
In addition to the bank's penalty, the regulator also fined Mr. Plunkett GBP 95,600 and banned him for life
from any regulated financial-services role. He left Barclays in 2012, according to a person familiar with the
matter. Mr. Plunkett couldn't be reached for comment.
"We very much regret the situation that led to this settlement. Barclays has undertaken a significant amount
of work to enhance our systems and controls and is committed to the highest standards across all of our
operations," Barclays Chief Executive Antony Jenkins said.
The process of setting gold's benchmark price has come under scrutiny from market regulators, including the
FCA as well as authorities in the U.S. and Europe, as part of a wider investigation into potential price-rigging
across markets.
The gold fix, a process established in 1919, is set by a group of banks in London twice each day. It is used,
for example, to price central-bank assets and the value of exchange-traded funds tied to gold. The other
banks on the London panel are Bank of Nova Scotia, HSBC Holdings PLC and Societe Generale SA.
Deutsche Bank AG recently resigned from the gold-fixing process, as well as a similar process for setting the
silver benchmark, after a three-month search failed to find a buyer for its seats on the panels.
"If [the gold-fixing banks] want to continue to operate the fix in any way like they're doing, they will need to be
a lot more transparent about what's going on," said Brian Lucey, finance professor at Trinity College Dublin
who has studied the gold market. "At this stage, there is a bit of a question mark hanging over its future."
Barclays is one of several banks that have been penalized in connection with efforts to manipulate Libor and
other interest-rate benchmarks. The bank is also one of about a dozen lenders that have become ensnared in
an investigation into possible wrongdoing in the global currencies market.
More than 20 traders and other employees have been fired or suspended in connection with the investigation
into possible misconduct in the foreign-exchange market.

Gold's Shine Dims in India --- Prices Are Sliding as Investors in the Country Expect Local Supplies to Rise
Commodities Report
Gold's Shine Dims in India --- Prices Are Sliding as Investors in the Country Expect Local Supplies to
Rise
By Biman Mukherji and Debiprasad Nayak
788 words
22 May 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Gold prices are falling in India, as investors bet that looser restrictions on imports will end a shortage in the
world's second-biggest market for the precious metal. But some analysts are predicting demand will quickly
rebound as gold becomes cheaper for Indian investors and jewelers.
On Wednesday, India's central bank said it would allow private trading companies to bring gold into the
country. Gold dealers expect the newly elected government, led by Narendra Modi, to make other changes
that allow more of the metal to enter India.
Gold costs $1,480 a troy ounce in India, down from about $1,580 a week ago. In the global futures market,
gold for June delivery fell 0.5% to $1,288.10 an ounce Wednesday on the New York Mercantile Exchange's
Comex division.
Gold is more expensive in India due to taxes on imports and other transactions, as well as government curbs
on who can import gold and how much they can import without re-exporting. Dealers also charge a premium
because strong demand from investors and jewelers keep supplies tight.
Indian investors are selling gold because they expect local supplies to rise, causing that premium to shrink.
Also, jewelers are purchasing only as much as they need immediately, said Girish Choksi, a large
Ahmedabad-based bullion dealer. "The prices have already come down because the new government is
expected to reduce import duties. We think that the prices will come down further," he said. "That is why
everybody is selling."
Ultimately, the lifting of import restrictions should lead to stronger demand in India, which would support
prices globally, analysts say. Indian investors and jewelers buy more gold than any other country except
China. Demand fell sharply last year after the Indian government throttled imports to narrow the country's
trade deficit.
"You've got one of the largest consumers of gold in the world being compromised because the government
has put an artificial tax on it, and removing it should make demand and price go up," said Joe Wickwire,
manager of the $1.2 billion Fidelity Select Gold Portfolio. Mr. Wickwire has been bullish on gold since the
start of the year, and has been increasing his holdings of gold mining companies to benefit from higher gold
prices.
To be sure, many analysts expect gold prices to fall this year, no matter what happens in India. The Federal
Reserve is expected to raise interest rates next year, diminishing the appeal of gold, which yields nothing.
Investors also are putting money in riskier assets, including stocks and high-yielding bonds, as global
economic conditions improve.
On Wednesday, gold futures prices dipped after minutes from the Fed's April meeting showed the U.S.'s
central bank hadn't changed its economic outlook, meaning a rate increase is still likely on the way.
About 40% of the gold purchased in India is by investors. The country's previous government, worried about
a ballooning current-account deficit and a slide in the value of the rupee, raised import taxes on gold and
required 20% of imports to be re-exported as jewelry and other finished products.
Many Indians are selling gold now, in case the tax is lifted.
"We have come here to sell about 20 grams of coins that we purchased last year. We don't want to take any
risk as prices are expected to fall further," said 42-year-old Neha Shah, a Mumbai resident, as she waited
inside a jewelry shop.

--- Page 83 ---
Page 83 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Indian buying of gold bars and coins plunged 54% to 45 tons during the first quarter of 2014 from a year
earlier. Jewelry sales fell 9% to 145.6 tons. Gold purchases were especially high last year, as a slump in
prices provoked heavy buying by bargain-hunters across Asia.
"I think [gold] could show a huge comeback, depending on how the [new Indian government] policy shapes
up," said P.R. Somasundaram, managing director at the World Gold Council's India office. The council
forecasts India's 2014 gold demand will be between 900 and 1,000 metric tons, compared with 975 tons last
year.
For now, the rush to sell is on.
"We are getting more sellers than buyers these days," said Jitendra Kantilal Jain, proprietor of Jugraj Kantilal
& Co., a large dealer in recycled gold in the western Indian city of Mumbai's Zaveri Bazar, the largest gold
market. "Most of these sellers are coming to offload their bars and coins, which they purchased a few months
back," he said.

Road to Conflict: Amazon Murder Mystery Raises Tensions in Brazil
Road to Conflict: Amazon Murder Mystery Raises Tensions in Brazil
By John Lyons
2,091 words
20 May 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
HUMAITA, Brazil -- On a muggy February day, police made a grisly find on an Indian reservation in Brazil's
Amazon: the bodies of three men last seen driving toward the reserve on the Trans-Amazon highway, the
massive road Brazil bulldozed deep into the rain forest in the 1970s.
The discovery added grim clues to a murder case that has pitted the Amazon settler town of Humaita against
the Tenharim Indian reservation 80 miles to the east. Police charged six Tenharim Indians this month with
murder -- charges the six have denied as they await trial. In Humaita, where two of the victims lived, police
say locals rioted and set fires at an Indian health clinic and aid agency. There is ominous talk, locals say, of
more violence.
The triple murder exposes a troubling legacy of the Amazon's signature road. Four decades after Brazil
plowed a rutted red-clay track some 2,500 miles into its vast rain forest interior, the Trans-Amazon remains a
source of tension between the settlers who came with it, and the Indians who were living in its path. And
those tensions have boiled over as Brazil makes a new push to develop the world's largest tropical rain forest.
Some of those tensions are surfacing now because the Amazon region -- about the size of Western Europe --
has undergone a remarkable population boom, rising 50% in two decades to 25 million people as mines,
ranches and soy farms attract migrants. Workers are flocking here to help pave roads and build a string of
huge hydroelectric dams planned for the region's big rivers.
At the same time, Indian populations, once decimated by disease, are rising. With higher birthrates and
access to health care, the number of Indians living on reservations is increasing at nearly four times the
yearly rate of the general population; interests between these two growing populations have collided at many
steps of development.
The Kayapo tribe, for example, which opposed the Trans-Amazon, is leading protests against the
construction of the giant Belo Monte dam near the town of Altamira, where construction of the massive
thoroughfare was inaugurated in 1970. Further west on the Trans-Amazon near the town of Jacareacanga,
the Munduruku tribe has formed militias to expel illegal gold miners from their land. Federal police say they
don't track figures on land-dispute crime, but Brazil's Indigenous Missionary Council, a Catholic advocacy
group opposing Amazon development, says some 450 Indians were killed between 2003 and 2010, often in
fights over property and other disputes.
Dealing with the Amazon conflicts has become a new priority for Brazilian President Dilma Rousseff, who
campaigned four years ago on driving more development in the Amazon and is seeking re-election this year.
In the aftermath of the murders and riot in Humaita, Ms. Rousseff sent an army general and scores of soldiers
to the small town keep the peace.
Such force, though, is rare in this region, where frontier justice can be as relevant as the legal kind. Around
250 agents enforce federal crimes in the entire Amazonas state, where the murders took place.
Indeed, the three murdered settlers were missing for 10 days before Federal Police made the six-hour drive
over dirt roads and two ferries to search the reservation. That came after enraged residents of Humaita rioted
to demand an investigation, police say, setting fire to the local office of the federal Indian services agency,
Funai, an Indian health clinic, boats and a dozen pickup trucks in a night of arson that sent Indians and Funai
workers fleeing for their lives.
The most recent tensions "pretty tragically show the complexities of modern Amazon development," said
David Cleary, an expert on the Amazon at The Nature Conservancy, an environmental protection advocacy
group. With the indigenous populations expanding, he said, the relationships with the settlers can become
more difficult, while the role the government plays is unclear. "Suddenly, it all boils out of control and people
are dead."

--- Page 87 ---
Page 87 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Built at a cost of over $500 million in today's U.S. dollars, and stretching through malarial jungles, the
Trans-Amazon was a centerpiece of the 1964-85 military government's strategy to populate the Amazon with
Brazil's poor. "A land with no people for people with no land," was the slogan. Conventional wisdom was few
tribes lay in the road's path.
"If you rewind the film, the belief was the Indians they [the road builders] would encounter along the way
would be few and quickly assimilated," said Steve Schwartzman, a director at the Washington, D.C.-based
Environmental Defense Fund, who has worked on Amazon conflict issues since the 1980s.
That turned out to be wrong. Road construction spurred a humanitarian disaster, as Indians from dozens of
tribes living in the path of roadwork began dying of flus and other diseases. Disease was compounded by
debt slavery, prostitution and alcohol, anthropologists say. Over time, the Brazilian government
acknowledged many of the mistakes, trying to rectify some by demarcating reserves for the Indians. "There
was slavery with the opening of the Trans-Amazon, and death," said Antonio Tenharin, a tribal spokesman.
Today, Tenharim Indians have one foot in the past and another in the present. Around the village, tribal
members wear Western clothes but speak their own language. Men use canoe paddles to stir manioc
roasting in vats over great wood fires. There is no phone service, but a remote transmitter paid for by the
government connects the village to the Internet. The best way to reach Tenharim leaders is via Facebook.
But tribal members say they also need other present-day tools, like appliances, fuel and higher education. "I
would prefer to live in isolation out here," said Humberto Tenharin, a 24-year-old tribe member who was
wearing a black T-shirt of the heavy metal band White Snake. But because the road brought change, "the
Indian can't live exactly how things were before," he said. "We need to go to the city to buy food, to buy
clothes, to study."
Over the years, many Amazon Indians have earned college degrees and work in the government sector. But
needing cash, some Indians went into league with illegal loggers and gold miners seeking to operate on their
lands, authorities say. In 2006, the Tenharim Indians decided to begin collecting a toll -- $30 for trucks and
$10 for cars -- on the Trans-Amazon where it crosses their land. The toll was illegal, which the tribe didn't
dispute but said it was justified as compensation for the ills brought by the road. Federal officials let it be.
In Humaita, locals viewed the toll as extortion. Paying it reinforced their view that government institutions like
law enforcement ignore the Amazon. According to people who drove through it, the scene at the remote toll --
a pole across the road well out of cellphone range in the jungle -- could be tense, especially at night, with
young Tenharim Indians milling about, some drinking alcohol, and at least a few carrying guns.
Now, the toll is a crime scene. Police say five tribal members shot and buried three men who arrived from
Humaita on the morning of Dec. 16. Caio Paiva, the public defender representing the accused Tenharim men,
says the federal police case against his clients is weak. Mr. Paiva plans to challenge the police investigators'
reliance on anonymous informants, rather than hard evidence, for example. "None of their evidence proves
that these men killed anyone," Mr. Paiva said.
Located at the junction of the Trans-Amazon and the Madeira River, some 1,700 miles from Rio de Janeiro,
Humaita is a bustling hub for the region's loggers, ranchers, gold prospectors, fishermen and other settlers.
As tensions with the Indians grew, locals would grumble that Tenharim chiefs were buying expensive pickups,
while the Tenharim said they felt the sting of racism in Humaita, where some referred to their language as
simply "Blablablalala."
A peaceful veneer began to crack on Dec. 2, when Tenharim chief Ivan Tenharin, 45, allegedly fell off his
motorcycle and died while driving home from a hardscrabble logging outpost called "180" on the eastern edge
of the reserve. Police -- and the Tenharim Indians who found the chief -- both said he fell off his bike and hit
his head.
But as they mourned their fallen chief, some tribe members speculated that perhaps settlers who opposed
the toll had a hand in his death, according to people who heard the talk.
A few days after the chief's death, the regional director of Funai, Iva Bocchini, wrote a statement on a Funai
website calling the death suspicious. During a subsequent news conference, army investigators seeking a
motive cited the blog, saying it may have confirmed tribal suspicions about their chief's death. The
investigator's theory was that the opinion of a top Funai official carries extraordinary weight with tribe
members who have depended heavily on the agency.
In an interview published on Feb. 9 in Brazil's Carta Capital magazine, Mr. Bocchini said he never affirmed
that the chief had been murdered. Instead, he said his "text tried to give the Indians a voice," which he said
was an obligation of his work. Officials for Funai declined to make Mr. Bocchini available for further comment.

--- Page 88 ---
Page 88 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Police allege that just over a week after the Mr. Bocchini's statement, Tenharim men killed three settlers who
pulled up at the toll in a black four-door Volkswagen coupe. Though they barely knew each other, the victims
were all fathers who worked in a service economy taking root in Humaita -- a break from the typical image of
Amazon settlers as loggers or ranchers.
Stef Pinheiro, a 43-year-old teacher, was at the wheel of his first car, the black Volkswagen, purchased a day
earlier in Humaita. "It was Stef's first time driving his own car on the Trans-Amazon," said Mr. Pinheiro's
widow, Irisnea Azevedo, 36.
In the passenger seat was Luciano Freire, a 29-year-old wholesaler who had helped set up the car sale. In
the back was Aldeney Ribeiro, a manager at the regional electrical utility. Mr. Ribeiro had hitched a ride in the
Volkswagen at a ferry crossing about an hour earlier.
Picking up Mr. Ribeiro may have been a fatal move. According to a judge's ruling denying bail to the five
arrested in the case, police allege that a Tenharim spotter picked him out as a potential target for a revenge
killing while Mr. Ribeiro waited at the Trans-Amazon ferry. They then radioed the make of the car he entered
to the Tenharim village.
Federal Police and federal prosecutors declined to comment because the case is sealed. Carlos Terrinha, a
lawyer for the victims' families, says Mr. Ribeiro was targeted because he was known to the tribe and had
been threatened by the tribe before when he tried to collect an electricity bill. Tribal members say the tribe
has no connection to the murders.
Christmas Eve rolled around and the men had been missing more than a week. Humaita authorities said they
pleaded with Federal Police to search the reservation. They were told no one would be available until after
the New Year holiday, according to Mr. Terrinha. Federal Police declined to comment.
Fed up, residents of Humaita rioted on Christmas Day. The next day, a group of settlers from "180" burned
down the toll, prompting scores of police and soldiers to arrive the next day. A few days later, searchers
found parts of the Volkswagen on the reserve. After another month in the muck with a cadaver-sniffing dog,
they found the bodies.
Now, the task is to ease tension between Indians who fear going to town, and settlers wary of driving the
Trans-Amazon. Called in by President Rousseff, the army shut down the toll after the bodies were found. Led
by Col. Marcio de Goyes Alves, who runs the regional Army base, a group of soldiers guarded some 45
Tenharim Indians as they bought supplies and did errands in Humaita. The soldiers carried assault rifles, a
message to townspeople that the Indians must not be harmed.
Col. Alves says he believes peace will return soon here. But Mr. Terrinha, the lawyer, isn't so sure. He fears
what may happen when the guardsmen leave. "My worry, unfortunately, is that there may be some settling of
accounts," he said.

Risk's Allure Has Gold in the Cold
Commodities Report
Risk's Allure Has Gold in the Cold
By Ira Iosebashvili
904 words
20 May 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Some investors are giving up on gold.
A rally in the price of gold at the beginning of the year has petered out, as an improving U.S. economy and
receding inflation fears send money managers in search of better returns in stocks and bonds.
Gold prices are down more than 6% since they peaked for the year in mid-March, but they still are up for
2014 thanks to that blistering start. Hedge funds and other investors cut their net bullish bets on gold by
nearly one-third in the past two months, according to Commodity Futures Trading Commission data. SPDR
Gold Shares, the biggest exchange-traded fund that buys gold, has seen its holdings decline to the lowest
level in more than five years.
Some money managers had hoped the bounce in gold prices at the start of 2014 marked the beginning of a
sustained recovery for the precious metal, which had its worst performance in three decades last year. Gold
is down 31.5% from its all-time high of nearly $1,900 set in August 2011.
The recent setback is reviving gold bulls' fears that last year's drop was no fluke. Better economic data are
encouraging investors to take more risks, and the Federal Reserve has stuck to its schedule for winding down
bond purchases, despite the economy's uneven performance over the winter. That has reduced concerns
about inflation, a major reason investors have held gold over the years.
Gold's decline after a promising beginning to 2014 shows how investors' hunger for yield is overriding any
lingering worries about the health of the global economy. In past years, recession, fears of high inflation and
Europe's debt problems supported gold, which many investors believed would hold its value during a crisis.
But the metal, which yields nothing and costs money to hold, looks less attractive now that money managers'
concerns about turbulence in financial markets have diminished.
"Gold is the quintessential super reserve currency," said Bart Melek, head of commodity strategy at TD
Securities. "When times were bad, people didn't mind paying a premium to own it. But now that the existential
risks have passed, that desire is no longer there."
Gold for May delivery ended Monday's session nearly unchanged at $1,293.70 per troy ounce, up 7.6% for
the year.
Investors piled into gold after the financial crisis, believing the metal would hold its value even as stocks and
bonds were falling. Gold kept rising over the next few years, as central banks implemented unprecedented
stimulus that weakened currencies and ramped up inflation fears.
More than five years after the crisis, inflation remains tame in the U.S., while deflation is a possibility in the
euro zone. The Fed is moving to end its easy-money policies, reducing gold's appeal as an inflation hedge.
"Right now, gold is not a productive asset. It is basically an insurance policy," said Peter Sorrentino, who
helps manage $14.8 billion at Huntington Asset Advisors in Cincinnati. "The fundamentals that drove the price
down last year are still intact."
Mr. Sorrentino's fund has cut its gold allocation to 8%, from a high of 15% in 2011. Prices would have to dip
as low as $1,220 an ounce for the fund to consider adding to its position, he said.
On Thursday, central banks in the euro zone, Switzerland and Sweden removed a gold-sales ceiling that had
been in place since 2009. The European Central Bank said they had no plans to sell "significant" amounts of
the metal.

--- Page 90 ---
Page 90 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Last month, the World Gold Council warned that appetite for the precious metal in China, the world's biggest
buyer, is waning after investors' decadelong buying spree. An association of Hong Kong jewelers said last
week that demand for gold in China has fallen 30% from last year.
"Unless you're interested in gold coins or jewelry, there really is not much reason to buy gold right now," said
Ted Harper, who helps manage around $10 billion at Frost Investment Advisors LLC in Houston. "There is
still room on the downside before prices turn higher." His fund has cut its holdings of precious-metal miners to
below 4%, from a high of 6.2% in 2011.
To be sure, gold will always have its fans. Some investors are betting that years of central-bank stimulus in
the form of bond buying has cheapened currencies. Others simply believe the precious metal is attractive
after last year's drop, and due for a bounce. Gold prices did pop in mid-March, around when Russia annexed
Crimea.
"Our view on gold right now, is that it's really cheap," said Nicholas Johnson, who co-manages a portfolio at
Pacific Investment Management Co.'s $14.3 billion Commodity Real Return Strategy Fund. The fund has
purchased gold futures in recent weeks, Mr. Johnson said.
Still, the gloom in gold has been hard for many market participants to ignore.
"The character of the market has changed," said Frank Lesh, a broker at Futurepath Trading, a firm that
executes trades for retail and institutional investors. "We used to have people calling in all the time, adding to
their gold positions. Now they trade for a few dollars and get out. The hot money is gone."

Thursday's Markets
Investors Abandon Riskier Assets --- Blue-Chip Index Sinks 167.16 Points and Bond Prices Rise in
Wake of Discouraging Economic Data
By Alexandra Scaggs, Chris Dieterich and Katy Burne
968 words
16 May 2014
The Wall Street Journal

C1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Investors stepped up their retreat from riskier assets, with U.S. stocks slumping to their biggest decline in five
weeks and bond prices roaring higher for the second day in a row.
The action underscores stocks' continuing struggle to gain ground in 2014 following a rally last year, amid
tepid signs from the U.S. and global economies. Thursday's market moves followed the release of
disappointing data in Europe, where first-quarter economic output rose just half as much as analysts
expected, and a soft profit report from retailer Wal-Mart Stores Inc.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average slid 167.16 points, or 1%, to 16446.81, its biggest point and percentage
drop since April 10. All but two of the 30 component stocks declined, with Wal-Mart falling $1.91, or 2.4%, to
$76.83.
Small-cap shares that have been hit hard in recent weeks were hammered again in early trading before
staging a recovery. The Russell 2000 index dropped 7.15 points, or 0.6%, to 1095.99, its lowest close since
Feb. 5. The decline put the index 9.3% below its record close on March 4, nearing a correction that is typically
defined as a 10% decline from a recent high. Early Thursday, the index was down as much as 1.9%, its
second intraday foray into correction territory in two weeks, amid the latest uneven data.
The National Association of Home Builders said Thursday that an index of builders' confidence in the market
for new single-family homes fell to its lowest level in a year, and the Federal Reserve said industrial
production fell more than expected in April from the prior month.
"Some of this economic data isn't decisive evidence the U.S. economy is accelerating, which reflects more on
the small caps," said Mark Luschini, chief investment strategist for Janney Montgomery Scott, which
manages about $2 billion in its asset-management arm.
The selloff continued in Asia early Friday, especially in Japan, where the Nikkei was down 1.5%. Elsewhere,
Australia's S&P/ASX 200 was down 0.4% and South Korea's Kospi was down 0.5%.
On Thursday, the yield on the 10-year U.S. Treasury note dropped 0.042 percentage point to 2.502% after
earlier falling as low as 2.472%, its lowest level since Oct. 23. Bond yields fall when prices rise.
Approaching the halfway point of 2014, investors have become more worried about the outlook for global
growth, which was widely expected to heat up this year.
Rather than fleeing stocks altogether, investors have sold off shares of small companies trading at lofty
valuations, particularly in the technology and biotech industries. They have bought more companies that
produce returns regardless of the economic conditions, known as defensive shares, such as dividend-paying
utility stocks. The S&P 500 utility sector is up 10% this year, making it by far the biggest gainer in the index.
The Dow, despite recent retreats, remains 1.6% below its record hit on Tuesday.
"We're worried about risk, and the riskier parts of the market," said Christian Ledoux, director of equity
research at South Texas Money Management, which manages $2.5 billion. That "includes small caps . . . so
we're tilting our portfolios more defensively."
Stocks slid on both sides of the Atlantic, with the Stoxx Europe 600 dropping 0.9%.
In the U.S., declines were broadly distributed. Nine of the S&P 500's 10 sectors slid, with materials and
energy stocks leading the pullback.

--- Page 92 ---
Page 92 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The S&P 500 fell 17.68 points, or 0.9%, to 1870.85, and the Nasdaq Composite Index shed 31.33 points, or
0.8%, to 4069.29.
The pullback from riskier assets hasn't swept up junk bonds issued by low-rated companies. That is because
there is still so much money chasing income-producing securities amid still-low interest rates and because
the U.S. economy continues to expand, reducing fears that defaults will tick up.
"People have an insatiable desire for income, and credit quality is still decent," said Gershon Distenfeld,
director in high yield at AllianceBernstein.
The volume of junk bonds being sold in the U.S. is down 12% from year-earlier levels, supporting the prices
of existing bonds, said data provider Dealogic.
Junk bonds from speculative-grade borrowers First Data Corp. and Clear Channel Communications Inc. were
about two cents lower in price on Thursday, and a derivatives index tracking U.S. high-yield bonds fell to
106.75 cents on the dollar from 107.2 cents Wednesday, according to Markit data.
Most indicators of market fear remain quiet. Gold prices, which typically rise during times of market stress,
dropped 0.9% to $1,293.50 a troy ounce. The CBOE Volatility Index, an options-based measure of expected
stock-market swings, rose 8.2% Thursday but remains low by historical standards, at 13.17. The long-term
average is about 20.
Mr. Ledoux, of South Texas Money Management, expects large-stock benchmarks to end the year with
gains, because he said U.S. companies will continue to increase profits. But he is worried that global
economic growth won't pick up as much as many investors had expected.
Small-cap shares are still trading at lofty levels compared with larger peers.
The Russell 2000 is trading at 18.3 times its expected earnings for the next year, according to Russell
Investments. The S&P 500 is trading at 15.2 times its forecast earnings for the next year, according to
FactSet.

Palladium, Platinum Rise on Mine Unrest
By Ira Iosebashvili and Laura Clarke
479 words
15 May 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Palladium prices climbed to a nearly three-year high and platinum surged Wednesday as escalating violence
amid strikes at major mines in South Africa highlighted risks to supply.
Palladium for June delivery, the most actively traded contract, rose $11.50, or 1.4%, to $828.80 a troy ounce
on the Comex division of the New York Mercantile Exchange, its highest closing price since Aug. 1, 2011.
Platinum for July delivery gained 2% to close at $1,485.70 an ounce, the highest close since March 6. It was
platinum's largest one-day percentage gain since Jan. 2.
South Africa's Lonmin PLC, the world's third-largest platinum mining company, said several hundred striking
mine workers were blocking colleagues from returning to work as they press on with a more than
four-month-old strike. Earlier this week, several miners were hacked to death with machetes after they
crossed picket lines.
About 80,000 workers are on strike in South Africa, which accounts for about 80% of the world's platinum
supply and roughly a third of the world's palladium, because the two metals are often found and mined
together. The metals are commonly used to clean emissions from automobile engines.
Mining companies had kept platinum and palladium ore in reserve ahead of the strikes, but analysts say
those stockpiles are running low. That is pushing prices higher, said Peter Hug, head of trading at Kitco
Metals.
"The miners have now not received a paycheck in four months and the mines have lost billions of revenue,
and capitulation is still not in sight," Mr. Hug wrote in a note to investors.
Palladium prices have also been squeezed by fears of sanctions against Russia, the world's largest producer
of the metal, as the conflict in Ukraine continues. Pro-Russian separatists in eastern Ukraine voted to secede
from the rest of the country in a referendum on Sunday, and the European Union responded by modestly
tightening its sanctions against Russia. So far, none of the actions has directly targeted Russia's palladium
industry.
Analysts at Citigroup estimate global palladium supplies are sufficient to meet demand for another 15 weeks.
About half those supplies, however, are held by Russia, which carefully controls the sales of its stockpiles,
Citigroup analysts said.
Gold for June delivery rose 0.9% to close at $1,305.90 an ounce, after low inflation numbers throughout the
euro zone reinforced the notion that the European Central Bank would need to ease monetary policy to
quicken economic activity in the region. Some investors buy gold in times of monetary easing, believing it will
hold its value better than paper currency.
Silver prices rose, with the July contract up 1.2% to close at $19.775 an ounce, the highest level since April
15.

Slow Growth Leaves Peso Bulls Stranded --- Sluggish Economic Conditions in U.S., Mexico Have Damped Investor Hopes That the Currency Was...
Currency Trading
Slow Growth Leaves Peso Bulls Stranded --- Sluggish Economic Conditions in U.S., Mexico Have
Damped Investor Hopes That the Currency Was Poised for a Rebound
By Nicole Hong
743 words
8 May 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
One of the most popular bets in the foreign-exchange market is proving to be a huge letdown.
Investors' hopes for a stronger Mexican peso haven't been fulfilled, as sluggish economic conditions on both
sides of the U.S.-Mexico border weigh on the currency.
Money managers piled into the peso after it sold off in the middle of last year, betting that a recovering
economy in the U.S. and policy measures in Mexico would lead to a rebound in the currency's value. Around
the start of this year, banks including Deutsche Bank AG said buying the peso was one of their top trades for
2014.
But the U.S. economy expanded just 0.1% in the first three months of 2014, while Mexico has struggled with
sluggish construction and weak domestic demand. Many investors also became frustrated by the slow pace
of change in Mexico's energy sector, where new regulations allowing foreign investment are widely viewed as
key to unlocking faster economic growth over the long term.
The peso is up 0.6% against the dollar this year, trailing MSCI Inc.'s emerging-market currency index, which
is up 1.5%. On Tuesday, the peso was trading at 12.96 per dollar. Mexican stocks also are down 2.2% this
year, worse than the 0.1% gain in the MSCI Emerging Markets Index. Deutsche Bank remains bullish on the
peso, but sees limited gains in the next few months, said Drausio Giacomelli, its head of emerging-markets
research.
Mexico's currency is one of several wagers, including bearish bets on gold and Treasurys, that have
disappointed investors this year. With the peso no longer the potential moneymaker it appeared to be at the
start of the year, some investors are chasing higher yields in less-stable economies such as South Africa and
Turkey.
"The Mexican peso just hasn't lived up to expectations," said Ward Brown, a Boston-based portfolio manager
at MFS Investment Management, which oversees $420.6 billion. Mr. Brown reduced his positions in the
Mexican peso and Mexico's sovereign bonds earlier this year.
The Mexican economy expanded only 1.1% in 2013, the lowest since 2009, and grew just 1.4% in the first
two months of this year.
The weak economy has prompted the Bank of Mexico to keep interest rates at a record low 3.5% to
encourage borrowing. That puts pressure on the peso because investors can find higher returns elsewhere in
the emerging world.
Brazil, for instance, has raised its benchmark interest rate nine times in the past year to 10.75%. Money has
poured into Brazilian debt and the real this year, even though many analysts and investors say Mexico's
economy is in better shape.
"The macro story in Brazil is infinitely worse than in Mexico, but capital is flowing to Brazil because the yields
are much higher there," said Alberto Ramos, chief Latin America economist for Goldman Sachs Group Inc.
But the peso still takes a hit when investors sour on developing economies. The currency is one of the world's
most heavily traded, making it the easiest emerging-market currency to sell during a global market rout.
"When we go through a bout of risk aversion . . . the peso gets whacked pretty quickly," said Paresh
Upadhyaya, director of currencies at Boston's Pioneer Investments, which manages $238 billion. "The peso
doesn't get any traction from the stronger, long-term fundamentals that underpin the currency." Mr.
Upadhyaya has maintained bullish bets on the peso over the past year, calling the bets "frustrating."

--- Page 95 ---
Page 95 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Many peso holders say they aren't ready to give in. They say constitutional amendments allowing foreign
companies to invest in Mexico's energy sector will boost demand for pesos for years to come. The
amendments passed in December, but Congress hasn't yet approved laws to implement changes.
Long term, the peso is still better positioned than most emerging-market currencies, especially if the Federal
Reserve begins raising rates next year, investors say.
"We're still confident the stronger peso will eventually materialize, just at a slower pace," said Tom Nakamura,
a portfolio manager at AGF Management Ltd. in Toronto, which oversees $36 billion. Mr. Nakamura added to
his bullish bets on the peso in April.

Gold prices fell after Federal Reserve Chairwoman Janet Yellen presented an upbeat assessment of the U.S.
economy in her testimony to Congress.

Gold prices have been under pressure in recent weeks, as signs of an economic rebound in the U.S.

paving the way for interest-rate increases next year. Gold is a zero-yielding asset that struggles to compete

to Congress's Joint Economic Committee, pinned recent economic weakness on the harsh winter. The Fed
chief also said that indicators point to a rebound in spending and production, "putting the overall economy on

"Anything with a hawkish tone to it is going to be hard on the gold market," said Sterling Smith, a futures

for the country's general elections.

Banks' Profit Engine Still Sputters
Banks' Profit Engine Still Sputters
By Justin Baer and Saabira Chaudhuri
877 words
6 May 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Just a month into the second quarter, Wall Street is bracing for another disappointing batch of trading results.
J.P. Morgan Chase & Co.'s disclosure in a regulatory filing late Friday that revenue from its markets
businesses would drop about 20% in the second quarter from a year ago caught investors, and Wall Street
executives, off guard. Shares of J.P. Morgan dropped 2.5% on Monday, while Goldman Sachs Group Inc.,
Citigroup Inc., Morgan Stanley and Bank of America Corp. all registered declines of between 1.1% and 2%.
Friday's forecast highlighted a trend that has been playing out for the past several quarters.
Fewer shares, swaps and bonds are changing hands on many markets. And many of those markets have
gotten less volatile, making it harder for bank traders to profit from price swings.
J.P. Morgan's early warning marked the latest sign that Wall Street's trading businesses, a major source of
profits for big banks, is still muddling through a difficult slump. The decline has been more pronounced on the
trading desks tied to bonds, interest-rate swaps, foreign currencies, commodities and other debt securities.
"We're in a low-volume, low-volatility environment, which is not good for markets," said one senior trading
executive at a large bank. J.P. Morgan's disclosure "is a litmus test. They're in everything."
While bank executives acknowledged their traders face the same challenges J.P. Morgan is confronting,
many said they thought early May was too soon to draw conclusions on full-quarter results of businesses
notorious for their unpredictability. What's more, J.P. Morgan posted strong results in the second quarter of
2013, making for a tough comparison, analysts said.
Still, some top executives have offered a glimpse on what April was like for traders. Ruth Porat, Morgan
Stanley's finance chief, said last week during a television interview that "the volumes we talked about during
the first quarter, with lower activity, persist."
Trading results are typically among the most volatile slices of big banks' quarterly results and among the
biggest profit drivers, and any insight into how these businesses are doing can move markets and lead
analysts to adjust their earnings expectations.
The scrutiny has intensified as investors have become more frustrated with a prolonged slump in what has
been one of Wall Street's biggest profit engines, trading in fixed income, currencies and commodities, known
as FICC. Industrywide trading revenue has fallen in 13 of the past 17 quarters, much of that driven by
declines in FICC businesses. Another batch of dour results will only fuel pessimism that the slump is here to
stay.
In April, calmer markets in foreign exchange, interest rates, oil and gold limited bank traders' opportunity to
profit from swings in prices, according to ISI analyst Glenn Schorr. In many markets, volume was down from
a year ago as well. Many hedge-fund managers struggled, Nomura Securities analyst Steven Chubak said,
limiting their appetite to take more trading risk.
A number of analysts on Monday cut their earnings estimates for J.P. Morgan by as much as 13 cents a
share for the second quarter. Evercore analyst Andrew Marquardt slashed his per-share earnings estimate to
$1.29 from $1.42, for instance. Analysts said forecasts for the bank's trading results had been too high, given
the difficult conditions, and suggested J.P. Morgan's disclosure might have been a bid to set lower
expectations. J.P. Morgan declined to comment.
So far, analysts have largely maintained their earnings estimates for other big banks that have large trading
businesses. Oppenheimer analyst Chris Kotowski said "it is probably a little premature" to cut estimates for all
the banks with big capital-markets arms without more information.

--- Page 98 ---
Page 98 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
While analysts expect FICC trading revenue largely to be down at the big banks, the opacity of most banks'
disclosures on trading makes it hard for them to forecast how the quarter is likely to turn out, they said.
For J.P. Morgan, the bad news seems to be getting worse. The 20% decline in markets revenue that the bank
expects for the second quarter is sharper than the 17% fall it posted in the first quarter. That drop, in turn,
was worse than the 15% decline Chief Executive James Dimon in late February had said the firm was seeing
so far in that quarter.
While J.P. Morgan's prediction suggests other banks will suffer as well, said Sandler O'Neill analyst Jeff
Harte, "the magnitude of a 20% decline strikes me as a little J.P. Morgan specific."
For one thing, J.P. Morgan had abnormally strong markets revenue in the second quarter of last year, said
KBW analyst Chris Mutascio. "We did not expect such strength to recur," he said. By contrast, Bank of
America's year-earlier results were "much weaker," he said, making the comparison with last year easier.
Citigroup's Keith Horowitz said he thought J.P. Morgan and other banks would have an easier time this
quarter nearing year-earlier numbers. While April and May 2013 were strong for many trading businesses,
June wasn't a good month, he said.

Ukraine Needs a Lifeline -- Now
Ukraine Needs a Lifeline -- Now
By Marco Rubio
772 words
6 May 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A13
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Ukraine's future as a sovereign nation is under threat as Russia continues to behave aggressively in the
region. President Obama and European leaders are considering imposing broader economic sanctions to
punish Vladimir Putin for unlawfully annexing Crimea. They should do it quickly.
This would make it clear to the Kremlin, which is deeply involved in managing Russia's economy and military,
that belligerent behavior has consequences. But in addition to hurting Russia with sanctions, the U.S. should
strengthen Ukraine economically. This will help the country withstand Mr. Putin's attempted takeover.
One way to increase the chances of a prosperous future in Ukraine is to strengthen the nation's currency, the
hryvnia -- for a stable monetary foundation is necessary for economic growth. Even as the value of the
Russian ruble has fallen almost 9% against the dollar in 2014, Mr. Putin takes comfort in knowing that the
hryvnia is doing worse: It has plummeted 35% against the dollar since January. The currency's weakness is
part of his plan to bring Ukraine to its knees.
This is where America and our European allies can throw a wrench into Mr. Putin's designs, rather than
standing idly by as the hryvnia collapses under physical and psychological intimidation from Russia. We
should encourage the establishment of a Ukrainian currency board, an institutional arrangement that anchors
the value of national money to a more stable currency. Under a currency board, the hryvnia would be
convertible into the dollar or the euro at a fixed rate, and backed by Ukraine's own hard currency reserves.
The International Monetary Fund would supplement the reserves with a special-purpose loan arrangement.
A currency board would help Ukraine's money become as reliable and stable as the world's dominant reserve
currencies. The effects would ripple throughout the economy: Foreign investors could have confidence that
the hryvnia is not in a death spiral, and Ukrainians would know that Mr. Putin cannot annihilate the value of
their personal savings. Such stability would encourage the nation under siege to maintain its faith in free
people and free markets.
Equally important: Moscow would immediately face the dismaying reality that Ukraine's money is suddenly far
more dependable than its own. Russia is already on a spending blowout to save the ruble as economic
conditions deteriorate: Russia's central bank has spent more than $23 billion intervening in foreign exchange
markets since January. On April 25, the bank raised its key interest rate by 50 basis points to 7.5%, a
desperate attempt to tamp down the inflationary effects of a weakening ruble. Monetary policy is not
Russia's forte in global affairs, and so the U.S. and Europe should use their advantage strategically to hurt a
vulnerable adversary.
In an April 10 letter to Treasury Secretary Jack Lew, Sen. Ted Cruz (R., Texas) and I suggested that the
Treasury Department work with the International Monetary Fund to create a currency board to help Ukraine
transition to more solid economic footing. The IMF has a long track record of supporting currency boards and
currency board-like systems. Johns Hopkins economist Steve Hanke, for instance, has designed such
systems for Estonia, Lithuania, Bosnia-Herzegovina and Bulgaria. Estonia has enjoyed a remarkable political
and economic transformation thanks in large part to a currency board introduced in 1992, which helped the
country transition to the euro zone.
Strengthening the national currency and bringing a disciplined monetary regime would send a powerful signal
of support to Kiev as the new government attempts to bring economic reform and democratic institutions to
Ukraine. Most important, establishing a currency board would avoid wasting IMF and other international
assistance, which includes U.S. taxpayer dollars, on an unstable economy.
At a time when it is vital to bolster trust in democracy and free markets, Ukrainian leaders and the IMF should
recognize the value of a stable monetary base and financial system. The IMF should provide the technical
experience necessary to ensure that the system, which would be run by Ukrainian monetary authorities, is

--- Page 100 ---
Page 100 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
implemented properly. The IMF, after all, was established for the precise purpose of managing a fixed-rate
exchange system anchored by a gold-linked dollar.
America must continue to urge innovative measures to demonstrate that we stand with the Ukrainian people,
who have already shown courage and conviction in confronting Mr. Putin's menacing threats. And so we
should extend to them the safe-haven benefits of having a trustworthy sovereign currency.
---
Mr. Rubio, a Republican from Florida, is a member of the Senate Foreign Relations Committee.

Assault on the Chilean Miracle
Americas
Assault on the Chilean Miracle
By Mary Anastasia O'Grady
885 words
5 May 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A17
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Former Chilean Finance Minister Eduardo Aninat has a good question for the architects of the corporate tax
increases now moving through the Chilean Congress: "What weed are they smoking?"
Mr. Aninat is no supply-side zealot. His resume includes five years in the left-of-center government of
Christian Democrat President Eduardo Frei (1994-2000) and later a stint as deputy managing director of the
International Monetary Fund. But he is concerned that the plan from the government of newly inaugurated
President Michelle Bachelet to sharply increase taxes on capital will damage Chilean growth and
development.
He says Ms. Bachelet should instruct her team to hold "an in-depth discussion" about the matter. But her
government and its New Majority coalition, controlling both houses of Congress, say no way: They won the
election. They dictate.
Three decades of fast growth -- led by liberal economic policies -- have made Chile the most prosperous
country in Latin America. Its annual per capita income of more than $19,000 is up from $5,000 in 1990. The
percentage of Chileans living in poverty stands at 14.4%, down from 45% in 1985.
The country also stands out politically in the region for its adherence to a rule of law that protects minority
rights and eschews banana-republic populism.
Now Ms. Bachelet and her minions in Congress are signaling a game change that suggests a return to the
political polarization of the early 1970s. It is hard to avoid the conclusion that they see their legislative
majorities as their chance to finally ram the utopian dream of the late President Salvador Allende down the
collective Chilean throat.
Sen. Jaime Quintana, spokesman for the New Majority coalition in the upper chamber, said as much in
March, when the opposition complained that the ruling coalition was using a "steamroller" in Congress. Not "a
steamroller," Mr. Quintana said. "We are going to use a high-powered steam shovel because it is necessary
to destroy the antiquated foundations of the neo-liberal model of the dictatorship."
Ms. Bachelet won the presidential runoff election with more than 60% of the vote and her hard-left allies
believe she has a mandate to dismantle the market economy. But even with a 67-49 seat majority in the
lower house and a 19-16 majority in the Senate, it won't be so easy. She is short the absolute majorities in
Congress she would need to rewrite the constitution and put the state at the center of the economy.
But she can still hit the market. The tax measure she has sent to Congress raises the corporate rate to 35%
from 20%. Perhaps even more damaging, it eliminates a key provision in the tax code that allows companies
to delay paying taxes on earnings if those earnings are reinvested rather than paid out. Known by its Spanish
initials as the "FUT," this provision has provided much of the capital that fueled Chile's rapid growth in the last
three decades.
An animated government television spot that Barack Obama might admire began running last week. It's
"unjust that the biggest firms and the richest Chileans don't pay their fair share," the narrator says while a
man dressed in a suit and tie with pesos spilling out of his briefcase stands next to a scale loaded with gold
bars and tipped in his favor.
Finance ministers of two previous left-of-center governments have expressed doubts about the plan. Mr.
Aninat questioned the wisdom of eliminating the FUT at a time "when Latin America is slowing down and
commodities are going out of fashion." The proposal is fueling "uncertainty," he said, in already uncertain
times. He challenged the government to show how it came to the conclusion that investment would be
unaltered. "In the academy and at the central bank they say that it [would be] affected," he declared.

--- Page 102 ---
Page 102 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Andres Velasco, minister of finance during Ms. Bachelet's first Socialist government (2006-10), said last week
that the FUT is a crucial source of capital for small businesses. In a presentation to Chilean entrepreneurs
last week, Juan Andres Fontaine, minister of the economy during the center-right presidency of Sebastian
Pinera, connected the dots between the decline in income inequality and unemployment in Chile since 1990
and pro-growth tax policies. He also noted that while large businesses have nearly closed the productivity
gap with their U.S. counterparts, "medium and semi-large" firms still run far behind. "They are the ones that
will lose the most with the abolition of the FUT," he warned.
The reaction in the Bachelet camp has been predictably vicious. Last week Finance Minister Alberto Arenas
threatened to consider retroactively eliminating the FUT if opponents in Congress continued to insist on
debate. Bachelet Senate ally Guido Girardi warned that if the president's campaign promises of more
populism are not met, the country should expect unrest in the streets. It is evident that for this crowd not
much has changed since the early 1970s. They preach tolerance when they're out of power and practice
envy and intransigence when given a chance to govern.

Global Finance: With No Buyer in Sight, Lender to Exit Gold-Fix Panel
Global Finance: With No Buyer in Sight, Lender to Exit Gold-Fix Panel
By Francesca Freeman
443 words
30 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C3
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Deutsche Bank AG has failed to sell its seat on the London gold-fixing panel following a three-month search
for potential buyers, raising fresh concerns about the credibility of the historic benchmark.
The German lender is one of five banks that set the twice-daily gold "fix" -- a snapshot of the market price --
in a process that has operated more or less unchanged since 1919. The bank had been looking for someone
to buy its seat after announcing in January that it would exit the fixing panel as part of a wider effort to scale
back its commodities business.
On Tuesday, the bank said it has resigned its seat, as well as its place on the three-bank silver-fixing panel.
The bank was unable to find a buyer for either position, according to a person familiar with the matter, and will
cease taking part in the process as of May 13.
The London fix plays a key role in the $20 trillion-a-day gold market and is used to price central bank assets
and the value of exchange-traded funds tied to gold, for example. But the fix has recently come under
scrutiny from regulators in the U.S., U.K. and Germany who are looking at it as part of a wider probe into a
range of financial benchmarks. Deutsche Bank also recently hired an outside consulting firm to examine its
role in the fix, according to a person familiar with the matter. The German lender and the other gold-fixing
panel members -- Barclays PLC, HSBC Holdings PLC, Bank of Nova Scotia and Societe Generale SA -- also
recently formed a steering committee to review the process, according to people familiar with the matter.
Representatives for HSBC, Barclays and Societe Generale declined to comment. A spokesman for Bank of
Nova Scotia didn't respond to a request for comment.
Some observers said a drop in the number of members on the gold-fixing panel could further damage its
credibility.
"I guess if you want to get a snapshot of what large institutional players are thinking -- which is what the fix is
really about -- then the more people you have [on the panel] that are dealing with large institutional players
the better," said Brian Lucey, a finance professor at Trinity College Dublin who has studied the gold market.
"More is better in terms of taking a temperature of the market."
Mehdi Barkhordar, a managing director at precious-metals processing and trading company MKS SA, said it
was "a tragedy" that Deutsche Bank had pulled out.

buy back shares. The bank said it discovered an error in the way it calculates its capital levels. The bank
slumped 1, or 6.3%, to 14.95.

Crude oil gained 0.2% to settle at $100.84 a barrel. Gold slipped 0.1% to $1,298.90 a troy ounce. The dollar
gained ground against the yen but slipped against the euro.
European markets advanced, with the Stoxx Europe 600 gaining 0.2%, boosted by merger news.

CFO Journal: Lose Your Triple-A Rating? Who Cares?
CFO Journal: Lose Your Triple-A Rating? Who Cares?
By Vipal Monga and Mike Cherney
1,092 words
29 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Once coveted and brandished by more than 60 companies and subsidiaries in 1980, the gold-plated triple-A
credit rating has almost vanished from corporate America.
Only three companies -- Microsoft Corp., Johnson & Johnson and Exxon Mobil Corp. -- hold that distinction
today, the lowest on record.
Who cares?
Not Jan Siegmund, chief financial officer of Automatic Data Processing Inc. The payroll processor lost the top
credit rating on April 10 because it announced plans to spin off its dealer services business, which is focused
on marketing for auto dealers and manufacturers.
ADP's shareholders don't appear to care either. Since then, the company's stock is up 0.6%. ADP has no
outstanding bonds.
"There was no fallout," Mr. Siegmund said. "We feel that double-A is a perfectly fine rating."
The decline in triple-A ratings reflects a shift in attitude among corporations and investors about the value,
cost and risk of bonds. Historically low interest rates and economic growth have scattered investors in
search of higher yields and dulled their fear of default. That has led to a steady, downward drift in credit
ratings. In fact, 81% of newly rated companies in 2013 got a rating in the single-B category from Standard &
Poor's Ratings Services, which investors call "junk."
Against that backdrop, investors see a negligible difference between the top two rungs of the ratings ladder,
and trade them as if they had almost equal risk. So now more analysts and investors say shareholders of
companies with the triple-A rating are actually paying for a privilege that offers little reward.
"It's a very good question that any responsible CEO and CFO should consider: is having a triple-A rating
worth it?" said Mark Puccia, a managing director at S&P, one of the rating services that downgraded ADP.
Credit ratings are the corporate version of an individual's FICO score. S&P, Moody's Investors Service and
Fitch Ratings grade corporations on their ability to repay their debts. Unlike consumer credit scorers,
however, the firms charge companies for the ratings.
The highest-rated companies have some distinct qualities, such as little debt, high cash flow and profit
margins, strong brands and leadership positions in their industries, said Daniel Gates, a managing director at
Moody's.
In theory, that should make borrowing much cheaper for the triple-A companies. But the market isn't treating
the companies that way.
Microsoft, for example, sold $450 million of five-year bonds last April with a yield that was 0.32 percentage
point over Treasury notes. Less than a week later, double-A-plus rated Apple Inc. sold $4 billion of five-year
bonds with only a slightly higher yield premium of 0.40 percentage point. Both were among the lowest
premiums ever paid by corporate borrowers.
As of April 22, bonds issued by double-A rated companies were trading with yield premiums only 0.05
percentage point higher than the highest-rated bonds, according to Barclays.
To be sure, the increase in overall risk could become a problem in another financial meltdown. Investors, in
particular, found they liked holding triple-A long-term bonds in the wake of Lehman Brothers' collapse. At the
peak of the recent financial crisis, in April 2009, the yield spread between the top-tier bonds widened to 1.93
percentage points, the largest since 1998.

--- Page 107 ---
Page 107 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Jesse Fogarty, a portfolio manager at Cutwater Asset Management, said triple-A corporate debt is among the
few things that can be sold during times of financial stress, making it useful if an investor needs to raise cash
quickly.
"If you did need to raise liquidity, a triple-A Johnson & Johnson would be one of the best things you'd have to
be able to do that," said Mr. Fogarty, whose firm manages $25 billion, mainly for pension funds and insurance
companies. "Many times in periods of stress, you don't sell what you want, you sell what you can. These
assets are kind of what you turn to."
A Johnson & Johnson spokesman said the company likes the highest credit rating, because it gives the
company "greater flexibility in managing our business, virtually unlimited access to the capital markets and
the most favorable interest rates to finance our business."
The pharmaceutical company sold $900 million of 10-year bonds in June 2008, as financial markets teetered,
and was able to get them priced at a premium of 1.03 percentage points to comparable Treasurys. By
contrast, PepsiCo Inc., which was rated Aa2 by Moody's, sold $1.75 billion of bonds only a month earlier with
a premium of 1.25 percentage points.
Maintaining the highest rating is a potentially expensive proposition for the companies themselves, said Joe
Mayo, head of corporate credit research for Conning Inc., which manages more than $85 billion in assets,
primarily for insurance companies.
"The benefit to them in terms of lower borrowing costs really is not that significant," he said.
Many analysts argue that companies with pristine balance sheets are penalizing their shareholders by not
borrowing enough money.
The highest-rated companies could increase shareholder returns by adding a bit more debt to their balance
sheets and giving that money back to shareholders, said Martin Fridson, the chief investment officer at
investment adviser Lehmann, Livian, Fridson Advisors LLC.
Microsoft, for example, could boost its per-share earnings by 10% if it borrowed $50 billion more to expand its
buyback program, said Chris Hickey, analyst with Atlantic Equities. The software company reported per-share
earnings of $2.58 last year.
"I don't think triple-A as opposed to a double-A is meaningful to equity investors," Mr. Hickey said.
A Microsoft spokesman declined to speak about the credit rating, but said the company has borrowed almost
$25 billion in the bond market since 2009 and returned $189 billion to shareholders through dividends and
buybacks over the past decade.
Mr. Siegmund, ADP's finance chief, recalls the triple-A rating used to carry some weight. In fact, his sales
force used it as a "prestigious element" when they approached new clients.
But now that the company has dropped to a double-A rating and clients and investors haven't blinked, it is
content to let that pitch-point go.
"We're certainly not lobbying for" a return to triple-A, said Mr. Siegmund. "We're very happy and content with
our rating today."

John Kester contributed to this article.

Gold Twinkles Above $1,300 --- Investors' Concerns About Russia, Ukraine Add to Metal's Luster as a

It was the third session of gains in a row. Gold for April delivery ended $10.20, or 0.8%, higher at $1,300.70 a

The more actively traded June contract also rose $10.20, or 0.8%, to finish at $1,300.80 a troy ounce.
Ukrainian officials on Friday said they were moving ahead with an operation against pro-Russian separatists

firefight at a pro-Russian stronghold but halted their advance after Russia activated thousands of troops it had
massed along the border.
Investors were eager to buy gold before the weekend to avoid being caught flat-footed by developments in
Ukraine, said Adam Klopfenstein, senior market strategist at Archer Financial Services. "Everyone expected
Russia to step back, but they are not," Mr. Klopfenstein said. "This gives people the jitters, especially going
into the weekend."
Some investors view gold as a safe place to store their wealth during periods of economic or geopolitical
uncertainty, believing it will hold its value as other assets decline. For the past few weeks, investors had
discounted rising tensions in Ukraine and focused on an improving U.S. economy, helping gold fall 5.7%
from its March high.
Gold-market analysts said that while the situation in Ukraine could deteriorate, Europe and the U.S. weren't
likely to get involved in the conflict beyond leveling a new round of sanctions at Moscow. "On one hand, it's a
volatile situation, because we don't know what Russia will do next," said Ira Epstein, of the Linn Group. "But
gold's upside is limited, because there is little chance of the conflict spilling beyond Ukraine."
Among the obstacles faced by gold are a strong stock market and rising interest rates, as well as a rising
dollar, analysts at Barclays said in a note to investors. Investors are less likely to own gold, which doesn't pay
a dividend, if they can get good returns from other investments. Barclays expects prices for the precious
metal to average $1,250 an ounce by the end of the year.
In other markets, platinum and palladium continued to climb after mining companies in South Africa said they
hadn't reached a resolution with unions to settle the three-month strike there. South Africa is the source of
about 37% of the world's palladium and about 80% of global platinum. Threats of sanctions directed against
Russia, the source of roughly 40% of the world's palladium supply, moved prices as well, investors said.
Platinum for July delivery rose $14.70, or 1%, to settle at $1,424.30 an ounce, a one-week high. Palladium for
June delivery climbed $8.90, or 1.1%, to $811.20 an ounce, the highest settlement since April 14.
