WEEKEND INVESTOR --- Commodities: To Buy or Not to Buy? --- The prices of many raw materials are rallying; Here's what you need to know if...
WEEKEND INVESTOR --- Commodities: To Buy or Not to Buy? --- The prices of many raw materials
are rallying; Here's what you need to know if you are weighing whether to brave a risky market
By Christian Berthelsen
1,874 words
26 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B7
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Commodity prices are on the rise, as gold, corn and other basic materials climb back from steep declines --
and outpace U.S. stocks.
The rebound may stir hopes that a longer-term boom has resumed after three rough years for natural
resources, and a measured bet could pay off.
But for ordinary investors, commodities often are a raw deal. They should take a hard look before loading up.
First, weigh whether you need the exposure. Perhaps the main reason investors hold commodities is to
hedge against inflation. But inflation remains muted in the U.S. Moreover, many investors in broad-market
index funds already hold shares in companies that could make more money if crude oil, nickel or some other
material gets more expensive -- and those companies can often turn a profit even if prices stagnate.
Companies in the energy, utilities and basic-materials sectors represent about 17% of the market value of the
S&P 500, for example.
Then consider the drawbacks. Commodities can provide diversification from stocks and bonds, but they
generate no income in the form of dividends or interest payments. They also are vulnerable to prolonged
declines, such as the three-year tailspin that culminated in 2013, when a 9.5% drop in the Dow Jones-UBS
Commodity Index made commodities the worst-performing major U.S. asset class -- including stocks, bonds
and real estate -- based on total return to investors. By contrast, the S&P gained 32%, including dividends.
"To invest in commodities, you would have to take away from something else in the portfolio. You're taking
from equities, with higher returns and the same volatility, or you're taking from bonds that have the same
return and less volatility," says Mark Keating, a partner at Willow Creek Wealth Management in Sebastopol,
Calif., which oversees $700 million.
The Dow Jones-UBS index has generated a 9.9% return for investors this year through Thursday, compared
with a 2.3% return on the S&P 500, according to FactSet.
Even advisers who think commodities can be useful often recommend only small doses, perhaps 3% to 5% of
a portfolio. An investor who is particularly concerned about the risk that the U.S. dollar will lose much of its
value might hold some gold, which can act as a store of value at times, though it also can behave like a risky
asset.
In addition, investors need to carefully study the many mutual funds and exchange-traded funds that have
cropped up over the past decade or so to offer exposure to commodities, experts say. The funds often carry
steep fees and hidden risks that could eat into returns, so investors should understand how they work.
Here is what investors need to know about what drives commodity prices and how to place a smart wager.
From the start of 2000 through the end of 2010, the Dow Jones-UBS Commodity Index, which follows future
contracts for 22 commodities from aluminum to zinc, more than doubled. Copper gained 417%, and cotton
184%. The single biggest reason for the rally was that China was growing rapidly.
Still, that only addresses the demand side of the equation. Supplies of many raw materials also were
constrained in the years leading up to that decade, because tepid sales and prices had led commodity
producers to limit outlays needed to boost output.
That put commodity investors in a strong position, and many rushed to take advantage. Investors had less
than $1.8 billion in mutual funds and ETFs that focus on commodities at the end of 2003, but that amount had
swelled to more than $182 billion at the peak in August 2011, according to Chicago-based
investment-research firm Morningstar.

--- Page 110 ---
Page 110 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Now, the situation is different. China's economy is growing more slowly, and ramped-up production of many
materials is helping to keep a lid on prices. While many commodity markets are rising, some are falling.
For example, coffee has shot up 94% this year through Thursday, due to a drought in top exporter Brazil,
while a virus that has killed millions of pigs has pushed the price of lean-hog futures up 48%. But copper --
widely used in appliances, homes and elsewhere -- is down 8.2% due to concerns about Chinese growth,
while lumber has lost 9.5%.
"When China was growing at 13%, it was almost a no-brainer because China consumes 40% of global
commodities," says Shelley Goldberg, an independent commodity strategist who advises institutional clients
on investing in sustainable resources. "Today, it's not such a simple story."
Yet there are signs that at least some ordinary investors still hope to ride a hot market.
Between August 2011 and the end of last year -- a period when commodity prices were in a funk -- investors
pulled more than $23.3 billion out of commodity mutual funds and ETFs, according to Morningstar. After
pulling out more money in January, investors put $400 million back into the funds in February and March, as
commodity markets rebounded.
Investors considering following suit have an often-bewildering array of funds to choose from, most of which
have relatively short track records by which to judge performance.
The funds fall into two broad categories -- those that invest in a wide variety of raw materials across the
various commodity subsectors, such as energy, agriculture and precious metals, and those that focus more
narrowly on a single sector or even a single material, such as gold, copper or corn.
Broad-based funds are the better choice for an investor who wants general commodity exposure and lacks
expertise or conviction about what will happen to prices for a particular type of material.
The broad funds typically track an index of materials, though some follow their index more closely than
others.
For example, the iPath Dow Jones-UBS Commodity Index Total Return exchange-traded note closely tracks
the Dow Jones-UBS Commodity Index. The fund charges 0.75% in annual fees, or $75 for every $10,000
invested, according to Morningstar.
The largest commodity mutual fund by assets, the $14.1 billion Pimco CommodityRealReturn Strategy Fund,
also follows the index to a great degree, but fund managers have some leeway to tweak exposure by, for
example, increasing or decreasing holdings of a specific commodity relative to the index or buying futures
contracts that won't come due until months later, if they think prices for a certain material are more likely to
rise further down the road. The fund charges annual fees of 1.19%.
The funds also have different ways of handling a quirk of the commodities markets. When commodity
contracts mature each month, fund managers typically buy the next month's contracts to replace them.
But if prices for the new contracts are higher -- a phenomenon known as "contango" -- the additional cost can
significantly erode the fund's returns. Contango can occur when, for example, there is sufficient supply in the
near-term but either a shortage of supply or a surge in demand looms.
Plain-vanilla commodity index funds often don't try to mitigate that risk, which also can work in an investor's
favor if the new monthly contracts are less expensive, which is known as "backwardation."
Other funds, such as the PowerShares DB Commodity Index Tracking Fund ETF -- the largest broad-based
commodity ETF by assets, at $5.7 billion as of Thursday -- pick monthly contracts that will come due months
down the line in some cases, to limit the damage from contango or maximize the benefit of backwardation.
The fund charges 0.85% in annual fees.
Commodity funds can carry other risks that might catch investors off-guard. Purchasing a commodity futures
contract typically only requires that an investor or a fund put up a small percentage of the contract's face
value, so funds have to decide what to do with the remainder of their assets.
The Pimco fund, for example, has less than a quarter of the fund's capital tied up in commodity contracts, and
it tries to give returns a boost by investing most of the remainder in Treasury inflation-protected securities
and other Treasurys.
Morningstar noted last August that the fund's declines for the year to that point were largely because bond
prices fell as investors began to anticipate a reduction in the Federal Reserve's efforts to stimulate the
economy by buying bonds. The fund is up 11% year to date.

--- Page 111 ---
Page 111 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Funds that focus on a single type of commodity tend to be smaller. One exception: popular funds backed by
gold or other precious metals, which often have lower fees because the cost of actually storing the materials
is relatively low.
For investors with strong views on the future prices of particular commodities, there is an array of funds
available that track, for example, corn, natural gas or crude oil. But the funds can be risky and sometimes
carry high fees.
Experts say there are smart ways to hold hard assets, including understanding what purpose they are meant
to serve and finding low-cost ways to achieve that aim.
"When we're constructing a portfolio for high-net-worth clients, one of the objectives is to protect real
spending and quality of life," says Jeffrey Heisler, investment strategist at TwinFocus Capital Partners, a
Boston firm with about $2 billion under management.
"People are natural consumers of food and energy," he says. "Commodities hedge out and diversify and
balance that risk we all naturally hold."
The firm's clients collectively held about $3.4 million in a broad basket of commodities through the
PowerShares fund as of Thursday, and another $23.3 million in SPDR Gold Trust, an ETF backed by gold
bullion. The SPDR fund is the largest retail commodity fund of any kind, with $32.9 billion in assets, and
charges 0.40% in annual fees.
Some investors prefer a similar fund also backed by bullion, iShares Gold Trust, which charges 0.25% in
annual fees. Individual shares also cost less, because a single share represents 1/100th of an ounce of gold,
rather than 1/10th of an ounce for the SPDR fund. The iShares fund has $6.8 billion in assets.
Perhaps most important, investors need to have realistic expectations. Throughout history, commodities
markets have gone through periods of boom and bust, and investors who were fortunate enough to hold
commodities during the run-up last decade saw what a boom looks like.
But investors also risk a bet going bust. Researchers have found that spot prices for raw materials are flat
over time, once inflation is accounted for. In other words, they offer no positive return in the long run.
"An ounce of gold bought a good men's suit or a toga 2,000 years ago, and it buys a good men's suit today
as well," says William Bernstein, author of "The Investor's Manifesto" and a co-principal of portfolio manager
Efficient Frontier Advisors, based in Eastford, Conn.
"The relative value hasn't changed."

Page 112 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
REVIEW --- Books: Blotting the Balance Sheet
REVIEW --- Books: Blotting the Balance Sheet
By James Grant
1,538 words
26 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C6
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The Reckoning
By Jacob Soll
Basic, 276 pages, $28.99
It's shirtsleeves to shirtsleeves for great empires, prosperous city-states and the member companies of the
Dow Jones Industrial Average. Debt or sin or war or the tax man finally lays them low. Now comes Jacob Soll
with a new theory of the decline and fall of earthly institutions. He fingers the accountants.
According to the author of "The Reckoning," successful societies -- while they last -- are those that properly
cast their figures. They confront their liabilities as well as their assets. In their enterprise and politics,
accountability is the watchword -- until one misfortune or another sets the red ink to overflowing.
Mr. Soll rides this thesis hard. His narrative races from antiquity to the early Christian era down to the
Medicis, the 17th-century Dutch, the 18th-century English and French, the early American Republic, Charles
Dickens's Britain, the railroads of the "Robber Barons" -- all the way to WorldCom and the chronically
unbalanced U.S. federal budget.
Since accounting is not, to some people, intrinsically fascinating, Mr. Soll spices his story with big historical
personalities. We encounter St. Matthew, the most numerate of the apostles; the control-freaky Sun King,
Louis XIV of France; the crooked Sir Robert Walpole, the author of the bailout of the South Sea Co. in the
early 1720s; the 18th-century pottery-maker Josiah Wedgwood, pioneer in cost accounting; Jacques Necker,
who tried to bring order to the accounts of pre-Revolutionary France; Robert Morris, who attempted to do the
same for Revolutionary America; and Arthur Andersen, founder of the accounting firm that perished with
Enron.
Mr. Soll earns high marks for brevity -- all this storytelling he polishes off in a little over 200 pages -- as well
as for scholarship: His 20-page bibliography is longer than most of his chapters. His grand thesis is another
matter. Is good accounting a cause of the wealth of nations or is it an effect of the virtues -- individual liberty
and the rule of law, to name two -- that foster financial and political truth-telling? Does careful reckoning
implant these essential moral qualities or do honest people count straight and true whatever the state of their
knowledge about generally accepted accounting principles?
The issue is joined in 21st-century America. Accountants, auditors and financial analysts we have. Facts, too,
we possess -- by the hard-driveful. What we seem to lack is the willingness to face those facts and the
discipline not to bend them. On this last point I call into evidence the tendency of besotted investors to accept
flattering "adjusted" financial metrics in lieu of standard ones.
During the Internet bubble of the late 1990s, "page views" or "eyeballs" did service for revenue. Now "cash
earnings" are fobbed off as the functional equivalent of net earnings. (Suffice it to say that the former, which
eliminate, for instance, acquisition costs, are usually substantially higher than the latter.) Years ago, orthodox
analysts chafed over the neologism "EBITDA," which means "earnings before interest, taxes, depreciation
and amortization." Now EBITDA seems conservative in comparison with such concepts as "EBITDAR" (which
excludes rent costs, restructuring costs, or both) and "adjusted" EBITDA (which omits a host of other
expenses). Modern corporate finance is increasingly faith-based.
"To preserve the integrity of his reports," Mr. Soll quotes Arthur Andersen himself saying, "the accountant
must insist upon absolute independence of judgment and action." Let him insist. It will go for naught if those
independently rendered reports go unread.
Fifteenth-century Spain and 18th-century France went broke through ignorance as much as mismanagement.
Their rulers, Mr. Soll shows, lacked timely and well-organized data. There's no such excuse today. The

--- Page 113 ---
Page 113 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
combined budgets of the Office of Management and Budget, the Congressional Budget Office and the
Government Accountability Office top $600 million. Foundations and think tanks dot the Washington
cityscape. In New York, the continually updated digital read-out of the public debt that jolts passersby at West
44th Street and Sixth Avenue shows that we collectively owe $17,532,077,000,000 -- or we did on Friday at
10 a.m. And that's not counting the huge unfunded liabilities of the Social Security and Medicare systems.
The U.S would make the best-informed sovereign bankrupt in history.
Mr. Soll's story largely concerns the technique of double-entry bookkeeping, with its elegantly
counterbalanced assets and liabilities. It is a system that almost forces managerial attention not only on profit
and loss but also on debt, net worth and solvency. As Mr. Soll hustles through history, he stops to ask
whether a particular society employs the double-entry method or not. One could ask a deeper question: What
is the nature of the monetary values that the balance sheets and income statements express?
In Leviticus 19:36, which is quoted in "The Reckoning," the Lord commands his people to keep "just
balances, just weights." In the U.S. Constitution -- Article I, Section 8, which is not quoted -- the Founders
grant to Congress the right "to coin money, regulate the value thereof, and of foreign coin, and fix the
standard of weights and measures."
What these mighty powers, sacred and profane, seemed to be driving at is fixity. What the modern monetary
world features is flux. As a unit of measure, the dollar is undefined: At last the cliche "it is what it is" has found
its semantic purpose. A fiat currency is just what it is, a digital impulse or a piece of scrip. However many
trillions of George Washingtons the Federal Reserve deems necessary to lift the stock market or press down
interest rates, Janet Yellen can cause them to materialize with a few taps on a computer keyboard.
Perhaps those trillions will one day inflate the prices of things at the checkout counter as they have already
raised up the prices of stocks and bonds and real estate. In his discussion of the inflationary upheaval of the
early 1970s, Mr. Soll reveals a latent suspicion of the monetary printing press. He should be encouraged in
that well-founded distrust. Thus he writes of the aftermath of the 1971 presidential order by which Richard
Nixon retired the gold dollar and launched today's pure-paper edition: "With the idea of value now uncertain,
accounting had become more speculative, and account books, harder to accurately audit." So it remains.
Early and late, Mr. Soll teaches the lesson of how hard it is to see with one's eyes closed. Philip II of Spain
and Louis XIV of France were divinely seated sovereigns who were, perhaps, even less welcoming of
accountants bearing bad financial news than are today's popularly elected governors and presidents. Nor did
the institution of enlightened accounting always protect a republic against disorder and violence. Johan de
Witt, a mathematically learned member of the 17th-century Dutch ruling elite, was a stickler for rigorous
financial management. He ended his days in the hands of a mob that expressed its displeasure by cutting off
his fingers and toes and eating his internal organs.
One thinks, a little, in this context of the dispute between Standard & Poor's and Timothy Geithner. In 2011,
S&P demoted the credit of the U.S. Treasury, which Mr. Geithner then led. News of the downgrade, to
double-A-plus from triple-A, did not amuse the Obama administration, which may or may not have expressed
its pique by singling out S&P in a lawsuit for the crime of bungling the ratings of mortgage-backed securities
during the run-up to the 2008 financial crisis. The matter is before the courts, so far sans mob.
What financial crimes would provoke a mob today can hardly be imagined. Perhaps a digitally aroused crowd
could, in a retrospective way, organize itself to protest the still-shocking bailout of the cream of Wall Street a
few years back.
Mr. Soll, though a keen proponent of accountability, misses a bet when he fails to mention the convention of
"double liability." In America before the coming of the Federal Deposit Insurance Corp., the stockholders of a
bank were responsible for the solvency of the institution in which they owned a fractional interest. In the event
of impairment or insolvency, they -- not the taxpayers -- were required to stump up the funds with which to
pay the creditors.
The 21st-century reader will rub his eyes. Were most banks not limited-liability corporations? They were. And
is the liability of the stockholder of such a corporation not, well, limited? Indeed it was and is. Banks were the
exception. Between the Civil War and the Depression, the stockholders of a failed bank got a capital call from
the receiver. They were liable for an amount up to and including the par value of their shares.
They got the dividends in good times. They owned the problem in bad times. In short, they were accountable.
They are no more, at least not in that most tangible way. I submit this fact for consideration in the next edition
of "The Reckoning."
---
Mr. Grant is the editor of Grant's Interest Rate Observer.

It's Less Clear Now That the Fed Is on the Right Path
Letters to the Editor
It's Less Clear Now That the Fed Is on the Right Path
668 words
25 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A12
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
George Melloan repeats the myth that "the Fed pays a quarter of a percentage point to borrow from the
banks the money it uses . . . for 'quantitative easing' ("In Going Long, the Fed Is Short-Sighted," op-ed, April
16).
The only defense of this interpretation is that the financial arrangements in question have some
characteristics of a "forced loan." Banks must accept newly created Fed deposits from whomever sells
securities to the Fed, and the resulting bank reserves do pay 0.25% interest. On the other hand, it is
misleading and confusing to claim banks are lending to the Federal Reserve. Banks don't initiate or approve
these "loans," the "loans" won't be repaid and aren't carried as loans on the banks' balance sheets, and the
Fed has total control over the interest paid, if any, on the banks' reserves (not loans).
This confusing terminology obscures the most important power of the Federal Reserve: its virtually unlimited
ability to create deposits and reserves. To obfuscate this reality is to encourage an inflationary disaster
somewhere down the road -- a road paved with good intentions of reducing unemployment.
Mr. Melloan finally gets it right when he recognizes the dangers resulting from the huge levels of excess
reserves in the system, but he gives no clear explanation that these reserves were created by the Fed to
finance its quantitative easing programs.
Em. Prof. Robert F. Stauffer
Roanoke College
Salem, Va.
---
Mr. Melloan rightly states, "The Fed is clearly doing everything it can, at the expense of small savers, to
provide the U.S. Treasury with minimal borrowing costs to help it finance a profligate administration and
Congress." Simply put, the Federal Reserve, rather than being the independent agency it's supposed to be, is
a creature of the Treasury.
Patrick Totman
Scottsdale, Ariz.
---
The Fed is inexorably destroying retirement nest eggs which have taken decades of frugality to create. The
risk-averse saver is being forced to choose between watching his stash evaporate due to inflation, accepting
negative after-tax returns on savings or risk it chasing positive returns in equity dividends. The other choice
for safe, positive return is (surprise) "going long" on CDs and Treasury bonds.
Given the economic philosophy of Ben Bernanke and Janet Yellen, I've come to believe that the Fed is
determined to fundamentally transform the financial landscape by forcing the saver to either "go long" with
idle money (five-year CDs/10-30-year bonds) or go broke. The beneficiaries are banks, which can then
project a constant cost of funds, and all levels of government, from Washington, D.C., to the local school
districts, which can call -- or pre-call -- bonds to refinance long-term debt. This assumes, of course, people
are foolish enough to buy new 30-year paper at 3.5%.
Mike Smith
Sugar Land, Texas

--- Page 116 ---
Page 116 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
---
As of April 16, the Fed had $56.1 billion of capital on its balance sheet. If yields increase 0.33% and the Fed
"marks to market," the decrease in value of the Fed's Treasury portfolio would eliminate its capital.
Most of the Fed's $4.2 trillion in liabilities appear to be short term and as a consequence have short
durations. The value of the liabilities wouldn't decline nearly as much as the portfolio of Treasurys and other
securities. That would leave the Fed with few options to repair its balance sheet. It could simply exempt itself
from the "mark to market" rule that applies to commercial banks, even though it would be hypocritical. An
alternative would be to revalue its gold certificates, which are currently valued at $42.22 per ounce for a total
of $11 billion. But, the statutory price of gold is set by law and would require legislation to amend the Gold
Reserve Act of 1934. That could be difficult in the current Congress.
Robert G. George
Easton, Conn.

World News: Lack of Opportunity Frustrates South African Voters --- With Ruling Party Poised to Win May Elections, President Faces Mounting...
World News: Lack of Opportunity Frustrates South African Voters --- With Ruling Party Poised to Win
May Elections, President Faces Mounting Anger; Striking Miners Question Longtime Allegiances
By Devon Maylie
903 words
24 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A11
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
RUSTENBURG, South Africa -- The country's ruling African National Congress is expected to win national
elections in May, but public frustration is growing over widening income disparity and the government's
perceived inability to address the issue.
The victors will preside over an increasingly fragile political and economic landscape, especially here in South
Africa's platinum heartland, where strikes have dearly cost the world's biggest producer of the metal.
Talks to end the latest strike, which is in its third month, are set to resume Thursday. Mining companies say
they have taken a loss of more than $1.4 billion in revenue and that workers have lost $500 million in salaries.
President Jacob Zuma and his party are facing public anger that has swelled since the March disclosure of
the $23 million upgrade to his private home, a cost seven times higher than Nelson Mandela's security
upgrade, and far more than previous presidents.
When a speaker at an ANC event here last month tried to rally the crowd behind the president by shouting
"Zuma is with us," the crowd was mostly silent.
"No, I don't support Zuma," said 21-year old Mamello Agnes Mupedi, who lives in nearby Marikana, the site of
violent mining protests two years ago. "All he wants is money."
South Africa under the ANC has made strides since its first democratic elections in 1994.
Seven million more households have access to electricity compared with two decades ago in the country of
51 million. Access to education for people of all colors has vastly improved. And life expectancy has
increased by eight years in the past eight years, reflecting progress in health care, notably the treatment of
HIV-AIDS.
But the gap between rich and poor has increased, based on the Gini Coefficient, which measures a society's
equality.
What is more, the income disparity between white and black South Africans remains stark. The average
annual income for a black South African remains one sixth of a white South African, government data show.
Empowerment programs that offer equity stakes and other concessions to nonwhite South Africans have
failed to close these gaps.
Instead, they have reinforced perceptions that a few well-connected people gain at the expense of the
broader population.
The work on Mr. Zuma's home has compounded these perceptions. In March, the state anticorruption
watchdog, the Public Protector, said $23 million in taxpayer money had so far been spent to upgrade the
home in his birthplace of Nkandla, in rural KwaZulu-Natal province.
The security upgrades involved a chicken run, a cattle corral and a swimming pool. Mr. Zuma's architect
earned $1.5 million in fees overseeing the project.
"Not only are the streets aflame with angry poor people, but the state's own ability to turn the situation around
is severely weakened," editor Songezo Zibi wrote in South African newspaper BusinessDay last month.
"South Africa stands at a precipice of its own making."
After telling a crowd while campaigning last month that he didn't use taxpayer money to upgrade his home,
Mr. Zuma this month said he would respond in more detail to the allegations at a later time, but that he is

--- Page 118 ---
Page 118 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"concerned" about the allegations of maladministration and cost inflation around the security upgrade. He
has publicly said he didn't ask for the upgrades so shouldn't have to pay for them.
The ANC accused the Public Protector of aiding the party's political rivals by releasing the report weeks
before elections. It also said the party would find those responsible for allowing the cost overruns, but that Mr.
Zuma couldn't be responsible for subordinates' decisions. Public Protector Thuli Madonsela said the ANC
attacks against her department were part of electioneering.
Still, despite the troubles facing the ANC, many black voters don't see an alternative and still feel an
allegiance to the party for bringing freedom to the country.
The party that secures a majority of the 400 seats in Parliament chooses the president, and the ANC has said
it would give Mr. Zuma a second five-year term.
In Rustenberg -- located in a part of the country where mining strikes played a big role in weakening
apartheid, and where the union to which most miners belong was until two years ago allied with the ANC --
the sense of resentment is directed equally at the companies and at the president.
"Why would I bother wearing one of these ANC T-shirts?" asked unemployed 22-year old Tirelo Kaledi, when
he was offered a T-shirt with the face of Mr. Zuma. "We're tired of them making promises and not delivering
to the rest of us."
The strike costs could soon pass the losses incurred during work stoppages in 2012, which shut most of the
country's gold, platinum and iron-ore production and peaked when police fired into a crowd of protesters at a
Lonmin PLC mine, killing 34 people.
Mbulelo Magula, a striking miner who said he proudly cast a ballot for Mr. Mandela in 1994, said he won't
vote for the ANC anymore.
"Look at where the president lives and where we live," he said. "The government isn't doing anything for us."

Two Largest Gold Miners Talked Deal --- Barrick and Newmont Get Close but Call Off Merger for Now; Both Hit by Lower Prices, Overexpansion
Two Largest Gold Miners Talked Deal --- Barrick and Newmont Get Close but Call Off Merger for Now;
Both Hit by Lower Prices, Overexpansion
By Gillian Tan, Alistair MacDonald and Dana Mattioli
905 words
19 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Barrick Gold Corp. and Newmont Mining Corp. recently held abortive talks over a deal that would have
combined the world's two largest gold producers at a time when they are battling a sharp drop in the price of
gold, according to people familiar with the matter.
The two companies had intended to announce a deal as early as Tuesday, one of the people said. They have
discussed combining a number of times before, people familiar with the matter have said, and it is possible
they could do so again.
The deal talks come as the companies try to adapt to lower gold prices. The precious metal's futures fell 28%
last year -- their biggest annual price drop since 1981.
Prices have fallen amid moves by the Fed to wind down its efforts to stimulate the economy. The stimulus
steps had been a source of support for gold, which is used by investors as a hedge against the inflation that
such efforts can spark.
Merging the companies, which have a combined market value of more than $30 billion, would create a
natural-resources giant with mines ranging from Indonesia to Africa and throughout North and South America.
In arguing for the merits of the long-rumored deal, analysts have noted the two miners have neighboring
operations in Nevada, the source of about 60% of Barrick's production, and operations in proximity in both
Peru and Australia.
While contending with lower gold prices, a combined Barrick-Newmont would have to grapple with some of
the mining industry's most problematic projects. In Indonesia, for example, Newmont faces a ban on the
export of its raw mineral ores as the government looks to force miners to do the lucrative work of refining
within the country.
For Barrick, the world's largest gold producer, the effort to unite with its rival follows one of its toughest years.
In February, the Toronto-based company posted a fourth-quarter loss of $2.8 billion after taking an
impairment charge for troubled projects, notably its giant Pascua Lama operation, which straddles the border
of Chile and Argentina.
The company also slashed its gold reserves by 26%, said it expected lower production in 2014 and cut its
capital-spending plans.
Barrick has been punished by investors after years of empire building, in particular the giant Pascua Lama
mine, which has been hampered by environmental problems in Chile and rising construction costs. That
project is now on hold and the company has written off over $6 billion of its value.
Founder and co-chairman Peter Munk, who will step down from the board later this month, has previously
talked of trying to make Barrick one of the very largest mining companies in the world.
Barrick and Newmont, which is based in Greenwood Village, Colo., have been shedding assets as they turn
their focus toward lower-cost, more-profitable mines. Newmont has generated close to $600 million from
selling assets since mid-2013, while Barrick has done more than $1 billion in divestitures.
Newmont, No. 2 in gold production after Barrick, traces its roots to 1916. It was founded by William Boyce
Thompson, who grew up in Montana but earned his wealth in New York. Barrick was founded in 1983 by Mr.
Munk.
Both companies, which have operations on five continents, have made a string of acquisitions over the years.
Barrick's largest was the takeover of rival Canadian gold miner Placer Dome Inc. for $10.4 billion in 2006,

--- Page 120 ---
Page 120 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
while Newmont's biggest addition was the simultaneous takeover of Australia's Normandy Mining Ltd. and its
largest shareholder, Canada's Franco-Nevada Mining Corp., for $4.4 billion in 2002.
Barrick's Cortez mine in Nevada produced 1.34 million ounces of gold in 2013, making it one of the world's
largest. But output is expected to decline to as low as 925,000 ounces this year.
Newmont's projects in the state include 14 open-pit and four underground mines and 14 processing facilities.
A merger of these operations would allow the two miners to cut costs and bolster profits.
In 2013, Barrick and Newmont together would have produced a total of 12.27 million ounces of gold and 683
million pounds of copper.
Although gold prices have picked up this year, Barrick shares have slumped 21% over the past 12 months,
giving it a current market value of about $21 billion. Newmont shares have slipped 35% over the same period,
giving it a current market value of about $12 billion. The stock market was closed Friday for the Good Friday
holiday.
There have been some notable mining deals announced lately.
In the past week, gold companies Yamana Gold Inc. and Agnico-Eagle Mines Ltd. said they reached an
agreement to buy Osisko Mining Corp. for about $3.5 billion, beating out an offer from larger rival Goldcorp
Inc. Glencore Xstrata PLC this month also agreed to sell a copper mine in Peru to a Chinese consortium for
$5.8 billion.
The $61 billion in mining mergers and acquisitions announced last year was the lowest tally since 2009 and
less than half the dollar value of deals reached the year before, according to Dealogic.

California's Malpractice Ruse
REVIEW & OUTLOOK (Editorial)
California's Malpractice Ruse
645 words
17 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A14
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
One of California's few emollients for employers is its limit on "pain and suffering" medical liability judgments,
which has improved access to medical care and held down health costs. But look out: Plaintiffs lawyers
abetted by Attorney General Kamala Harris are now trying to gut the cap with a ballot initiative dressed in
patient-protection garb.
In 1975 on his first tour as Governor, Jerry Brown signed legislation limiting attorney fees and non-economic
damages on medical malpractice claims to $250,000. Lawsuits were driving up malpractice premiums,
causing thousands of California physicians to close their practices and insurers to drop coverage of high-risk
specialties. While plaintiffs can still recoup unlimited compensation for future medical costs and lost wages,
the law has deterred attorneys from filing meritless lawsuits and reduced liability insurance costs.
A 2004 article in the Archives of Internal Medicine reported that California malpractice premiums have
increased by less than 3% annually, or one-third of the rate nationally, and have fallen by 40% in constant
dollars since 1975. Annual liability premiums for an OB/GYN in Los Angeles are $49,804 compared to
$140,092 in Chicago and $176,005 in Long Island. Neither New York nor Illinois limits non-economic awards.
California's cap, which is the gold standard in medical malpractice reform, has inspired legislation in more
than a dozen states, most notably Texas. In 2003 the Lone Star State limited non-economic damages to
$250,000. A January 2014 study by the Berkeley Research Group reported that the number of claims has
since declined by nearly 40% while the average award has fallen to $138,429 from $214,939. Between 2002
and 2012 Texas added 30 more physicians per 100,000 residents, according to a 2013 article in the Journal
of Gastrointestinal Surgery.
Attorneys unsuccessfully sued to overturn California's cap in 1985 and have been trying to persuade their
friends in the legislature to lift the limit for decades. However, they have faced pushback from other
Democratic interest groups including trade unions and low-income community health clinics.
Hence their new gambit to qualify an initiative for the November ballot that would lift the cap on non-economic
damages to $1.1 million, which going forward would be adjusted for inflation. Lawyers complain the 1975
cap didn't include an inflation indexer. However, the average payout has since risen more than 2.5 times the
rate of inflation due to soaring health costs, which factor into economic damages. Attorneys stand to win an
additional $127,500 on average per judgment if the initiative passes.
Patients will ultimately bear this cost. The Berkeley Research Group estimated that raising the cap on
damages to $1 million would increase malpractice premiums by between 16% and 38%, based on the
experience of other states that have imposed or eliminated limits. California's annual health costs would rise
by $9.9 billion, or $1,000 for a family of four.
Higher premiums would also discourage new doctors, particularly those in high-risk specialities, from setting
up practices in California and cause some doctors to leave or retire. A 2010 study by the Northwestern
University Feinberg School of Medicine reported that nearly half of residents trained in Illinois were leaving
the state with two-thirds citing high liability insurance costs as a reason. Many community health clinics that
serve low-income populations might also close.
Attorneys realize that their initiative will be a hard pill for voters to swallow, so they've encapsulated it in
measures that crack down on narcotics-abusing doctors. Ms. Harris has obliged her trial bar friends by writing
a ballot summary that buries the initiative's intent. "Medical negligence lawsuits" is mentioned only in the
summary's fifth and final line. Too bad there's no Hippocratic Oath for politicians like Ms. Harris.

U.S. stocks rallied Wednesday, bringing the S&P 500 back into positive territory for the year.
Sentiment got a lift from rising hopes that China and Japan will take steps to bolster their economies.
Meanwhile, a two-day bounce in technology and biotechnology stocks provided relief for a corner of the
market that has suffered big losses over the past month.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average rose 162.29 points, or 1%, to 16424.85, to notch its biggest three-day gain
this year. The S&P 500 index advanced 19.33 points, or 1%, to 1862.31, with all 10 sectors higher.
The Nasdaq Composite Index posted its third consecutive day of gains, climbing 52.06 points, or 1.3%, to
4086.23. Still, the Nasdaq is down 6.2% from its most recent high set on March 5. The Nasdaq Biotechnology
Index rose 2.4%.
While frothier segments of the market have seen heightened price swings in recent weeks, broad U.S. stock
benchmarks haven't given up much ground from record levels hit on April 2. The S&P 500 has lost 0.5% this
month, and is just 1.5% below its all-time high. The Dow industrials have held up better, down 0.2% this
month and 0.9% from their record level.
Much of the attention, however, remains on formerly highflying stocks that have taken a big hit. Even with a
rebound, the Nasdaq biotech index is down 18.5% from its most-recent high.
Those lower levels have attracted bargain hunters, some said.
"You're seeing real buyers come in" for shares of individual companies, said Viren Chandrasoma, managing

The day's rally began overseas.
A report showed that China's gross domestic product rose a slightly better-than-expected 7.4% in the first
quarter, but the slowing pace of growth raised hopes of more stimulus from the Chinese government.
Meanwhile, Japan's Nikkei Stock Average shot up 3% after Bank of Japan Gov. Haruhiko Kuroda said he
would closely monitor stock prices and that inflation was on a steady track toward a 2.0% target. China's
Shanghai Composite gained 0.2%.
Yahoo rose $2.14, or 6.3%, to $36.35 after first-quarter results exceeded expectations, and the company
provided an upbeat revenue outlook for the current quarter.
Bank of America slipped 26 cents, or 1.6%, to 16.13 after the banking giant reported it swung to a first-quarter
loss as a result of previously undisclosed litigation expenses, while revenue fell slightly less than expected.
New York investment bank Moelis & Co. rose 1.15, or 4.6%, to 26.15 in its public debut, after pricing its initial
public offering below its forecast range.
The yield on the 10-year Treasury note rose to 2.637% from 2.628% late Tuesday. Gold futures gained
0.2% to $1,303.10 a troy ounce, after falling 2% Tuesday to mark the biggest one-day percentage loss since
Dec. 19.
Crude-oil futures tacked on less than 0.1% to $103.76 a barrel. The dollar rose against the yen, but lost some
ground against the euro.

Gold Falls on China-Demand Worries
Commodities Report
Gold Falls on China-Demand Worries
By Tatyana Shumsky
421 words
16 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Gold prices fell 2%, the largest one-day drop since December, as worries about weaker demand from China
prompted some investors to reduce their gold holdings.
Gold for April delivery ended $27.20 lower at $1,300.00 a troy ounce on the Comex division of the New York
Mercantile Exchange. This was the steepest fall since futures slid 3.4% on Dec. 19, a day after the Federal
Reserve said it would begin winding down its stimulus program.
Gold's drop illustrates the outsize role China has come to play in the market. China edged out India to
become the world's top gold consumer in 2013, when homemakers and businesspeople bought the metal at
the lowest prices in more than three years to give as gifts or to preserve their wealth. China's strong demand
for gold helped stabilize prices, which slid 28% last year.
But data out of China on Tuesday made gold investors nervous about future demand. China's money supply
rose 12.1% in March from a year earlier, missing the central bank's target of 13% growth for the first time
since April 2012.
"If the money isn't flying around the economy, they don't have money to buy gold," said Adam Klopfenstein, a
senior market strategist with Archer Financial Services LLC in Chicago.
In addition, a World Gold Council report released Tuesday forecast that Chinese gold demand will stagnate
this year, remaining at the record 1,187 metric tons consumed in 2013.
The market will be carefully watching China's gross domestic product data for the first quarter, due out
Wednesday. Analysts expect economic growth for the quarter to slow to a 7.3% pace from 7.7% at the end of
last year.
"People are a bit nervous about the figure coming out from China, especially after the money-supply data we
got today, and they just wanted to take some money off the table," said Graham Leighton, a metals broker at
Marex Spectron in New York.
In other markets, arabica coffee for July delivery fell 6% to $1.9505 a pound on the ICE Futures U.S.
exchange after investors cashed in early gains. Prices initially rose to $2.10 a pound, the highest since
February 2012, on continued worries about supplies from Brazil. The world's largest coffee grower and
exporter has experienced the worst drought in decades, which is expected to crimp its harvest.

China Is Losing Its Voracious Taste for Gold --- Demand for Yellow Metal Expected to Remain Flat in 2014, Posing Threat to Rebounding Prices...
Commodities Report
China Is Losing Its Voracious Taste for Gold --- Demand for Yellow Metal Expected to Remain Flat in
2014, Posing Threat to Rebounding Prices After Big Drop Last Year
By Tatyana Shumsky and Chao Deng
798 words
15 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
China's appetite for gold is waning after a decadelong buying spree, suppressed by the country's economic
slowdown and constrained credit markets.
Demand in the world's biggest gold consumer is likely to stay flat in 2014, according to estimates from the
World Gold Council. Gold demand in China has expanded every year since 2002, when it declined,
according to the industry group, whose forecasts are closely watched in the gold market.
Decelerating Chinese gold demand could threaten the recent recovery in gold prices, some investors and
analysts say. Gold futures are up 10% year to date, after falling 28% last year, the biggest annual drop since
1981. Unrest in Ukraine, a bumpy start to the year for stocks, and the Federal Reserve's commitment to low
interest rates in the long term have propelled gold higher. Investors typically seek the metal as a haven in
times of turmoil and a store of value during periods of low rates.
On Monday, front-month gold for April delivery rose $8.50, or 0.6%, to $1,327.20 a troy ounce on the Comex
division of the New York Mercantile Exchange.
Chinese consumption has helped to underpin gold prices since 2001, when many price and trading
restrictions were relaxed. Last year saw frenzied buying as Chinese investors and jewelry buyers sought to
capitalize on low prices. Chinese demand jumped 32% in 2013, vaulting the country past India to first place in
the rankings of the world's gold consumers. But it is unlikely that record pace can be maintained, even if
prices turn lower, according to the World Gold Council.
"We're looking at best for it to be on par with 2013," said Albert Cheng, managing director for the Far East at
the World Gold Council. The council is releasing its latest report on China's gold market Tuesday.
Although the report doesn't offer a figure for estimated Chinese gold demand this year, it says 2014 will be a
year of consolidation. "Chinese consumers brought forward jewelry and bar purchases, which may limit
growth in demand in 2014," the report said.
Mr. Cheng pegs China's private-sector gold consumption, a category that includes jewelry, bullion and
industrial demand, to remain roughly at 2013's level of 1,187 metric tons.
"The situation in China is certainly a headwind," said Vedant Mimani, lead portfolio manager with the $80
million Atyant Capital Global Opportunities Fund, which is based in Miami. "This demand that's been there for
11 years has become questionable."
The fund holds bets on falling gold prices. Mr. Mimani said he expects gold to drop below $1,200 in the next
six months amid tight credit conditions in China.
In recent months, Beijing has focused on reining in China's system of unofficial lending and shoring up banks
against a property sector that many analysts believe is overheated. As a result, more cash is being tied up in
uses other than for gold.
In the longer term, the World Gold Council estimates that China's appetite will rev up again. Chinese gold
demand, excluding factory stockpiles, will increase 19% by 2017 to 1,350 metric tons, from 1,132 tons in
2013, according to the report. As China's economy continues to expand and citizens become more affluent,
more of them will purchase gold jewelry or bullion for the first time, Mr. Cheng said.
While gold's recent climb has been driven mainly by Western investors, demand from China also could pick
up if property values there drop sharply or if the financial system shows more signs of stress, said Joe Foster,
portfolio manager of Van Eck International Investors Gold Fund, with $719 million under management.

--- Page 125 ---
Page 125 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"Chinese citizens have a limited range of investment options . . . and they have a cultural affinity toward
gold," he said.
There was little activity visible on Monday at Chenghuang Jewelry, a store just outside Yuyuan Gardens, a
popular tourist attraction in Shanghai that is home to throngs of jewelry merchants. Sales associates stood
around chatting, while a handful of customers perused wedding jewelry and gemstones. No customers were
at the counter dedicated to gold-bar purchases.
"We're not seeing the kind of crazy buying we saw last year," said store manager Karone Huang. Last year,
"we couldn't even fill our orders fast enough."
A big reason, Ms. Huang said, is that many in China believe they already have enough gold on hand. And
the rebound in prices in the past three months hasn't been big enough to attract new buyers, she said.

losses. The index is down 11.7% in April.

would carry particular weight for investors, especially in the beaten-down technology sector where investors
had been paying high prices for relatively low earnings. "Expectations have been brought down greatly," he
said.

Another Tech Tumble Pushes Nasdaq Lower --- All Three Major Stock Indexes End Week in the Red;
Traders Are 'Getting a Little More Nervous' but 'Aren't Panicking'

Traders again said there was no specific news behind the latest tech selloff. Instead, some short-term traders
such as hedge funds sold part of their riskiest holdings and others pared back on stocks in general.
Yet many investors stressed that the tech-led decline isn't a reason for unusual concern. The fundamentals
underlying the market's advance over the past year -- low interest rates, an expanding economy and an
accommodative Federal Reserve -- remain largely in place. Traders said longer-term investors, such as
mutual funds, have largely been sitting tight.
Traders are "getting a little more nervous," said Joe Saluzzi, co-head of equity trading at institutional
brokerage firm Themis Trading. "But they aren't panicking."

biotechnology and smaller technology stocks.
The index slid 3.1% in the week, the biggest weekly percentage loss since the week ended June 1, 2012. The
index is down 8.2% since closing at a 14-year high on March 5.
Much of the selling has come from hedge funds cutting their overall exposure to stocks in recent weeks, in
both bullish and bearish bets, according to data provided by Credit Suisse.
Funds have especially been cutting their exposure to growing companies, which tend to have more volatile
share prices, while boosting bets on more defensive sectors, such as dividend-paying utilities and consumer
and pharmaceutical firms.
Among onetime highfliers hit hard recently, Gilead Sciences gained 55 cents, or 0.8%, to 66.03 Friday, after

Stocks' early gains heated up after the afternoon release of the Fed minutes. The Dow had been up about 76
points before the release of the transcript.
"The markets are breathing a little bit of a sigh of relief," said Ryan Larson, head of equity trading at RBC

Also helping investor mood was an encouraging earnings report from Alcoa, which helped get first-quarter

When it comes to broad forecasts for the S&P 500, he said an unusually icy winter likely weighed on
first-quarter estimates, "but we do think that the recovery is going to pick up steam as we go through the
year."
Investors and strategists said they expect trading during earnings season to be bumpier this year. Analysts
expect first-quarter earnings from companies in the S&P 500 to decline 1.4% from last year, according to
FactSet.
Meanwhile, the yield on the 10-year Treasury note rose to 2.684% from 2.681% late on Tuesday.
Crude oil gained 1% to settle at $103.60 a barrel, after running up 2.1% on Tuesday as government

advanced 0.7%.

Gold Glovers in Short Supply --- Blowout Loss to the Orioles Is a Reminder That the Yankees' Aging
Defense Is Cause for Concern
By Daniel Barbarisi
955 words
9 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A24
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
BAL 14
NYY 5
The most telling moment in Tuesday's 14-5 demolition of the Yankees by the power-hitting Baltimore Orioles
didn't come on one of the three home runs the Orioles hit. Nor was it one of the fat pitches that Yankee starter
Ivan Nova served up in his brief outing.
No, it was Derek Jeter lunging in vain at a first-inning ground ball off the bat of Delmon Young that most
major-league shortstops would have snared for at least one out, and likely for a double play. Instead, the ball
went by Jeter's outstretched glove, Orioles leadoff man Nick Markakis advanced to third, and the Orioles
soon jumped out to an early lead.
"I know he's trying the best to get the double play, and unfortunately it doesn't work the way we wanted to, but
that's the game," said Nova. "That happens."
Before the game got out of hand, before Nova was pulled and reliever Vidal Nuno was fed to a hungry
Orioles offense that amassed 20 hits in the game, the Yankees showed why their defense has been a cause
for concern since the team began to take shape over the winter.
Jeter, who will turn 40 in June, is moving about as well as one would expect for a shortstop of that age who is
also coming off ankle surgery. Manager Joe Girardi defended his effort on Tuesday, though not exactly his
range. "He dove," Girardi said. "He did everything he could to make that play."
For these Yankees, who fell to 4-4 on the season, defensive issues abound well beyond Jeter, with some
players inexperienced and others well past their defensive primes: Kelly Johnson is trying to learn first base
and third base; second baseman Brian Roberts, at 36, isn't moving like he used to; and the Yankees are
playing catcher Francisco Cervelli at first with Mark Teixeira injured.
These and more were all on display Tuesday as the defense let Nova down, exacerbating the pitcher's own
struggles.
Even the Yankees' better defenders showed their weak spots Tuesday. After Jeter failed to field the
first-inning grounder, Baltimore's Chris Davis lofted a fly ball to shallow center field, with Markakis on third.
Jacoby Ellsbury -- an outfielder better known for his legs than his arm -- made an accurate throw to home
plate that lacked enough zip to nail Markakis, who slid in under Brian McCann's tag with the game's first run.
Both plays kept the inning alive long enough for Baltimore's Adam Jones to come to bat, and he dealt the
shaky Nova a nasty blow with a two-run homer to straightaway center field. Suddenly the Yankees were
down 3-0.
The poor defensive plays didn't stop there. Cervelli, filling in at first base, came in hard to field a well-placed
bunt by Baltimore shortstop Ryan Flaherty, but Nova hesitated and failed to go for the ball or cover the base,
letting Flaherty cruise into first.
"I confused myself a little bit there, and that's a play you should make," Nova said.
Later, Roberts came in a little slow to field a grounder in the fourth, then pulled Cervelli off the bag with his
throw as another run scored.

--- Page 133 ---
Page 133 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
And while it wasn't an error, rookie third baseman Yangervis Solarte, who did have two doubles and an RBI in
the game, let a hard-hit ball bounce under his glove for a double into the corner, another reminder that the
Yankees aren't exactly running out gold-glovers at every position.
Everything that happened Tuesday drove home the danger of playing older players all over the diamond,
while using others out of position.
While they still have standout defenders like Brett Gardner in left field and the steady McCann behind the
plate, at the moment the entire Yankee infield is one big question mark.
It speaks volumes that Cervelli, a catcher playing first base Tuesday for the first time since he was 15 years
old, was one of the better defenders on this day. "I think maybe with more games, I'll get more comfortable,"
Cervelli said.
It certainly wasn't all his fault. Nova was unimpressive, giving up seven runs on 10 hits in 3 2/3 innings, and
Nuno was even worse, surrendering seven runs on eight hits, including two home runs, in 3 1/3 innings of
relief. Every player in Baltimore's starting lineup got a hit and scored a run in the game. But neither pitcher got
much help from his defenders, making bad situations worse.
The defense should improve when Teixeira returns from the DL in a few weeks, but there is little the Yankees
can do about some of their other defensive deficiencies.
That makes it even more important that the team use data correctly to shift their infielders into the right
positions, anticipating where balls will go instead of needing to chase them down.
"I think [shifts are] extremely important," Girardi said. "There's obviously a lot of talk and data that goes into it.
We look at histories and our pitchers and what type of pitches they have, and we try to put them in the best
place possible. Sometimes they don't always go there, but we're playing the law of averages and you're going
to see it work a lot more than it not work."
If they don't, get ready for more diving plays and balls bouncing just beyond outstretched Yankee gloves.

A Look Inside America's Frack-Fueled Energy Boom
A Look Inside America's Frack-Fueled Energy Boom
By Russell Gold
1,861 words
8 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Fracking has revolutionized the energy industry and changed geopolitics. But what does the process of
removing natural gas and oil from rock actually look like? As part of his new book, "The Boom," Wall Street
Journal reporter Russell Gold visited a frack site. Here's what he found:
A few miles south of Killdeer, North Dakota, amid rolling grassland that stretches out of sight, workers huddle
close together to hear Josh Byington over the blustery prairie wind. "Welcome out here, gentlemen," says Mr.
Byington.
Just shy of forty years old, he has two decades' experience in the oil fields.
"Pay attention to what is going on. This is big business. Hit the wrong switch, it could be your life or someone
else's." He pauses. "No one gets hurt today, right?"
There are murmurs of assent. One worker yells, "Exactly!"
"All right, gentlemen," Mr. Byington says as he looks around, "let's go have some fun."
I have come to North Dakota to observe the fracking of the Irene Kovaloff 11-18H, a well on the southern
edge of the Bakken Shale. It is one of one hundred wells that will be fracked in the U.S. on this particular day
in October 2012, 10 in North Dakota alone.
Energy companies have come to North Dakota because when they frack the Bakken, light sweet crude oil
comes out of the rock. The wells here produce a bit of natural gas also, and no one wants to wait around for a
connection to a gas pipeline, so they flare it off. At night, the onyx sky flickers with gas being burned off.
Mr. Byington oversees the movements of the couple dozen coverall-clad workers. His directive is simple
enough: force more than one million gallons of liquid into the Irene Kovaloff under enough pressure to
crumple the toughest car Detroit can turn out. When Mr. Byington and his crew have finished, this section of
the Bakken will be filled with thousands of tiny fractures, smashed into pieces like a shattered dinner plate.
The Irene Kovaloff, a recently drilled, long, and narrow hole in the ground, went straight down 2 miles until it
reached the Bakken Shale, and then made a gradual 90-degree turn and continued for another two more
miles.
Before workers transform the Irene Kovaloff into a producing oil well, they must make sure everything is
secure. Some workers spend their shift outside, smelling musty diesel and pulling their hoodies tight to keep
out the cold. Another group heads climbs into a temperature-controlled trailer called the data van. They take
off their hard hats and strip down to T-shirts under their coveralls. There is a coffee urn and a car stereo
bolted into a wall panel. On a recent night, Pink Floyd's "The Dark Side of the Moon" accompanied a frack.
Mr. Byington heads inside.
Inside the van, several workers with large headsets sit in gray swivel chairs bolted to the floor. They work at a
long desk, with computers and monitors displaying pressures, volumes, pH balances, and a dozen other
measurements. The van feels a bit like a NASA command center. But rather than clean-cut engineers, the
workers are scruffy. There is an assortment of beards and mustaches, and hair spilling out from under
baseball caps. The North Dakota oil field has boomed so quickly that certain basic necessities -- an
appointment with a barber included -- are hard to find.
People have been drilling into the Bakken for decades. The first well was in 1953. It was fracked with 4,900
pounds of sand and 120 barrels of crude oil. Modern Bakken fracks are of a different scale. Mr. Byington
orchestrates a job that uses four trucks full of sand and more than one million gallons of water, which sit in
sixty blue shipping containers that formed a wall around two sides of the pad.

--- Page 135 ---
Page 135 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
At each well, Marathon Oil's playbook is the same. Assemble a drilling rig and drill a well. Disassemble. Frack
the well. Move on. Repeat.
At the Irene Kovaloff, problems arise. The wind is too strong to assemble the pipes. A leak appears during a
routine prefrack pressure test. Inside the van, Sly Henderson, a thin man with short-cropped hair and a thin
mustache riding atop his upper lip, issues commands. A blue-clad worker delivers a dozen hard blows with a
large hammer to the faulty valve to tighten it. The leak reappears. Mr. Henderson sighs. "Let's replace the
gasket," he says. He picks up a Styrofoam cup and spits tobacco juice into it.
For two weeks at a stretch, Mr. Henderson lives in one of the many man camps that have been built in North
Dakota. Cobbled together in a hurry, these sprawling complexes of connected modular buildings can hold
seven hundred to 1,000 workers each. The largest operator of these man camps houses one out of every
hundred North Dakotans.
"Everyone comes for the money. Everyone comes because they're out of work," says Mr. Byington. "The
guys who make it, who stick around, come from a working background. They're used to working hard for long
hours."
After the gasket is replaced, Mr. Henderson speaks through his headset. "OK, everyone in position. Ready to
go?" There are murmurs of assent. Liquid rushes down the well and hits the Bakken. The frack begins.
The Irene Kovaloff is a small part of an enormous change in the U.S.'s relationship with oil. After World War
II, inexpensive oil fed an economy that roared. The country became an enormous consumer of oil, and one of
the world's largest oil producers. In late 1970 10 million barrels a day came from U.S. wells. And then began
a period of slow decline. But consumption didn't slow. It kept increasing. Rising imports made up the
difference.
By 2030, North America could become an oil exporter. Of course, these kinds of predictions are often wrong.
But the Irene Kovaloff -- and thousands of other wells like it -- are at the forefront of a major geopolitical
change. For generations, U.S. foreign policy aimed to keep the spigots flowing overseas and the channels of
oil delivery unimpeded by foreign potentates. As liquid enters the Irene Kovaloff, all of these foreign
entanglements seem less pressing.
The hydraulic heart of fracking is the liquid pumped into the well. Almost all of it is water: snowmelt from the
upper Rockies. In the Bakken and elsewhere, companies transform the water into a viscous liquid designed
to carry sand deep into the new fractures. As it heats up underground, the gel reverts to a watery state. This
change allows the sand to drop out and remain in the fractures, holding them open like pillars in a coal mine.
The water flows back out.
Bobby Kinsey, a fluid technician at the Irene Kovaloff, oversees this alchemic transformation. An open plastic
tub of potato salad and a Dr Pepper sit near his computer. Mr. Kinsey mixes up a test batch, using water and
syringes of liquefied guar and ingredients mysteriously labeled "30AG" and "32." Mr. Kinsey shrugs when I
ask what they were. I later discovered the first is similar to the fuel used in camping stoves. The other is a
mixture of boric acid and methanol.
Mr. Kinsey has mixed up a batch of what looks like Slime, the 1970s toy sold in small green plastic trash cans
that children let ooze through their fingers.
Water and guar make up about 99.1% of the liquid; the chemicals are the rest. Even in such small
concentrations, the volume of chemicals can add up. A laundry list includes 98 gallons of phosphonic acids,
118 gallons of magnesium hydroxide, and 138 gallons of 2-butoxyethanol, a compound used mostly by dry
cleaners and paint manufacturers.
Before 2008, there were a lot of attempts to frack long horizontal wells. Companies such as EOG Resources
and Brigham Exploration devised ways to seal off sections of the well and deliver pressure onto the rock
more effectively. The horizontal legs of wells got larger, the wells more expensive, but they produced more
oil. The cost to produce a single barrel was halved.
By driving down the cost per barrel, companies led by Brigham Exploration expanded the number of wells
that could be drilled profitably in the Bakken. A few years ago, 5,000 Bakken wells in North Dakota alone was
a dream. In 2010, the state predicted there would be 20,000 wells drilled over the next two decades. Within a
couple of years, it had more than doubled that estimate.
Bud Brigham founded his eponymous company in 1990 and has lived through the modern history of energy
production. "Used to be we'd go drill a wildcat well, one-in-ten chance of success," he said. "Now, with this
new technology. we drilled over a hundred consecutive successful wells in North Dakota . . . without a dry
one."

--- Page 136 ---
Page 136 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
This string of success isn't limited to Brigham. By the time Marathon Oil drilled the Irene Kovaloff in October
2012, it had drilled nearly 300 wells. Nearly every one found oil. Some were better producers than others, and
some suffered from escalating costs that crimped profits. But by fracking the rock, oil flowed from the shale.
In the data van, Sly Henderson keeps an eye on the gauges and array of numbers. Mounted on a wall is a
white monitor that graphs STP-1, or surface treater pressure -- a technical way of saying how much pressure
is on the Bakken. The liquid is being pressed against the rock at between 7,000 and 8,000 pounds per square
inch. Sit on the ocean floor in the Gulf of Mexico's deepest trench, and the pressure from 12,000 feet of water
above isn't equal to the pressure being applied by the frack fluid. When the Bakken gives in to this assault
and fractures, fluid will rush into the cracks, creating a momentary drop in pressure.
As Mr. Henderson waits for this signal, he cracks open a tin of chewing tobacco. For a minute, the line
displaying pressure is flat, like an electrocardiogram of a patient whose heart has stopped. Then a small dip
in pressure, followed by a spike and another dip. It looks like the patient's heart has restarted for a couple
beats. The patient is alive. The Bakken has been fracked
The next night, the 30th frack of the Irene Kovaloff is completed. It takes three hours longer than expected,
but otherwise the well is a success. Soon came light, sweet Bakken crude mixed with the water. On its first
full day, it produced 800 barrels of crude -- a good, but not great, result. By early 2013, Marathon had pulled
20,000 barrels of crude from the well. Considering that the oil had been locked away until the frack, it was
good enough.

From "The Boom" by Russell Gold. Copyright (c) 2014 by Russell Gold. Excerpted with permission by Simon
& Schuster, a division of Simon & Schuster, Inc.

WEEKEND INVESTOR --- The New Basics -- Wealth Effect: How to Save Your Retirement
WEEKEND INVESTOR --- The New Basics -- Wealth Effect: How to Save Your Retirement
By Brett Arends
1,042 words
5 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B8
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Invest $100,000 in a typical retirement account when you are 25, and how much should you expect to have
when you collect your gold watch 40 years later?
Based on some standard industry assumptions about investment portfolios, returns and volatility, a money
manager might tell you that the "average" outcome would be in the region of $1.2 million, adjusted for
inflation.
But here is the problem: That "average" masks a lot of trouble. Even if you use those same standard
assumptions, more than half the time you will end up with less than $750,000. And a lot of the time you will
end up with less than $350,000. Contrary to popular myth, the results aren't distributed normally, in a bell
curve. Thanks to volatility, they are bunched at the low end.
From $1.2 million to $350,000 or less -- that is going to make quite a difference.
This mathematical analysis appears in "Investing for Retirement: The Defined Contribution Challenge," a new
paper by Ben Inker and Martin Tarlie at GMO, a Boston-based investment firm with $117 billion under
management.
It took me back to my high-school math, and the difference between the three types of average -- the mean,
the median and the mode. To put it in a nutshell, that $1.2 million is the mean, or simple average, and it pretty
much is useless for planning purposes.
Few financial issues are more important to us all than planning for our retirement. And yet our approach to
the subject is riddled with some serious logical and mathematical errors, Messrs. Inker and Tarlie argue.
We misunderstand averages, volatility and the likely distribution of returns, they say. And we may be
misunderstanding financial history as well.
Many retirement planners, including the people from the 401(k) company hosting the free seminars at your
workplace, derive their forecasts for future returns from the past. That is perfectly understandable, as far as it
goes. But it is flawed.
Data from New York University's Stern School of Business show that since 1928, U.S. stocks, as measured
by the S&P 500, have earned average returns of 9.6% a year, while 10-year Treasury notes have earned 5%
a year.
But today the 10-year Treasury sports a yield of just 2.8%. Good luck trying to squeeze 5% a year from a
2.8% bond, Mr. Inker notes. Meanwhile, the S&P 500 now trades at around 25 times cyclically adjusted
per-share earnings of the past 10 years, a measure known as the Shiller price/earnings ratio, after Yale
finance professor and Nobel laureate Robert Shiller.
Yet data tracked by Prof. Shiller going back to the 19th century has found that when the U.S. index has
traded at these levels, subsequent returns have typically been dismal -- often failing even to keep up with
inflation.
"This is a dangerous time to be close to retirement," said Mr. Inker, co-head of GMO's asset-allocation
committee, when we met this past week in the firm's offices overlooking Boston Harbor. Because of elevated
asset valuations, relying on standard planning may "leave you in serious trouble."
So what can we do about it?
Some of the authors' conclusions aren't controversial. Investors need to get a better understanding of the
risks of poor returns. And they need to save more -- often much more -- to compensate.

--- Page 138 ---
Page 138 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Two of Messrs. Inker and Tarlie's other conclusions may raise eyebrows. First, and counterintuitively, they
say that many investors may need to take the plunge and invest more aggressively than conventional wisdom
suggests. That means holding more stocks and fewer bonds. That is because, even though stocks are
volatile and returns may be well below historic averages, over time they still tend to beat bonds by a wide
margin. And many investors simply can't afford low returns.
Second, they argue that investors should hold more foreign stocks, as opposed to U.S. stocks.
GMO believes international stocks are better value than those in the U.S. and offer better returns. But the
argument stacks up even if you don't necessarily embrace that analysis. If we should diversify as much as
possible to earn the best returns for the risk, shouldn't we invest globally as a matter of course? Why should
we have most of our eggs in the S&P 500?
At a social gathering, I once met the head of "target date" retirement funds at a big investment company. I
asked him why, across the industry, all such funds -- in which allocations grow more conservative over time --
were so overinvested in U.S. stocks at the expense of global diversification.
"Because that's what the clients want -- it's what we can sell," he confessed. It had nothing to do with what
was actually likely to be best for the clients in the long run.
A spokesman at the Investment Company Institute, which represents the mutual-fund industry, noted that
international stocks, including emerging markets, accounted for 30% of the equity allocation of the typical
target-date fund. But this still is well below their share of the global stock-market indexes or global economic
output
It is hard to see why the default allocation shouldn't be to put one third of one's equity allocation in U.S.
stocks, one third in developed overseas markets and one third in emerging markets -- even if you aren't
taking a view about which is better value.
GMO has a reputation for taking a deeply cautious view on markets. But that doesn't undermine Messrs.
Inker and Tarlie's analysis. And it leads to one other implication that they don't pursue but which I can't avoid
raising. If investment returns from stocks and bonds look so meager today, it may be sensible to park more
money on the sidelines in cash or an equivalent, such as a money-market or stable-value fund -- and wait,
and hope, that stocks or bonds will get cheaper.
Is that "timing" the market? Yes. Is it "risky"? Yes. But so is trusting the "averages."
