Page 139 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Stocks' Momentum Drained --- Dow Shrinks From Record and Nasdaq Drops 2.6% as Highfliers Lose Altitude
Friday's Markets
Stocks' Momentum Drained --- Dow Shrinks From Record and Nasdaq Drops 2.6% as Highfliers Lose
Altitude
By Matt Jarzemsky
954 words
5 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average tumbled to its steepest decline in three weeks, as investors stepped up
their sales of once-highflying technology shares following the latest monthly report on U.S. job growth.
The 30-stock Dow fell 159.84 points, or 1%, to 16412.71. But the selling was much more intense in the
faster-growing Internet, social-media and biotechnology companies that rallied sharply in the first two months
of 2014 before pulling back in recent weeks.
The washout accelerated Friday, following a morning rally that took the Dow Jones Industrial Average and
S&P 500 to fresh intraday highs.
The Nasdaq Composite Index fell 110.01 points, or 2.6%, to 4127.73, its biggest daily slide since Feb. 3. The
Nasdaq Biotechnology Index fell 4.1% to its lowest close since Jan. 6.
Parts of the market known more for steady returns and regular dividend payouts, such as telecommunications
and consumer-staples shares, held up better but didn't escape the downdraft. Those shares have benefited
over the past month as hedge funds and other investors have pulled back from biotechnology and other
so-called momentum plays.
"The out-of-growth, into-value unwind that's taken place over the last few weeks and has really decimated
guys, is starting to spill out into the broader market," said Ian Winer, director of equity trading at Wedbush
Securities.
The early market focus was on the jobs report. The U.S. economy added 192,000 payrolls in March, the
Labor Department reported, just shy of the 200,000 median forecast of economists polled by The Wall Street
Journal. Readings on February and January job growth were revised higher.
That, investors said, showed the U.S. economy is in a moderate growth mode and provided an early pop for
stocks. But there was little follow-through among investors and attention shifted to tech and biotech shares.
The S&P 500 dropped 23.68 points, or 1.3%, to 1865.09.
"It's been a long time since I've seen this kind of carnage," said Seth Setrakian, co-head of global equities at
proprietary trading firm First New York Securities.
Hedge funds and other short-term traders have piled into Internet and biotech stocks in search of big returns
in 2014, he said. In the first two months of 2014, the iShares Nasdaq Biotechnology ETF soaked up $526
million, according to ETF.com.
But over the past two weeks, $280 million was withdrawn as shares turned lower.
"A lot of people had been performance chasing in these momentum names," Mr. Setrakian said. "Now they
are panicking."
In March, a number of top hedge funds posted losses thanks to soured bets on these momentum stocks,
though the Nasdaq Biotech Index remains up 41% over the past year.
U.S. Treasury bond prices rallied broadly as the latest employment report curtailed concern that the Federal
Reserve may raise interest rates sooner than many expect. Analysts and fund managers said the data didn't
show clear evidence that the pace of the U.S. growth would accelerate after recent weakness.

--- Page 140 ---
Page 140 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The Fed has signaled it is keeping a close eye on the labor market as it weighs interest-rate policy and
determines the pace at which it is unwinding its stimulus program this year.
On Friday, the benchmark 10-year note's price rose 17/32, pushing its yield down to 2.726%. When bond
prices rise, their yields fall.
"The bond market tells you that the report does nothing to change the trajectory of the Fed's interest-rate
policy," said Jason Brady, head of fixed income at Thornburg Investment Management in Santa Fe, N.M.,
which oversees assets of $91 billion.
Stock selling Friday was concentrated in some of the biggest gainers over the past year. Facebook slumped
$2.74, or 4.6%, to $56.75, though the shares are up 110% from a year ago. Tesla Motors declined 13.18, or
5.8%, to 212.22 Friday, trimming its 12-month advance to 405%.
In the technology sector, "the momentum lower is increasing the amount of stock for sale," said J.T.
Cacciabaudo, head of equity trading at brokerage firm Sterne Agee & Leach. "This is real selling across the
board."
EMC and Cisco Systems, which have had less stellar returns the past year, declined 49 cents, or 1.8%, to
27.40 and 38 cents, or 1.6%, to 22.71, respectively.
In other sectors, the selling "still feels pretty orderly," said Bill Nichols, senior managing director in equities at
New York brokerage Cantor Fitzgerald LP. "I wouldn't say there's capitulation-type selling, but volume's
definitely bigger today."
Nine out of the S&P 500's 10 sectors fell on the day, six of them by at least 1%.
Amid recent weakness in these corners of the market, the health-care- and tech-heavy Nasdaq composite is
down 1.2% on the year. The S&P 500 and Dow average are up 0.9% and down 1%, respectively.
Gold futures rose 1.5% to settle at $1,303.20. Crude-oil futures added 0.8% to $101.14. The dollar declined
versus the euro but gained against the yen.
Among other stocks in the news, IMS Health Holdings rose in its trading debut, after the prescription-data
provider's initial public offering priced within its expected range. The offering raised $1.3 billion, marking
2014's second-biggest corporate debut so far. IMS Health shares advanced 3.00, or 15%, to 23.

Caught in the School Debate
Caught in the School Debate
By Mara Gay
1,042 words
5 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A17
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Karen McLean says she doesn't understand why the school where she sends her twin boys is struggling,
fighting for basic resources while classrooms just steps away gleam with fresh paint and are filled with new
computers and art supplies.
Shea Reeder says she put her son's name into a lottery three years ago because she wanted more for him.
Ms. McLean's 8-year-old sons, Micah and Shawkeem, attend P.S./M.S. 149, a district school on West 117th
Street in Harlem overseen by the New York City Department of Education. Ms. Reeder's 10-year-old son
George attends Harlem Central, a publicly funded charter run by the charter network Success Academy
Charter Schools.
The two Harlem schools lie at the heart of the battle over charter-school expansion in New York, and Ms.
Reeder and Ms. McLean said they are caught in the cross hairs.
In late February, Mayor Bill de Blasio revoked a plan that would have allowed Harlem Central and its nearly
200 students to co-locate in the 117th Street building, alongside both the district school and another Success
Academy charter already on site. But the freshly signed state-budget deal forces the city to accommodate
charter schools within public-school buildings, or provide part of the funding toward private space.
That deal, brokered by Gov. Andrew Cuomo and seen as a major victory for charter-school advocates, has
eased the way for schools like Harlem Central to expand -- and intensified tensions over space.
Both parents said they are fed up with a political battle they said has pitted schools and parents from the
same neighborhoods against each other but offered few answers about how their children will receive a
quality education.
"I understand them. We're the same," said Ms. McLean, 35, a Harlem-based freelance makeup artist. "Most
of us, on all sides, we barely have anything to begin with. We're fighting to have what we barely have."
"A lot of times, it is, 'Well, my children and your children live across the same block in the same projects,'" Ms.
Reeder said.
The battle is primarily over co-location, a practice that took off under former Mayor Michael Bloomberg in
which two or more schools share space in the same education department building.
P.S./M.S. 149, where Ms. McLean's sons attend third grade, already shares space with Success Academy
Harlem 1. Barbara Darrigo, the principal of P.S./M.S. 149, said she is proud of how the schools collaborate.
But a third of her students receive special-education services, and Ms. Darrigo said many require extra space
and therapies that can make co-location difficult.
Ms. Darrigo said her special-education students receive therapies in a "closet-like" room and that her school
has no space to hold music classes.
She said she constantly worries that the contrast between the schools will make her students feel less than
worthy.
"I'm in the red," Ms. Darrigo said. "I can never create a classroom that looks like theirs -- the new lighting, the
sleek furniture, the technology."
She added: "The children are very aware of what they do and do not have. A pen is like gold here."
The principal of Success Academy Harlem 1, Danique Day Loving, said the political battle has made
coexisting more difficult.

--- Page 142 ---
Page 142 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"Because of the charter-school fight -- and it feels like it is a war -- there's not always the best relationship,"
Ms. Loving said. "It doesn't have to be that way."
Because of the budget deal, George Reeder will be able to start sixth grade later this year with his
classmates at Harlem Central. But because the city still has substantial control over its public-school
buildings, where exactly the school will open its doors remains unclear.
For Ms. Reeder, keeping George at Harlem Central -- where he has developed a love for science-fiction
books and Greek mythology -- had become a primary focus.
"It's like a family," she said. "His school, his teachers here, they're invested in him."
For Ms. Reeder, who works as the assistant principal of a Bronx Success Academy charter school and once
taught at a district public school, neighborhood schools in the Bronx, where the family lives, aren't an option.
"I wanted more for him," she said.
Ms. McLean's two sons were born prematurely, and they have learning disabilities such as
attention-deficit-hyperactivity disorder that she said made it difficult to find the right school. At P.S./M.S. 149,
she said, the boys receive critical therapies.
And she said the school -- which received an "F" under the city's grading system in the 2011-12 school year --
is improving under Ms. Darrigo. Last year, it received a "B."
Ms. McLean said Shawkeem has taken an interest in math and Micah has become a voracious reader of
nonfiction.
She will be sticking with P.S./M.S. 149 -- for now, she said. But she does have a few charter-school
applications at home, she said: "I'm just curious."
Mr. de Blasio has said he isn't opposed to charter schools but wants to focus on changing the entire
public-school system. He has also said he failed to fully explain his decision on charter-school expansion and
vowed to help find a space for the Harlem Central students this year.
At Riverside Church last month, the mayor called the city to come together over the issue. "What can unify us
is that sense of urgency that we can't accept the status-quo," Mr. de Blasio said.
A spokesman for the mayor declined to comment for this article.
Pedro Noguera, an education professor at New York University who resigned in 2012 from the state board
that grants approval of charter schools, said the lack of constructive dialogue around charter schools in New
York is upsetting.
"There's a vilification that is unhealthy," he said.
He added: "When we're competing, we're not collaborating. That's what I find most disturbing. We're fighting
battles in court when we should be working together to figure out what works for our children."

WEEKEND INVESTOR --- Hulbert on Investing: Choosing Bonds Over Gold
WEEKEND INVESTOR --- Hulbert on Investing: Choosing Bonds Over Gold
By Mark Hulbert
914 words
5 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B7
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
It's a no-brainer, right? Owning bonds is bad when interest rates are on the rise.
Not so fast.
A review of past rising-rate periods shows that bond investments performed surprisingly well. So don't be too
eager to let the fear of higher rates lead you to sell your bonds and move into assets -- such as gold -- often
billed as providing protection during such times.
To be sure, predictions of higher interest rates have been common for quite some time, and so far increases
have been quite modest. But many investors continue to believe that average rates over the next five to 10
years will be much higher than they have been in recent years.
So just how poorly would bonds perform in a rising-rate environment? Consider the performance from 1966
through 1981 of a portfolio of intermediate-term U.S. government bonds -- those with five-year maturities.
That period, during which the five-year Treasury's yield tripled, is thought by many to be one of the worst in
U.S. history for bonds.
Yet, according to Ibbotson Associates data, such a portfolio produced a 5.8% annualized return in that period.
How? The answer lies in the increasing yield the portfolio earned as bonds matured and the proceeds were
reinvested at higher interest rates -- more than making up for the losses of the bonds themselves that those
higher rates caused.
This result isn't a fluke of the 1966-81 period, according to a study appearing in the January-February issue of
the Financial Analysts Journal. The authors -- Martin Leibowitz and Anthony Bova, managing director and
executive director at Morgan Stanley, respectively, and Stanley Kogelman, a principal at New York-based
investment-advisory firm Advanced Portfolio Management -- found that, as a general rule, the greater yield
earned during a rising-rate environment offsets the declining bond prices those rates cause.
Bond investors "need not be unduly worried about the impact of higher rates on their multiyear returns," they
concluded.
What about gold?
The World Gold Council argues that investors should consider the metal as a protection against rising rates.
The council, which represents the industry, said in a recent report that a stock-bond portfolio that also owns
gold delivers higher risk-adjusted performance than a portfolio that owns stocks and bonds alone.
Juan Carlos Artigas, the group's director of investment research, said in an interview that the conclusion is
highly dependent on assumptions about how each investment will behave in the future -- not only its
performance, but also on the extent to which it is correlated with other investments.
One of the group's most crucial assumptions, Mr. Artigas says, is that the outlook for bonds in coming years
is poor. In that case, he says, replacing five to 10 percentage points of the bond portion of your portfolio with
gold would increase returns without also increasing volatility.
Mr. Artigas says that his study didn't fully account for the rising yield of a bond portfolio that continuously rolls
over into new bonds, and that his "approach is perhaps not the most accurate."
A related finding of the Financial Analysts Journal study also is relevant when deciding whether to replace
any of your bond holdings with gold: Because of the offsetting effect of higher yields and lower bond prices,
bonds contribute "far less" to overall portfolio volatility than has heretofore been widely assumed.

--- Page 144 ---
Page 144 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
This is especially important, since gold is quite volatile -- and not just relative to bonds but also to stocks.
According to Claude Erb, a former fixed-income and commodities manager at mutual-fund firm TCW Group,
gold over the past four decades has been 29% more volatile than the S&P 500 and nearly three times as
volatile as intermediate-term U.S. Treasury bonds.
In an interview, Mr. Erb said that it would therefore be a bad idea to replace any of your bonds with gold.
If you believe interest rates will rise markedly in coming years, then you should focus on bonds with short
maturities, he says. That way, the offsetting effect of the higher yields will more quickly kick in than if you
owned bonds with longer-term maturities.
Two shorter-term investment-grade bond funds are recommended by at least three of the 42 investment
advisers monitored by the Hulbert Financial Digest who have beaten a broad stock-market index fund over
the past 15 years, a long enough period to largely eliminate the role played by luck. They are the Vanguard
Short-Term Investment-Grade Fund and the Vanguard Intermediate-Term Investment-Grade Fund. Each has
a 0.20% annual expense ratio, or $20 per $10,000 invested.
The first owns bonds with an average maturity of about three years and sports a 1.5% yield, while the
average maturity of the second is about six years and yields 2.7%.
For those of you who nevertheless want to own gold in your portfolio, the recommendation of the few
top-performing advisers who have a gold allocation is to invest in bullion rather than in the stocks of
gold-mining companies. Their most-recommended investment vehicle is the SPDR Gold Trust, which
charges annual expenses of 0.40%.
Its sponsor? The World Gold Council.
---
Mark Hulbert is editor of the Hulbert Financial Digest, which is owned by MarketWatch/Dow Jones. Email:
mark.hulbert@ dowjones.com

Currency in Crisis: Bitcoin's Boosters Struggle To Shore Up Confidence
Currency in Crisis: Bitcoin's Boosters Struggle To Shore Up Confidence
By Robin Sidel, Michael J. Casey and Christopher M. Matthews
2,227 words
4 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Corrections & Amplifications
The name of Jennifer Shasky Calvery, the director of the Treasury Department's Financial Crimes
Enforcement Network, was incorrectly given as Jennifer Calvary Shatsky in a Page One article on Friday
about bitcoins.
(WSJ April 5, 2014)
(END)
When Patrick Murck walked into a small, drab conference room at the U.S. attorney's office in Manhattan last
month, the freewheeling world of the virtual currency bitcoin got uncomfortably real.
The 38-year-old Mr. Murck is general counsel of the Bitcoin Foundation, a trade group that promotes bitcoin.
So he wasn't surprised when the group got a subpoena from federal prosecutors who wanted to know all
about Mt. Gox, the trading exchange that collapsed after announcing it lost roughly $500 million of bitcoins,
mostly owed to customers.
But when he showed up, the room also was filled with representatives from the Federal Bureau of
Investigation, Internal Revenue Service and Treasury Department's Financial Crimes Enforcement Network.
Investigators peppered him with questions for two hours, according to people familiar with the meeting. The
startled Mr. Murck, flanked by his two lawyers, said he was just as baffled as anyone by the mystery of the
missing bitcoins, according to people familiar with the meeting.
Bitcoin boosters like Mr. Murck used to spend most of their time explaining, extolling and evangelizing about
the electronic-only currency, created on computers and traded between people who store their bitcoins in
digital wallets. Those efforts helped attract venture-capital investors, soothe regulators and lawmakers, and
ignite a rocket-ship liftoff in the value of bitcoins.
Now the virtual currency is reeling from a string of crises. Some of its staunchest defenders have been swept
into the investigation, and the job of promoting bitcoin has turned into a crisis-management slog.
Mt. Gox's demise in February deepened suspicions among law-enforcement officials and regulators that the
virtual currency is a haven for money launderers, tax cheats and high-tech pickpockets. The value of a bitcoin
has tumbled about 60% from its peak in December to $445.42 as of Thursday, according to a price index
created by CoinDesk, an online publication that tracks trading on digital-currency exchanges.
"This currency is custom-designed for fraud," says one person briefed on the investigation into Mt. Gox, once
the world's most popular bitcoin exchange. On Tuesday, a U.S. bankruptcy-court judge ordered Mt. Gox's top
executive, Mark Karpeles, to answer questions under oath from lawyers representing customers with frozen
bitcoin accounts.
"There was some weakness in the system, and the bitcoins have disappeared," Mr. Karpeles said in Tokyo
on Feb. 28. "I apologize for causing trouble."
There is no sign that prosecutors or investigators are targeting the Bitcoin Foundation, and Mr. Murck isn't a
subject of the continuing probes, according to people familiar with the matter. But the growing difficulty of
persuading doubters that bitcoin is legitimate is accelerating a push by some of the currency's supporters to
bring more supervision to a corner of the financial world that has reveled in its freedom.
Bitcoin can't "stay amateur. It has to be professionalized. It is stunning and aggravating how plentiful the bad
actors are," says Arjan Schutte, founder of Core Innovation Capital. The venture-capital firm, based in Los

--- Page 146 ---
Page 146 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Angeles, is a large investor in Ripple Labs Inc., a digital network that handles payments between traditional
and virtual currencies.
The Bitcoin Foundation is basically the official establishment of a loose collection of technology geeks,
businessmen, and big-government skeptics from around the world. The 18-month-old group has thousands of
members, plus roughly $3 million in dues and donations contributed in bitcoins.
Some of the group's ties are complicating the scramble to shore up confidence in the virtual currency. Mt.
Gox's founder and chief executive helped pay for the Bitcoin Foundation's launch and was a board member
until he quit five days before the Tokyo-based exchange filed for bankruptcy protection. Another board
member, Charles Shrem, was criminally charged in January with money laundering at his company, which
swapped dollars for bitcoins and collected a fee on the transactions.
Mr. Shrem pleaded not guilty and stepped down from the group's board. He couldn't be reached for comment.
Mr. Karpeles declined to comment.
Last fall, a bitcoin-mining subsidiary of CoinLab Inc., a startup company run by Bitcoin Foundation Chairman
Peter Vessenes, filed for bankruptcy. Mr. Vessenes couldn't be reached for comment.
In response, some people with ties to the trade group want to add more sophisticated board members,
including well-known venture capitalists who are backing bitcoin-related startups. The group plans to hold an
election later this month to fill the vacancies left by Messrs. Karpeles and Shrem. Meanwhile, Mr. Murck is
redoubling his outreach to regulators, central bankers and even law-enforcement officials.
Tiptoeing into the mainstream will further alienate bitcoin defenders from libertarian users of the virtual
currency who call the Bitcoin Foundation a mouthpiece for businesses trying to profit from bitcoin's growing
use.
Cody Wilson, a co-founder of Dark Wallet, a service designed to keep digital transactions invisible, says the
trade group's mission is antithetical to bitcoin's principles.
"People who a few years ago were pretty radical are now putting on a suit and tie and just saying: 'Even if
bitcoin can't be a thing that will change the world, I can make a lot of money,' " Mr. Wilson says.
Bitcoin was launched in 2009 and is widely considered to be at an early stage of development. About $6.75
billion worth of the digital currency is in circulation, according to a report issued Wednesday by Fitch Ratings.
In February, bitcoins were used in about $68 million of transactions a day on average, up more than tenfold
from a year earlier, Fitch analysts estimate. In comparison, eBay Inc.'s payment service PayPal had $492
million in average daily transaction volume in 2013, while Visa Inc. processed about $19 billion a day.
Merchants that take bitcoins as payment include dating website OkCupid, blogging site WordPress.com, the
Sacramento Kings basketball team and Clips Hair Studio in Little Rock, Ark.
Despite the recent slide in the price of a bitcoin, it still has more than tripled in the past year. The Wall Street
Journal Dollar Index rose 0.2% in the same period, and the Dow Jones Industrial Average climbed 14%.
Venture capitalists are flocking to bitcoin conferences, including one in San Francisco last week where Mr.
Murck was surrounded by suits and ties.
"It's the first time that I went to a bitcoin conference and felt underdressed," says Mr. Murck, who wore jeans
and a button-down shirt.
Bitcoin is neither legal tender nor endorsed by central banks, and the technological spine of the virtual
currency's infrastructure is maintained mostly by volunteers. There are no large, publicly traded bitcoin
companies, no bureaucracy, no headquarters and no code of conduct.
That puts Mr. Murck under pressure to offset bitcoin's bad headlines. "My job is to explain how this thing
works," he says.
Mr. Murck wanted to pursue a career in public-interest law but worked on technology cases at a law firm
instead to pay his law-school bills. In 2009, he moved to Seattle with his wife and took a job at a startup
company that was exploring opportunities in virtual currency.
He left a few months after his son was born in 2011, opening his own software-design development and
legal-services firm.
Mr. Murck was one of the Bitcoin Foundation's founding members and volunteered as general counsel. He
worked to unite the fledgling group's members on hot-button issues such as whether bitcoin businesses
should follow federal anti-money-laundering rules.

--- Page 147 ---
Page 147 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Last spring, he maneuvered his way onto a panel at a conference co-sponsored by the International Centre
for Missing and Exploited Children. The subject: how criminals allegedly use virtual currency for illegal
purposes.
He also was introduced to Jennifer Calvary Shatsky, director of the Treasury Department's Financial Crimes
Enforcement Network, which had just issued a set of guidelines for virtual currencies. Last August, Mr. Murck
became the Bitcoin Foundation's full-time, paid general counsel.
The trade group now has eight full-time employees and is registered as a nonprofit group. Its one-room office
in Seattle includes a file cabinet with a magnet that proclaims: "I believe in honest money. Gold, silver and
bitcoin."
Jesse Powell, an entrepreneur who runs a bitcoin exchange called Kraken, says Mr. Murck has been "really
helpful to a lot of people." Mr. Powell adds: "If you don't have someone talking to regulators, they will leave
bitcoin to their imagination and think that everyone using it is selling drugs and buying kiddie porn, and that's
not the case."
Bitcoin's big problems began when Mr. Shrem, 24, was arrested Jan. 26 at New York's John F. Kennedy
International Airport. He was charged with conspiring to help customers of online bazaar Silk Road
anonymously buy narcotics and forged passports. Before his arrest, the charismatic Bitcoin Foundation
co-founder was one of the most popular speakers on the bitcoin conference circuit.
"I didn't see that coming," says Mr. Murck, who said his mobile phone filled up with text messages and voice
mails about the arrest. The lawyer thought it might be a joke, so he called Mr. Shrem.
"Give me a call. What happened?" Mr. Murck recalls saying in a voice-mail message. Mr. Shrem was the
founder of payment processor BitInstant.
Jammed into a middle seat on a flight to Washington, the lawyer says he put on noise-canceling headphones
and read the U.S. government's 27-page complaint against Mr. Shrem "Serving on the Board of the
Foundation is a privilege," Mr. Murck wrote in a Jan. 28 note on the group's message board. "Obviously our
board members must be held to a higher standard."
Mt. Gox halted all customer withdrawals less than two weeks later, saying a bug in the underlying software
code of bitcoin had allowed hackers to alter a one-time code in transactions. The alteration meant bitcoin
recipients could falsely claim they hadn't been paid.
Two other exchanges hit by the same problem, Bitstamp Ltd. of Slovenia and BTC-e of Bulgaria, resumed
normal trading after working out a fix with software coders coordinated by the Bitcoin Foundation. Mt. Gox
never reopened and filed for bankruptcy. The missing bitcoins represent 7% of the world's supply. Mr.
Karpeles has since said Mt. Gox has 127,000 customers and found about 25% of the missing bitcoins.
As Mt. Gox neared collapse, other Bitcoin Foundation board members got fed up with Mr. Karpeles about the
lack of information he provided about the exchange's woes, people familiar with the matter say. He seldom
said much to other directors or participated in board meetings, withdrawing even more as the problems
deepened. Mr. Karpeles couldn't be reached.
Mr. Murck was supposed to attend a bitcoin conference in Barbados co-sponsored by the trade group, but
backed out when he got the Justice Department's subpoena, he says.
Federal prosecutors are trying to find the missing bitcoins. People familiar with the matter say subpoenas
sent to Mt. Gox demand customer-transaction logs and materials related to solicitation of investors.
The U.S. attorney's office in Manhattan also has a pile of bitcoins worth more than $100 million that were
forfeited to the government as part of a criminal case against Silk Road. Prosecutors shut down Silk Road
last year and charged its alleged founder, Ross Ulbricht, with several counts of conspiracy.
He has pleaded not guilty and denied being Silk Road's founder.
"We seized it and we hold it and we treat it like regular currency," said Richard Zabel, deputy U.S. attorney for
the Southern District of New York, in January at a bitcoin hearing by New York's Department of Financial
Services. Federal officials are allowed to sell the seized bitcoins but haven't yet.
Jeremy Allaire, a longtime technology entrepreneur who is setting up a bitcoin service for consumers,
compares the chaotic environment to the Internet's earliest days.
"The amateur-hour enthusiasts that started bitcoin companies in the first generation are being weeded out in
a totally appropriate fashion and . . . replaced by legitimate companies that are working with the financial
industry and government," he says. "There is still more shaking out that needs to happen."

--- Page 148 ---
Page 148 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Mr. Murck says the Bitcoin Foundation is encouraging rules that would help prevent "more bad outcomes." At
a February meeting of state banking regulators, he said the bitcoin industry "is being held to a higher
standard than other payment systems."
He added: "As far as I'm concerned, that's fine."
Mr. Murck has reasons to help investigators and root for a rebound in the value of bitcoins. He is paid in
bitcoins -- and was a Mt. Gox customer when the exchange collapsed. He won't say how many of his bitcoins
are missing. "It isn't an insignificant amount," he says with a sigh.

S&P Extends Climb Into Record Territory --- U.S. Stocks Rise as Reports on Jobs, Factory Orders
Stoke Optimism; Blue Chips Finish Just Short of Highest Close Ever
By Alexandra Scaggs

Stocks continued their march upward, with the S&P 500 notching another record close while the Dow
industrials finished just short of an all-time high.
The S&P 500 index added 5.38 points, or 0.3%, to 1890.90, marking its second consecutive all-time closing
high and its eighth so far this year.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average advanced 40.39 points, or 0.2%, to 16573.00, its highest close so far this
year. The average ended the session less than 0.1% shy of its Dec. 31 record of 16576.66.
The Nasdaq Composite Index edged up 8.42 points, or 0.2%, to 4276.46.
Stocks rose after a pair of economic reports fueled investors' optimism about U.S. growth.
Recent data have helped reinforce the view that a string of disappointing reports on the U.S. economy earlier
this year was the result of an unusually icy winter rather than underlying weakness.
"There has been this worry that [economic] numbers might disappoint," said Stephen Auth, chief investment
officer of equities for Federated Investors, which manages $376 billion. "The risk has been that if we didn't get
improvement, you'd see markets pull back. But generally, the tone of the numbers have been as expected, or
slightly better," he said.
Still, the S&P 500's latest advance into record territory has been a slow grind compared to last year's
blistering rally. The index is up 2.3% so far this year, a marked slowdown from last year, when the index
notched a 10% rise in the first quarter alone and tacked on an additional 20% over the rest of the year.
Trading volume Wednesday was average, traders said. Some said they were still keeping an eye on highly
valued technology and biotechnology stocks, which had suffered a sharp pullback in March before
rebounding earlier this week. The Nasdaq Biotechnology Index ended the day up 0.1%.
If such so-called momentum stocks "roll over and set new lows, that's bad for the market," said Dave Lutz,
head of exchange-traded-fund trading at Stifel Nicolaus & Co.
Private-sector employment rose by 191,000 jobs in March, according to data compiled by Automatic Data
Processing and Moody's Analytics. That increase was just shy of expectations for a 200,000 gain and
followed on the heels of an upwardly revised 178,000 rise in February.
Meanwhile, factory orders for February were stronger than expected, climbing 1.6% on the month, while an
increase of 1.2% was expected.
"The economy is getting ready to pick up," said David Donabedian, chief investment officer of Atlantic Trust,
which manages $24 billion.
Gold futures gained 0.9% to $1,290.50 a troy ounce, after falling for a fifth consecutive session Tuesday to
settle at a seven-week low. Crude-oil futures fell 0.1% to $99.62 a barrel Wednesday.
The dollar edged higher against the euro and the yen.
In Europe, the Stoxx Europe 600 tacked on 0.2%, after rising 3.7% in a six-session win streak through
Tuesday. Data showing producer prices for February fell on the month, versus expectations of an unchanged
reading, stoked some hopes for further stimulus when the European Central Bank meets on Thursday.

Markets Review & Outlook: First Quarter --- Commodities Report: Car Demand Driving Platinum Price
Markets Review & Outlook: First Quarter --- Commodities Report: Car Demand Driving Platinum Price
By Laura Clarke
433 words
1 April 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C9
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Commodity-fund managers are again coveting precious metals, and supply pressures and increasing
demand are fueling special interest in platinum and palladium.
Investors ditched gold in 2013, with prices falling 28% as U.S. economic-policy makers set a course that
seemed less likely to stoke inflation. The metal tends to rise in value when prices increase for goods more
broadly.
Platinum and palladium, precious metals bought mainly for industrial use, showed greater resilience amid a
tight supply-and-demand outlook, falling just 11% and rising 1.6%, respectively.
Platinum and palladium are used in catalytic converters that scrub fumes from car emissions, and fund
managers say they are a strong bet with supply-demand dynamics expected to tighten further.
A strike in South Africa has cost producers of platinum and palladium. The nation provides 80% of the world's
platinum and 37% of its palladium.
"That's the biggest strike that they've had since the mid-1980s, and there are no signs of any resolution,"
says Rick Eling, head of investment at the U.K. arm of South African company Sanlam.
Many investors favor palladium, where supply is facing even more pressure and where demand may be
stronger.
Alongside South African risks to the metal, Signia Wealth investment strategist Gautam Batra reckons
potential trade disruptions in Russia stemming from U.S. sanctions in reaction to the annexation of Crimea
could push palladium prices to $1,000 an ounce. Russia is the world's largest provider of the metal, producing
about 40% of the global supply.
Platinum is used largely in diesel vehicles, and palladium is more suited to cars that burn gasoline. Diesel
vehicles are more popular in Europe, where the car market continues to recover gradually. Gasoline vehicles,
on the other hand, are favored more in markets that are generally faring better, such as China, where vehicle
sales are strong, and the U.S., where auto demand is back at pre-financial-crisis levels.
HSBC predicts palladium supply will trail demand by 1.06 million ounces this year, double last year's shortfall.

As Broader Fears Fade, Gold Is Tarnished --- Investors Pile Into Riskier Plays as Ukraine Tensions
Diminish, Fed Signals Interest-Rate Increase; 'Why Invest in Gold?'
By Ira Iosebashvili
729 words
28 March 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Some investors are backing away from gold, as the prospect of higher U.S. interest rates and an easing of
tension in Ukraine sends them in search of riskier assets.
Gold for March delivery ended Thursday at $1,294.70 a troy ounce, down 6.1% since hitting a six-month high
on March 14. Prices have fallen on seven of the last nine trading days.
The pullback has cooled demand for the metal, which had been on a hot streak for much of 2014. Investors
were on the hunt for safe places to park their cash amid a selloff in emerging markets and weak U.S.
economic data. Gold, coming off its worst year since the early 1980s, looked cheap. But a spate of good
economic news has freshly undercut the rationale for owning gold, investors say.
The U.S. economic outlook has improved, and Federal Reserve Chairwoman Janet Yellen said last week that
the central bank was on track to raise rates next year.
Furthermore, fears over Russia's move to annex Crimea, another reason investors bought gold, have
diminished as the standoff hasn't escalated into a wider military conflict.
Gold and other precious metals tend to struggle during periods of high interest rates because they do not
earn interest or dividends, unlike bonds and stocks.
Gold's latest tumble underscores how difficult it could be for the metal to ascend to its former heights, absent
a major shock to global markets. Money managers tend to buy gold during times of economic and political
uncertainty as it is seen as holding its value as other markets fall, but they prefer stocks and higher-yielding
bonds in calmer times.
The S&P 500 is just 1.5% off its record high from March 7. Many investors who had bought gold to protect
against inflation left the market last year and are unlikely to return, analysts say.
"With everything that has come out in the last few weeks, it doesn't seem like gold is going to fare so well,"
said Dan Pavilonis, a senior commodities trader at RJO Futures. "People see the stock market near its highs
and they think, 'Why invest in gold?'"
Traders point to the Federal Reserve policy meeting that ended March 19 as a turning point for the gold
market. The Fed cut the pace of its monthly bond purchases and signaled it was on a path to raise rates.
Gold bulls had seized on weak data earlier this year, predicting the Fed would delay plans to tighten
monetary policy. Some investors see gold as a hedge against a weak dollar and high inflation, two
potential side effects of the Fed's stimulus.
Gold prices soared in the years after the financial crisis, when the Fed reduced interest rates and began
purchasing bonds. Much of those gains were given up last year after the Fed signaled it was looking to
reverse these policies.
As the Fed shrinks the amount of money it pumps into the economy every month, it makes the dollar more
attractive and reduces the allure of gold.
The latest Fed meeting confirmed that downward pressure on gold prices remains.
Gold's rally "was a nice hope, but it didn't really pan out," said Rob Haworth, senior investment strategist at
U.S. Bank Wealth Management, which has $115 billion under management.

--- Page 153 ---
Page 153 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
As gold has declined over the past year and a half, Mr. Haworth has been telling clients to own less gold
than the fund's internal benchmark recommends.
To be sure, Russia has amassed troops along Ukraine's eastern border, raising concerns that Moscow may
move to destabilize the country or even attempt to annex more territory. A sluggish U.S. recovery and slowing
growth in China could still send nervous investors back into the gold market. Even after two weeks of
declines, gold is still up 7.7% this year.
"The Russia and Ukraine situation is not completely out of the woods yet," said Jason Rotman, president of
Lido Isle Advisors., a commodities-investment firm in Newport Beach, Calif.
Mr. Rotman said he plans to buy gold if it hits the $1,280 level.

bond-buying program. The comments left investors scrambling to reposition themselves and fueled a debate
over whether Ms. Yellen, hosting her first policy-meeting news conference as Fed chairwoman, was signaling
a shift in the Fed's stance.
For the most part, investors don't expect a rate increase from the Fed until late 2015, but the timing of Ms.
Yellen's comments would suggest a tightening of monetary policy as early as April of next year. Fed officials
had emphasized following the latest policy-setting meeting Wednesday that interest rates would stay very
low well into the future, and the Fed denied after the conference that any policy shift took place.
By the end of the day, stocks had recovered some of their losses, but remained lower for the session. The

Korea's Kospi was down 0.9% and Australia's S&P/ASX 200 was down 0.8%.
In late-afternoon trading, the benchmark 10-year note was 25/32 lower in price, yielding 2.770%. The yield
was at 2.714% right before the Fed's statement.
In the stock market, traders emphasized that for the most part, volumes were relatively low given the scope of
the move. Most of the selling, they said, was by short-term players and focused in index-tracking investments,
rather than individual stocks.

reason why this one wouldn't be as well," said Doug Crofton, managing director in cash equity trading at Bank
of AmericaMerrill Lynch.

longer maturities, suffered the most in Wednesday's selloff.

one-day basis since 2011, while the five-year yield increased the most since July 2013. When bond prices
fall, their yields rise.

Latest Bitcoin Craze? Actual Bank Vaults
Latest Bitcoin Craze? Actual Bank Vaults
By Evelyn M. Rusli
837 words
14 March 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Bitcoin was supposed to be a frictionless digital currency that required no banks or intermediaries.
Yet a raft of bitcoin thefts at exchanges like Mt. Gox is raising the question of whether the sophisticated
currency still needs the same sort of physical security infrastructure -- impenetrable steel vaults, armed
security guards, and even paper ledgers -- as cash and gold.
A Silicon Valley startup called Xapo is among a handful of young companies trying to become the Fort Knox
of bitcoin, building secret bank vaults deep in the earth that would safely store millions of dollars worth of
bitcoin code on computer drives. And if modern bank robbers still manage to pry open the vault? Xapo
promises to fully insure all deposits.
On Wednesday, Xapo said it raised $20 million in funding led by venture-capital firm Benchmark, to support a
network of underground vaults that the company says are in mountainous regions on multiple continents.
To break in, would-be robbers would have to face armed security guards, biometric scanners and a 24/7
video surveillance system. The company says it currently stores bitcoins for roughly 2,000 clients -- many of
which are hedge funds, family offices and other financial institutions.
"Trust in the participants, trust in the technology, trust in the ecosystem as a whole -- this is a really important
block for the bitcoin ecosystem," said Matt Cohler , a partner at Benchmark, which invested in Xapo alongside
Fortress Investment Group and Ribbit Capital.
With Xapo, a customer uploads bitcoins to a unique address. The bitcoins' private keys, the secret data used
to send funds, are encrypted and stored on physical hard drives and on paper, which are then placed in
Xapo's vaults.
When a customer wants to transfer funds from the vault to their "wallet," an authorized Xapo employee must
first confirm the transaction by taking several steps to verify the identity of the user. Once a day, these
authorized employees descend into the vault to fetch the private keys to execute the transactions.
According to the company, the code is secured in such a way that it is "mathematically unfeasible" for a third
party to read it. Transactions are completed in 24 hours or less.
Unlike credit-card transactions, bitcoins aren't safeguarded by consumer protections, so if they are stolen, no
laws or intermediary firms can help recover the losses.
Xapo says its vaults are backed by an insurance company, to protect against physical theft or malfeasance
by Xapo's employees. It doesn't, however, protect against user recklessness, such as sharing their
passwords with others.
Xapo's Vault product charges a 0.12% annual fee for each deposit. Its Wallet product, which allows people to
send or accept bitcoins, doesn't charge any fees for transactions.
Other companies are trying to provide similar services. Coinbase, for example, one of the largest bitcoin
exchanges, stores as much as 97% of customer information offline, according to its website.
The San Francisco company also stores sensitive information on servers that are completely untethered to
the Internet. But Coinbase doesn't fully insure users against losses. Elliptic Enterprises Ltd., a British
company, says it provides a bitcoin vault that offers insurance for a cost.
Whether these depositories are completely impenetrable by hackers remains to be seen, but the companies
say they are a necessity after the shutdown of several bitcoin exchanges.

--- Page 165 ---
Page 165 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Last month, one of the largest, Mt. Gox in Tokyo, lost nearly half a billion dollars of bitcoins after a hacking
attack, fanning fears that other exchanges could be vulnerable to attack.
A few days later, Canadian exchange Flexcoin said it was shutting down after hackers stole hundreds of
thousands of dollars in bitcoins.
The attacks have rattled the nascent industry. After hitting a peak price of more than $1,100 in November, the
value of bitcoin is now hovering around $600.
"Bitcoin today is like the Internet before the browser -- it was very hard to use," said Wences Casares, the
40-year-old founder and chief executive of Xapo, which has been working on its vaults for two years. "The
first problem is ease of use -- it's not user friendly enough -- and second, is security-service providers have
lost bitcoin in one way or another."
For Mr. Casares, born to Patagonian sheep ranchers in Argentina, the campaign to secure bitcoin is as much
personal as it is professional.
His country has long been racked by dramatic fluctuations of its currency, the Argentine peso. "My family had
lost everything, several times, from inflation, to devaluation and confiscation," he said.
Mr. Casares has devoted most of his career to creating financial products.
He developed Patagon, one of Argentina's first online financial-services firms, which was acquired by Banco
Santander for $750 million.
He also founded Banco Lemon, a Brazilian bank for the underbanked, which was later sold to Banco de
Brasil, and more recently Lemon.com, an online wallet, that was acquired this past December.

Some Gold in a Portfolio Makes Sense
Letters to the Editor
Some Gold in a Portfolio Makes Sense
218 words
12 March 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
A18
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The criticisms leveled against gold are the very reasons why physical gold should be a part of every properly
diversified investment portfolio ("A Word of Warning on Gold: Investor Fear May Be Fading," Global Finance,
Feb. 24). I refer to physical gold rather than gold-backed financial instruments, which are exposed to the
same financial and counterparty risks as all financial assets.
Rather than panning gold for what it is not, investors would do well by focusing on what it is -- the only
tangible, nonfinancial asset that is always liquid and practical to own without involving the financial system.
When we pay for fire insurance, we don't bemoan losing insurance premiums on account of making it through
the year without a fire. Similarly, having value of a physical gold allocation decline in a year when systemic
fears subsided and mainstream assets put in a banner year isn't a reason to cancel insurance.
Simon A. Mikhailovich
New York
Mr. Mikhailovich is a managing director at Tocqueville Bullion Reserve LP.

Bull Market Five Years On: Still Celebrating --- To Continue Gains, Stocks Are Liable to Rely More on the Economy Than Fed Support and Need...
Bull Market Five Years On: Still Celebrating --- To Continue Gains, Stocks Are Liable to Rely More on
the Economy Than Fed Support and Need Higher Corporate Profits
By E.S. Browning
1,198 words
8 March 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
As the bull market turns five years old, stock investors have a lot to celebrate.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average has soared 151% since stocks bottomed March 9, 2009, following the
financial crisis. That ranks it fifth in gains among the 32 bull markets since 1900.
It also is tied for fifth-longest-running, in trading days, with the one that ended in 2007, according to Ned
Davis Research.
If the current stock advance lasts another 16 trading days, it will be the fourth-longest, surpassing the one
that ended in 1987.
The Nasdaq Composite Index is up 242%, and the Russell 2000 small-stock index is up 251% in that time. A
bull market is commonly defined as a gain of at least 20% from a market low.
But this bull market is showing its age. The Dow, which rose 30.83 points Friday to 16452.72 on
stronger-than-expected job-creation news, is down 0.7% this year. The S&P 500 is up just 1.6% this year,
although it closed Friday at a record 1878.04, up 1.01 points.
The question now is whether the still-soft economy can push stocks higher as the Federal Reserve slowly
ends the multitrillion-dollar stimulus program that has supported stocks for so long.
The widespread expectation is the bull market isn't over but will rely increasingly on improvements in the
economy rather than Fed support. Many money managers believe the character of the market is changing.
"The stock market doesn't look like it is going to get year after year of double-digit growth as it has in recent
years, but the bull market can continue as long as you get continued economic growth pushing stocks
higher," said Russ Koesterich , chief investment strategist at investment firm BlackRock Inc ., which oversees
$4.3 trillion.
But further gains are far from guaranteed, Mr. Koesterich added. Stocks no longer are cheap by historical
standards. For them to keep rising, U.S. corporations must push profits higher at a time when profit growth
has slowed. An economic glitch or a sudden upswing in interest rates could put an end to the stock gains.
The S&P 500 now trades at 16 times its component companies' earnings for the past year, according to
FactSet. That is double its level of five years ago and almost identical to the level at which stocks peaked and
began their decline in October 2007.
A broader measure of stock prices developed by Nobel-prize-winning Yale economist Robert Shiller
measures the S&P against a 10-year average of earnings. It puts the S&P at 25 times earnings, far above the
historical average of 16.5 but not quite back to the 27.5 hit in 2007.
These measures show that stocks are riskier than before but don't tell what stocks will do next. Stocks can
remain expensive for years. Mr. Shiller's measure, for example, was at its current level in 2003 and moved
higher and lower for four years before the bull market ended in 2007.
The problem is that the market faces three big hurdles: expensive stocks, a slowing pace of earnings gains
and profit margins so high they are having trouble rising, said Scott Clemons , chief investment strategist of
Brown Brothers Harriman Private Banking.
"That means there is no margin of safety in any of those things," said Mr. Clemons, whose firm oversees $28
billion. The volatility with which stocks began the year could continue. "I think we will have multiple 5% to 10%
corrections in the next 12 to 18 months," he said. He expects the S&P 500 to finish the year with a gain, but
only by 6% to 8%, including dividends. That compares with a 32% rise last year, including dividends.

--- Page 170 ---
Page 170 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
The stock market's biggest driver so far has been the Fed's determination to keep the economy out of a
depression and heal the wounded financial system. The central bank cut overnight interest rates almost to
zero in 2008. Then it pushed down longer-term market rates by buying bonds in the open market, spending
more than $3 trillion since 2008.
Now the Fed is slowly trimming those bond purchases and is on track to end them entirely in the autumn. Its
next step will be to gradually let short-term rates return to normal, ending years of exceptional intervention.
Because those who bet against the Fed have lost, few professionals now predict a bear market, meaning a
sustained decline of 20% or more. If the economy and financial markets falter, many think the Fed will
intervene again.
This optimism has boosted stock demand among former skeptics, both ordinary individuals and people at
institutions with money to invest.
Ed Green, co-chief investment officer at Foster Group in West Des Moines, Iowa, has a new not-for-profit
client whose money was in cash. Mr. Green began gradually moving half to stocks. Late last year, with stocks
up strongly, the client asked to move new money into stocks faster, he says.
Individual investors, once the stock market's backbone, also have begun returning.
Investors put more than $100 billion a year into U.S. stock funds for six consecutive years starting in 1995,
according to Lipper Inc . But after the 2008 crisis, they withdrew more than they invested in 2009, 2011 and
2012. Now, some are returning.
Last year, U.S. stock funds received $172 billion, the highest annual net inflow on record. Flows to U.S. stock
funds have continued this year, at a slower pace. Net flows to bond funds, widely perceived as safer but less
lucrative, surged after the financial crisis but now have declined in size.
"We are returning to a more normal market, with more normal volatility," says Mr. Koesterich of BlackRock.
Dow Advances Friday
U.S. stocks closed mostly higher, although off the day's best levels, after February data showed more jobs
were created than economists had expected.
The latest employment report supported investors' belief that recent disappointing economic figures were a
temporary stumble because of the cold weather, but they didn't give them enough of a reason to make further
big moves into stocks.
The Dow industrials gained 30.83 points, or 0.2%, Friday, although they were up 84 points earlier in the
session. For the week, the index added 131.01 points, or 0.8%. The S&P 500 rose 1% on the week. The
Nasdaq Composite Index fell 15.90 points, or 0.4%, to 4336.22 Friday, but it was up 0.7% for the week.
Traders attributed the midday swoon to investors cashing in some gains ahead of the weekend, given the
uncertainty surrounding Ukraine.
Gold futures dropped 1% Friday to $1,338.10 a troy ounce. Crude-oil futures rose 1% to $102.58 a barrel.
In Europe, the Stoxx Europe 600 declined 1.3% Friday, as Ukraine kept investors on edge.
-- Tomi Kilgore

Heavenly Forex Intervention Sought
Heavenly Forex Intervention Sought
By Drew Hinshaw
881 words
7 March 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
ACCRA, Ghana -- A rising U.S. dollar has stirred many pastors here into nightly prayers for their fallen
currency to rise again.
"I command the resurrection of the cedi! In the name of Jesus!" preached evangelist Nicholas
Duncan-Williams of a megachurch called Action Chapel, recently. Then he addressed Satan: "Take your
hands off the central bank!"
African countries are having a devil of a year, due in large part to the slump in their currencies. From Gambia
to Zambia, sharp drops against the U.S. dollar are making imported goods more expensive, forcing
shopkeepers to raise prices, slowing business at many of Africa's gleaming new shopping malls, car
dealerships and fast-food restaurants.
In Ghana, the cedi lost 11% against the dollar in the first two months of 2014. Some radio personalities
blamed the free fall on the devil, others on mythical dwarves who practice witchcraft in the forest.
"These dwarfs, the black magic is what has made the cedi lose value," Anita Desooso, a political organizer
for the ruling National Democratic Congress told private radio station Adom FM recently.
Economists blame the currency weakness on more worldly reasons, such as the U.S. Federal Reserve's
decision to trim its bond-buying program. That has helped drive up the dollar against currencies globally.
But compounding these pressures from afar is a problem of Africa's own making: big budget deficits.
Many African governments preside over a rising and demanding middle class and face evermore competitive
elections. Yet their revenue still mostly comes from exports of raw materials like gold, oil, platinum, iron or
copper. And global prices for those commodities are falling.
That means African revenue is falling. But government spending generally isn't.
Zambia increased civil-servant wages by 50% last year, while prices for its key export copper fell 7%. That
fueled a budget deficit that the International Monetary Fund thinks hit 8.5% of gross domestic product last
year, nearly double the government's 4.3% target. As the outlook has dimmed, investors have fled. Last
week, Zambia's kwacha fell to a record.
South Africa faces a similar crunch. Its rand lingers near five-year lows to the dollar, just as officials are
preparing a massive, import-heavy infrastructure overhaul.
For months, Nigeria's central-bank governor warned that his country also could find itself scrambling for
revenue: "Nigeria is more than anybody else in big trouble," said Lamido Sanusi, comparing Nigeria to peer
oil exporters. On top of Nigeria's weakening currency, he said nearly $11.5 billion in oil savings has vanished
in a fit of election-year spending.
President Goodluck Jonathan recently ousted Mr. Sanusi, removing a voice of dissent, and sending the naira
to a record low.
A decade of dazzling growth has swept through Africa's biggest cities, leaving about 1,000 KFCs by Yum
Brands Inc . and 380 megastores from Wal-Mart Stores Inc .'s South African unit, Massmart Holdings Ltd .,
according to the companies. Advertisements in Accra offer freshly tiled condos at a princely sum of $3,000 a
month.
Now, the question is whether those shops and restaurants can survive their customers' diminished buying
power.

--- Page 172 ---
Page 172 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Ghana was the second-fastest growing economy of 2011, and Africa's fastest, thanks largely to a bonanza of
retail expansion. That binge of activity left the capital bedecked with shopping malls, U.S. hotels like Holiday
Inn and Best Western International Inc . and with helipad-topped condominiums that overlook shacks.
The government spent heavily, too. It tripled civil-servant salaries, a politically popular raise based on the
hope that new oil and gold extraction would keep soaring.
Instead, the price of gold fell 28% last year, lifting Ghana's budget deficit to almost 11% of the value of the
entire economy, and plunging the cedi 24% against the dollar.
Landlords and shopkeepers compensate by pricing in dollars. Ordinary Ghanaians stash their savings in the
greenback.
Last month, Ghana's central bank published new foreign-exchange restrictions to make it harder to buy
dollars here. The rules block depositors from moving cedis into dollar-denominated bank accounts. Since
they began, the currency has fallen an additional 7%.
Last week, the International Monetary Fund said Ghana's economy would grow by 4.8% this year, down from
6.1% projected four months ago.
Freshly erected shopping malls are empty. New airlines fly half full. Landlords say rents for flashy
condominiums are backsliding. Utilities are rationing water and electricity.
Ghana President John Dramani Mahama sees this financial tumult as his chance to remake Ghana into a
bustling agricultural and manufacturing hub, not a procession of outlet malls. His government raised its
value-added tax to 17.5% from 12.5% and plans new import duties. He hopes those measures will get
shoppers buying from Ghana's fledgling farms and factories.
"We've become a country that even imports toothpicks," Mr. Mahama said. "The opportunity exists for Ghana
to make a game change."
Next door, Nigeria is taking similar steps.
But of the $19 billion in foreign direct investment Ghana has attracted in the past five years, very little has
gone into new manufacturing or agriculture. Most has gone to build up hotels, malls or car dealerships.

Reporter's Journal: Energy Chiefs Sound Alarm Over Costs
Reporter's Journal: Energy Chiefs Sound Alarm Over Costs
By Russell Gold
773 words
7 March 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
B1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
HOUSTON -- Underneath their swagger and bravado, global energy chiefs gathered here for their annual
U.S. conference expressed a palpable sense of dread over the soaring costs of their signature oil and gas
projects.
"All of us are facing new realities and pressures," John Watson , chairman and chief executive of Chevron
Corp ., told a hotel ballroom jammed with an international assortment of men in suits and the occasional
woman. "Labor and capital costs have doubled over the last decade."
To pay for the rising price of extracting fossil fuels, the industry needs triple-digit oil prices, Mr. Watson
warned.
"The $100 barrel is the new $20," he said -- a sobering statement since global oil prices haven't been in the
$20 range since 2002.
Where once concerns focused on the apparent shortage of new elephant-size oil and natural-gas fields, this
year's IHS CERAWeek produced a lot of anxiety over soaring costs.
Executives "are optimistic about the resource and worried about cost," said William Maloney , the executive
vice president in charge of North American production for Norwegian state-controlled oil producer Statoil ASA
.
The energy industry thinks big and boasts of taking on moonshot-style challenges. Drill for oil in waters two
miles deep while perched atop a giant platform the size of skyscraper? Done that. Create an industrial plant
to chill natural gas in a country without measurable infrastructure? Coming right up.
In speeches and conversations, executives routinely talk of projects estimated to cost $40 billion or more. In
comparison, $40 billion represents the total economic output of the nation of Kenya, and more than the
amount President Barack Obama has requested to run the Department of Homeland Security next year.
BP PLC 's Shah Deniz project hopes to tap a natural-gas field the size of Manhattan underneath the Caspian
Sea. After it drills down and draws out the gas molecules, the British company will ship them more than 2,000
miles across seven countries to Europe. It will cost an estimated $45 billion, said Al Cook , the vice president
in charge of the project.
These megaprojects are no longer the exclusive domain of firms such as BP and Exxon Mobil Corp . In 2000,
just seven companies tackled projects that cost $5 billion or more, according to Luc Messier , the head of
project development and procurement for ConocoPhillips . Now there are more than 35, including many by
state-owned oil companies flush with government cash.
The result: Companies are competing for the same service contractors, engineers and equipment, causing
lengthy delays and ballooning costs. Rob Franklin , Exxon Mobil 's president of gas and power marketing,
estimates the cost of big gas projects has quadrupled over the past few years.
At the conference, it isn't unusual to bump into the chief executive of a big gasoline refiner, or come across
the Nigerian oil minister. English is the lingua franca, but groups of men in dark blue business suits speak
Russian, Portuguese, Chinese and a dozen other languages
While the big projects provoke the most angst, there is widespread recognition that the small scale --
especially around the U.S. shale boom -- has had significant impact. The one place where cost inflation
wasn't at the forefront of discussions was shale energy development and how it has benefited the U.S. To drill
and hydraulically fracture a well in Pennsylvania or North Dakota costs $10 million, at most.

--- Page 174 ---
Page 174 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Tim Probert , an adviser to the chief executive of oil-field services provider Halliburton Co ., said the industry
has successfully driven down costs in the U.S. Now it needs to "make better wells" that produce more oil and
gas.
Everyone wants to "emulate America's shale success," said Sam Laidlaw , chief executive of Centrica PLC ,
a British energy company. Rising oil and gas production has boosted the economy and is luring
petrochemical companies to invest along the U.S. Gulf Coast where raw materials are plentiful and
inexpensive.
"The U.S. is once again the place to be," said Joe Kaeser , president and CEO of Siemens AG, a German
industrial conglomerate, who said cheap energy is leading the manufacturing renaissance.
For an industry still trying to figure out how to do its megaprojects without spending mega-billions at a time
when energy prices may have plateaued, the best of times may be the most expensive.
"The industry's increasing demand for goods and services around the world caused significant cost
escalation," Chevron 's Mr. Watson said. "So costs have caught up with energy prices."

Lynn Cook contributed to this article.

ETF Market: Traders Eye a Big Player in Latest ETF Moves
ETF Market: Traders Eye a Big Player in Latest ETF Moves
By Chris Dieterich and Carolyn Cui
675 words
6 March 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C4
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Large swings in U.S. government -bond prices are drawing renewed investor scrutiny of whether
exchange-traded funds, or ETFs, are boosting market volatility.
Some $6.2 billion poured out of bond ETFs Tuesday as investor concerns over Ukraine cooled, driving a
global stock-market rally and a selloff in haven bonds.
Traders said Tuesday's exodus from bond ETFs was tied to the monthly rebalance of investment firm Good
Harbor Financial, which appeared to be in the market selling bond ETFs and buying stock ETFs, as well as
questions about Russia's plans for its large U.S. bond stash amid pressure on its currency, the ruble.
Paul Ingersoll, Chicago-based Good Harbor's chief executive, didn't respond to requests for comment.
Tuesday's bond ETF withdrawal was equal to roughly 2.5% of the $240 billion bond ETF market, according to
research and analysis firm ETF.com. In contrast, some $1.9 billion flowed into U.S. stock funds Tuesday,
representing 0.2% of total assets.
The shift came as investors reacted to cooling tensions over the Ukraine crisis, reversing flight-to-safety
buying in investments such as U.S. Treasurys and gold. The broad redemptions, mostly from funds holding
U.S. government debt, exerted some selling pressure in the $11.8 trillion Treasury market in
Tuesday-afternoon trading, traders said.
Prices for benchmark 10-year Treasury notes tumbled, driving the yield up 0.084 percentage point -- the
biggest one-day yield increase in more than three months. The 10-year note finished the day at a yield of
2.691%.
Stanley Sun, a fixed-income analyst with Nomura Securities in New York, said he fielded several calls from
clients asking about Tuesday's trading activity in bond ETFs.
"Because it's small right now, people are not going crazy about it," Mr. Sun said. "But it's becoming a trend,
and definitely something to keep on the radar screen."
On Wednesday, the yield rose further, to 2.698%. Yields rise when prices fall.
Investors often watch short-term ETF money flows for signs of shifting trends, though some traders said
Tuesday's ETF moves may have been more of an example of how one particularly large player can dominate
the money trail.
Most of Tuesday's one-day bond ETF flows were concentrated in the iShares 1-3 Year Treasury Bond ETF
and the iShares 3-7 Year Treasury Bond ETF , which each had withdrawals of more than $3 billion.
Tuesday was the largest single-day outflow ever for BlackRock Inc .'s iShare's fixed-income ETFs, a
spokeswoman said.
Good Harbor has a history of trading both the iShares 1-3 Year Treasury Bond ETF and the iShares 3-7 Year
Treasury Bond ETF and confirmed last month that it had moved all of its roughly $11 billion under advisement
into bond ETFs.
On Tuesday, traders and market evidence suggest that Good Harbor was selling its bondholdings for shares
of stock ETFs tied to the S&P 500 and the Russell 2000 indexes.
As reported last month in The Wall Street Journal, Good Harbor's main U.S. strategy can shift from 100%
bonds into 100% stocks in a day. Good Harbor usually trades on the first trading day of the month, but in
March it appears to have been in the market on the month's second trading day.

--- Page 176 ---
Page 176 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
Tuesday's outflow from bond ETFs marked a quick reversal of the record inflows the sector had in February.
According to Credit Suisse , U.S. bond ETFs had a net inflow of nearly $17 billion last month, an all-time high.
Interest-rate watchers said the selling in the Treasury market was also due to investors' fears that Russia,
one of the biggest foreign holders of U.S. government debt, would accelerate the selling of its dollar assets to
offset the slide in the ruble as a result of the crisis in Crimea.

Dow Retreats as Focus Turns to Economy --- Disappointing Reports on Jobs and Service Sector Keep

By Alexandra Scaggs

C4
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
Blue-chip stocks edged back a day after rallying, as investors turned their focus to the U.S. economy amid
another batch of soft economic news.
Disappointing reports on private-sector job growth and service-sector activity weighed on stocks during
Wednesday's session. But losses were limited as many investors continued to attribute weak economic
readings to severe winter weather.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average fell 35.70 points, or 0.2%, to 16360.18. The S&P 500 slipped 0.10 point, to

Stocks have made sharp swings in recent weeks on news about emerging-market turmoil, tensions in
Ukraine and the U.S. economy. But while the S&P 500 has gained 7% in the past 30 days, it is up just 1.4%
for the year.
On Tuesday, the Dow and the S&P 500 posted their biggest one-day point and percentage gains since
December as tensions over Ukraine eased. And with geopolitical worries receding, attention turned back to
growth prospects in the U.S.
Wednesday's economic data came in "lower than expectations, but not so low that people are upset by it,"
said Chris Gaffney , senior market strategist with EverBank Wealth Management. "We've got that built-in
weather excuse."
Trading was muted, with 3.35 billion shares changing hands in New York Stock Exchange composite volume,
compared with average daily volume of 3.57 billion shares this year.
After last year's 30% rally in the S&P 500, many investors said it has become tougher to find cheaply priced
stocks. That has led to diminished expectations for stock returns.
"Prices are fair to slightly expensive now, and we haven't had any real volatility in two years," said Colleen
Supran, a principal at Bingham Osborn & Scarborough in San Francisco. "So for that reason alone, we would
expect stocks to not do as well this year."
In economic news, Automatic Data Processing and Moody's Analytics reported that 139,000 new jobs were
created in February, up from a revised January count of 127,000, but missing expectations for 160,000. The
January gain originally was reported as 175,000. The ADP report is seen as a preview of the government's
February employment report due Friday.
The Institute for Supply Management's nonmanufacturing purchasing managers index, a reading on
service-sector economic activity, fell more than expected in February.
Mr. Gaffney said many investors have cut expectations for global growth. "We were overenthusiastic about
the growth prospects going into this year, and at the same time Mother Nature took a bite out of some of the
data," Mr. Gaffney said. "People have reset those expectations, and maybe we're just a little easier to please
now."
The yield on the 10-year Treasury note edged up to 2.698% from 2.691% late Tuesday, as prices fell. Gold
gained 0.2%, to settle at $1,340.20 a troy ounce, while crude oil lost 1.8%, to settle at $101.45 a barrel. The
dollar gained some ground against the euro and yen.

The Stoxx Europe 600 slipped less than 0.1%, after surging 2.1% on Tuesday to post the biggest one-day
percentage gain since July. Investors are awaiting the European Central Bank 's policy meeting on
Thursday.
In corporate news, Exxon Mobil was the biggest decliner in the Dow, dropping $2.72, or 2.8%, to $93.80, after

Smith & Wesson Holding rallied 1.94, or 16%, to 13.74, after the gun maker reported late Tuesday

Korea's Kospi was flat.

Stocks Take Hit From Ukraine Unrest
Monday's Markets
Stocks Take Hit From Ukraine Unrest
By Dan Strumpf
925 words
4 March 2014
The Wall Street Journal
J
C1
English
Copyright 2014 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved.
The crisis in Ukraine rattled financial markets, triggering a global stock selloff and driving up prices for oil,
gold and wheat, as investors assessed the potential impact of a showdown between Russia and the West.
All three major U.S. stock indexes posted their biggest one-day declines in a month. The losses on Monday
followed on the heels of declines in overseas markets after Russia moved troops into Ukraine's Crimea
peninsula over the weekend, prompting U.S. and European allies to threaten to retaliate against Russia with
economic sanctions.
Assets traditionally seen as safe, including Treasurys and gold, rallied.
The brunt of market pain was felt by Russia. The country's Micex stock index tumbled 11%, while the Russian
ruble plummeted to a record low against the dollar and euro. The Bank of Russia tried to quell the flight early
Monday by raising interest rates but was only partly successful. Meanwhile, yields on Ukraine bonds jumped
and the currency fell.
Other emerging markets fell broadly, with the iShares MSCI Emerging Markets exchange-traded fund
shedding 1.8%.
The upheaval comes just weeks after investors had endured a bout of economic and political worries in
emerging markets such as China and Turkey. In late January, U.S. stocks dove on those jitters, but then
recovered just as swiftly in February.
Investors in the U.S. said they were taking a cautious approach to events in Ukraine. Many said they weren't
ready to make major changes to their portfolios just yet. Sentiment toward U.S. stocks remains generally
bullish, and, if anything, some investors said they would be inclined to buy as prices fell.
The Dow Jones Industrial Average shed 153.68 points, or 0.9%, to 16168.03, while the S&P 500 gave up
13.72 points, or 0.7%, to 1845.73. The Nasdaq Composite Index dropped 30.82 points, or 0.7%, to 4277.30.
Traders said volumes weren't unusually high given the size of the move.
"Most people are of the mind-set that we have to wait and see how this plays out over the next day or two
before doing anything with their portfolios," said Joseph Spinelli, head of cash equity trading for the Americas
at Deutsche Bank . "The belief that it won't escalate to full-on military action seems to be the prevailing
wisdom."
In Europe, the Stoxx Europe 600 fell 2.3%, the biggest one-day drop since Jan. 24. Germany's DAX index
shed 3.4%, France's CAC-40 slumped 2.7% and the U.K.'s FTSE 100 slid 1.5%.
Meanwhile, in Asia early Tuesday, many markets edged higher after falling Monday. Hong Kong's Hang Seng
Index was up 0.3%, Japan's Nikkei was up 0.6%, while Australia's S&P/ASX 200 was up 0.4%
Monday's losses follow a U.S. stock-market rally in February that took the S&P 500 to a record and back into
positive territory for the year.
The index now is down 0.1% for 2014. The Dow is down 2.5% on the year.
Bob Landry , a portfolio manager who helps manage $16.7 billion at USAA Investments, said he was keeping
an eye on the events in Ukraine but said it would take a deepening of the crisis, such as a military
confrontation, to make big changes to his stockholdings. He said he is sticking by his bullish position on
emerging markets.

--- Page 180 ---
Page 180 of 190 © 2025 Factiva, Inc. All rights reserved.
"These things flare up from time to time, these geopolitical crises," he said. "Sometimes you can do real
damage to a portfolio if you're too quick to make changes."
Gold climbed 2.2%, to settle at $1,350.10 a troy ounce. Bond prices rose, with the yield on the 10-year
Treasury note falling to 2.607% from 2.660% late Friday. Bond prices and yields move inversely.
Meanwhile, some investors voiced worries about a jump in the prices of commodities tied to Ukraine. Ukraine
is a major grain supplier, and U.S. wheat prices rose 6.8%, the biggest gain in more than 20 months, on

amid worries over supply disruptions. Russia is among the world's largest oil suppliers.
Susan Bao, co-manager of J.P. Morgan 's U.S. Equity Fund, which oversees about $10 billion, said the crisis
could have an impact on the U.S. recovery if it causes oil prices to remain elevated. "When oil prices spike
up, when gasoline prices move higher, it's a tax for consumers," she said.
For now, she continues to hold a bullish view on U.S. stocks and is keeping an eye on possible declines for
opportunities to add to her portfolio. "If the U.S. economy continues its self-sustaining recovery, we can
handle. . .the small-scale global geopolitical shocks," she said.
Airline stocks, which are sensitive to higher fuel prices, retreated. Delta Air Lines fell 62 cents, or 1.9%, to
$32.59, while Southwest Airlines dropped 14 cents, or 0.6%, to 22.30.

crisis leads to economic sanctions against Russia, a major exporter of the fertilizer ingredient. Potash Corp.
of Saskatchewan gained 53 cents, or 1.5%, to 37.13.
